### [1] [Decoding EEG Signals to Explore Next-Word Predictability in the Human Brain](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18321)
*Boi Mai Quach,Binh T. Nguyen,Cathal Gurrin,Graham Healy*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates how word predictability and lexical/grammatical category jointly shape N400 brain responses during reading, showing larger predictability effects for content words (especially verbs) and highlighting that decoding analyses reveal richer temporal information than traditional ERP methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reading relies on interacting bottom-up linguistic structure and top-down prediction processes. Prior work typically isolated either predictability (N400 cloze effects) or lexical categories, leaving unclear how predictability modulates N400 responses across different word classes due to limited datasets. The study aims to fill this gap and to test whether more advanced decoding methods can uncover finer-grained neural representations than conventional ERP averaging.

Method: Participants read words while EEG was recorded at millisecond resolution. The analysis focused on the N400 time window (300–500 ms after word onset). Words were grouped by predictability (high vs low cloze probability) and by lexical/grammatical category (content vs function words; nouns vs verbs). The authors compared N400 amplitudes across these conditions and applied neural decoding techniques to classify predictability and word category from EEG signals, contrasting decoding performance with traditional ERP analyses.

Result: High vs low cloze probability elicited significantly different N400 responses, with effects being stronger for content than function words. Within content words, verbs showed larger N400 differences between predictability levels than nouns. However, nouns yielded more discriminable neural patterns regarding predictability than verbs in decoding analyses. Decoding methods captured more detailed and temporally specific distinctions in brain activity than standard ERP averaging.

Conclusion: Predictability and lexical category jointly shape N400 dynamics during reading. Top-down predictability effects are stronger for content words, particularly verbs, whereas nouns provide more distinct neural signatures of predictability. Decoding approaches surpass traditional ERP analyses in revealing the temporal richness and specificity of these cognitive processes, suggesting that combining prediction-based and category-based perspectives, along with advanced analysis methods, offers a more comprehensive account of the neural basis of reading comprehension.

Abstract: Humans invented reading and have passed down this complex skill across generations through language. This study provides empirical evidence of the neural mechanisms underlying bottom-up (related to high-order linguistic structure) and top-down (related to next-word predictability) processes, which interact to guide comprehension during reading. While previous studies have focused on either the N400 effects of predictability or lexical categories, research on how predictability influences N400 responses across different lexical categories is limited, mainly due to constraints in publicly available datasets. Here, we examine how predictability influences brain responses, recorded at millisecond resolution using electroencephalography (EEG), with a focus on the N400 time window (300-500 ms post-stimulus) across different lexical and grammatical categories. Our results indicate that significant differences in N400 responses between high and low cloze probability levels were more pronounced for content words than function words. Among the two primary content categories, verbs exhibited greater N400 differences than nouns, while nouns carried more distinct information about their predictability than verbs. Moreover, we demonstrate that the decoding technique is more effective than the event-related potential (ERP) traditional analysis in capturing more detailed and distinct representations of cognitive processes over time.

</details>


### [2] [A Classifier That Teaches Itself: Self-Improving, Frozen-gate Training (SIFT) for Dynamic Document Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18358)
*Bogdan Raduta,Horia Velicu,Alexandru Preda,Serban Chiricescu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: SIFT is a self-improving, production-oriented document classifier that uses a cheap sparse encoder + LightGBM model for most traffic, escalates low-confidence cases to an LLM judge for labeling, and safely retrains itself via a frozen-gate promotion mechanism to reduce labeling costs toward zero.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In real enterprises, document classification is not truly solved because the main bottlenecks are the cost and friction of building/maintaining labeled datasets and the operational risk of letting models retrain autonomously. Organizations fear regressions and lack continuous, low-cost labeling from real production data, so they either avoid retraining or rely on large up-front annotation efforts that don’t adapt well over time.

Method: The authors propose SIFT (Self-Improving, Frozen-gate Training), a dynamic classifier service. It uses a CPU-efficient pipeline: a SPLADE sparse encoder plus a LightGBM classifier as the primary model. Only low-confidence predictions are escalated to a more expensive LLM judge that provides labels. These labels are written back into a growing corpus built from production traffic. SIFT periodically retrains the cheap model on this self-feeding corpus. To avoid unsafe regressions, it uses a two-part "frozen-gate" promotion mechanism: (1) a regression check on F1 for critical labels and (2) a golden regression set that is never used for training. New models are only promoted if they pass both checks. Onboarding a new document family requires only configuration artifacts (label space, anchor phrases, judge glossary) instead of a full labeling project.

Result: The system achieves a virtuous feedback loop where the LLM judge trains the cheaper model, reducing the rate at which documents must be escalated to the expensive model over time. This leads to compounding accuracy improvements and a growing labeled corpus derived directly from real production traffic. The authors report an illustrative multi-domain deployment demonstrating that SIFT can operate safely across domains with autonomous, frequent retraining while keeping inference mostly on the low-cost pipeline.

Conclusion: SIFT operationalizes document classification in production by combining a sparse encoder + gradient-boosted tree model with LLM-based oversight and a robust, frozen-gate safety mechanism. It removes the need for large up-front labeling projects, allows routine autonomous retraining without unacceptable regression risk, and drives the marginal cost of new labels toward zero as the system runs. This approach reframes document classification from a static, one-off modeling exercise into a continuously improving, economical service.

Abstract: Document classification is a solved problem in the laboratory and an unsolved one in the enterprise. The blocker is rarely model architecture; it is the labeling project that must precede a model and the institutional fear of letting a model retrain itself once one exists. We present SIFT (Self-Improving, Frozen-gate Training), a dynamic classifier service, which attacks both. SIFT serves classification from a deliberately cheap, CPU-bound pipeline, a SPLADE sparse encoder feeding a LightGBM head, and escalates only the low-confidence minority of pages to an LLM judge. The judge's verdicts are written back into a labeled corpus, so the expensive model continuously teaches the cheap one: the escalation rate falls, the corpus grows from production traffic rather than from an up-front annotation effort, and accuracy compounds with use. Onboarding a new document family requires only a declarative bundle, label space, anchor phrases, and a judge glossary, not a labeling project. The harder problem is safety: an autonomously retraining classifier can silently regress. SIFT resolves this with a two-part promote gate, a critical-label F1 regression check plus a frozen golden regression set the model is never trained on, either of which vetoes promotion. This turns "retrain monthly without a human" from reckless into routine. We describe the architecture, the self-feeding corpus loop, the frozen-gate promotion mechanism, and an illustrative multi-domain deployment, and we discuss the economics of a classifier whose marginal labeling cost trends toward zero.

</details>


### [3] [Convolution for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18413)
*Yuchuan Tian,Yingte Shu,Wei He,Shuo Zhang,Tianchen Zhao,Chao Xu,Xinghao Chen,Yunhe Wang,Hanting Chen,Yu Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that adding tiny depthwise convolutions into Transformer blocks (on Q/K/V) gives useful local inductive bias and consistent accuracy gains for LLMs, with virtually no parameter overhead.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformers with self-attention capture global dependencies but do not explicitly encode local structure, which is important in natural language. Convolutions are good at locality but usually add many parameters or architectural complexity. The authors want to know if a very lightweight, depthwise convolution can be integrated into standard LLM blocks to provide local inductive bias without increasing model size or hurting performance, and to identify where and how to integrate it.

Method: They perform a macro-level ablation over 17 different insertion points for a depthwise convolution inside a Qwen3 Transformer block to find the best location, and then a micro-level ablation over convolutional design choices (residual vs non-residual, kernel sizes, normalizations, activations). The best-performing configuration is a residual depthwise convolution with kernel size 3 applied to the projected queries, keys, and values before the attention operation, with no extra normalization or activation. They evaluate this design on multiple Qwen3 model sizes and pre-training data budgets over seven downstream benchmarks, and also conduct a representation-level case study to understand how the convolutions change token representations, especially for repeated token IDs.

Result: The optimal configuration (residual depthwise conv, k=3, on Q/K/V pre-attention) consistently improves average accuracy across seven downstream benchmarks for various Qwen3 models and training data scales, while adding less than 0.01% additional parameters. Representation analysis shows that repeated token IDs become more sensitive to immediate context, indicating stronger modeling of short-range interactions.

Conclusion: Very small depthwise convolutions are an effective and nearly cost-free way to inject local inductive bias into Transformer-based LLMs. Applying a residual depthwise convolution with kernel size 3 to Q, K, and V before attention provides consistent performance gains and better short-range interaction modeling, suggesting that such convolutions are a practical complement to self-attention for improving locality modeling in large language models.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) largely rely on Transformers, where self-attention provides global token interaction but does not explicitly encode the locality of natural language. We study whether lightweight depthwise convolutions can supply this local inductive bias without materially increasing model size. Our macro-level ablation compares convolution at 17 locations in a Qwen3 Transformer block and finds the best results when convolution is applied to the projected queries, keys, and values before attention. A subsequent micro-level study favors a residual depthwise convolution with kernel size $k=3$, without additional normalization or activation. Across Qwen3 models and several pre-training data budgets, this design improves the average accuracy on seven downstream benchmarks while adding less than $0.01\%$ parameters. A representation-level case study further suggests that the convolution makes repeated token IDs more sensitive to their immediate context. These results support depthwise convolution as a lightweight complement to self-attention for modeling short-range token interactions.

</details>


### [4] [Building a European Multilingual Evaluation Dataset: The MMLU Localisation Project within the EMT Network](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18432)
*Pilar Sánchez-Gijón,Susana Valdez,Sofía Calvo Del Barrio,Florence Bellemont,Anna Kokkinidou,Mihai Cristian Brasoveanu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Collaboration localises the MMLU benchmark into 11 EU languages while doubling as authentic professional training for translation master’s students and revealing practical workflow challenges.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard LLM benchmarks like MMLU are largely English-centric, limiting fair evaluation across languages; there is also a need for realistic, project-based training environments for translation students.

Method: The DGT and EMT partner to localise the MMLU dataset into 11 European languages, involving master’s students in tasks such as translation, revision, project management, and multilingual coordination, and documenting methodological, administrative, and workflow issues encountered.

Result: A multilingual version of the MMLU benchmark is produced in 11 European languages and used as both an evaluation resource for LLMs and a training platform for students; the process surfaces concrete methodological and administrative challenges in large-scale localisation workflows.

Conclusion: Localising MMLU into multiple European languages both improves inclusivity of LLM evaluation and provides high‑value, authentic training for translation students, while also clarifying the practical challenges that institutions must handle when coordinating large multilingual projects.

Abstract: This paper reports on a collaboration between the Directorate-General for Translation (DGT) and the European Master's in Translation (EMT) to localise the MMLU dataset into 11 European languages. Beyond creating a more inclusive benchmark for LLM evaluation, the project offers master's students authentic, project-based professional training in translation, revision, project management, and multilingual coordination, while highlighting key methodological, administrative, and workflow challenges.

</details>


### [5] [Relay-Bench: Evaluating LLMs on Multi-Domain Reasoning Chains](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18438)
*Liam Swayne*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Relay-Bench is a holistic, text-only benchmark that evaluates LLMs on multi-domain, composite tasks within a single prompt, currently topped by GPT-5.5 (xHigh) at 43.3%.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM benchmarks often focus on single-domain, isolated tasks and may saturate quickly, failing to reflect real-world usage where models must juggle diverse tasks and tools in one interaction. The authors aim to create an unsaturated benchmark that better captures multi-step, cross-domain reasoning and tool use under realistic, complex prompting conditions.

Method: The authors design Relay-Bench as a collection of composite problems, each formed by chaining 2–13 single-domain subproblems into a single prompt. These composites demand reasoning across domains (e.g., visual reasoning, coding, math, information extraction with web-search focus, problem-solving, general knowledge, and data analysis). They further increase difficulty via prompt encoding and deliberate context bloat. The benchmark is text-only, but models are allowed and encouraged to use external tools such as code execution and web search, with no restrictions beyond the model harness.

Result: On this benchmark, the leading evaluated model, GPT-5.5 (xHigh), achieves 43.3% accuracy, indicating that even state-of-the-art systems perform far from perfectly and that the benchmark is not saturated. Performance numbers for other models are not detailed in the abstract but are implied to be lower.

Conclusion: Relay-Bench offers a challenging, unsaturated, and realistic evaluation of LLMs by testing their ability to solve complex, multi-domain, composite problems in a single prompt while using external tools. The relatively low top score suggests substantial headroom for improving cross-domain reasoning, robustness to prompt complexity, and effective tool integration.

Abstract: Introducing Relay-Bench, an unsaturated, holistic, text-only benchmark that measures LLMs' ability to complete an assortment of tasks from distinct domains in a single prompt. The leading model, GPT-5.5 (xHigh), scores 43.3%. The test set entirely consists of composite problems: groups of single-domain subproblems that are strung together into challenges that require reasoning across multiple domains in combination. Many of these problems then have layers of complexity added through prompt encoding and deliberate context bloat. Domains tested include visual reasoning, coding, math, information extraction (with a focus on web search), problem-solving, general knowledge, and data analysis. No restrictions are imposed outside of the model harness, and models are explicitly encouraged to leverage code-execution, web searches, and all available tools. All problems are composed of two to thirteen subproblems and do not require multi-modal input or output.

</details>


### [6] [Computational models of pragmatic reasoning with flexible generation of meaning and expression alternatives](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18443)
*Polina Tsvilodub,Fausto Carcassi,Michael Franke*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SAGE, a neuro-symbolic framework that uses language models plus rule-based components to model pragmatic language through generated alternatives, evaluation, and selection.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pragmatic reasoning crucially depends on sets of alternative utterances and interpretations, but current formal/computational models usually require these sets to be hand-specified, limiting scalability and flexibility. With powerful LMs now available, there is an opportunity to automatically generate and assess such alternatives while still retaining the interpretability and theoretical grounding of cognitive models of pragmatics.

Method: The authors define SAGE (ScAffolded Generative models for Explanation), which decomposes pragmatic language use into three module types: (1) proposers, LM-based generators that produce a wide space of alternative expressions or interpretations; (2) evaluators, modules (often LM-based) that score these alternatives on dimensions such as semantics, complexity, or typicality; and (3) selectors, rule-based components that implement a cognitively motivated task analysis of pragmatic reasoning. They instantiate this framework in three case studies—referential expression generation, M-implicatures, and Gricean implicatures—and evaluate via ablations, baselines, and quantitative fit to human behavioral data.

Result: Across the three pragmatic tasks, SAGE models show high accuracy and frequently outperform baseline models. Analysis of the internal components reveals that LM-based proposer modules robustly produce alternative utterances and interpretations well-aligned with pragmatic modeling needs. However, LM-based evaluators perform less well on theoretically precise or formally defined measures; they are better at producing intuitive, human-like judgments than at mirroring exact formal semantic or complexity metrics.

Conclusion: SAGE demonstrates that combining the generative capacity of LMs with explicit, rule-based cognitive modeling can yield powerful and relatively interpretable models of pragmatic language use. The success of LM-based proposers highlights the value of data-driven alternative generation, but limitations of LM-based evaluators underscore that LMs are not yet ideal stand-ins for formal measures in cognitive theories. Overall, the work positions neuro-symbolic, scaffolded LM architectures as promising but still imperfect explanatory models of human pragmatic reasoning, motivating further refinement of evaluation modules and integration with formal theory.

Abstract: Pragmatic language use requires reasoning about alternatives: the alternative expressions a speaker might have chosen, or the alternative interpretations a listener might entertain. Formal and computational models of pragmatics must therefore specify the sets of alternatives that interlocutors reason over, which is often done through manual specification. Here we propose a framework, ScAffolded Generative models for Explanation (SAGE), that combines the explanatory transparency of cognitive models with the generative flexibility of language models (LMs). SAGE decomposes a pragmatic process into three kinds of modules: proposers, which use LMs to generate an open-ended space of candidate alternatives; evaluators, which assess those alternatives (e.g., their semantics, complexity, or typicality); and selectors, which implement the rule-based computational steps of a cognitively motivated task analysis. We assess SAGE in three case studies spanning pragmatic generation and interpretation-referential expression generation, manner (M-)implicatures, and Gricean conversational implicatures. SAGE models are evaluated critically using established methods from computational cognitive modeling, including ablations, baseline comparisons, and quantitative fit to human data. Across studies, SAGE models achieved high accuracy and often outperformed baselines, but component-level analyses reveal an asymmetry: LM proposers reliably generated alternatives well-suited to pragmatic modeling, whereas LM evaluators are better at providing intuitive judgements rather than judgements of theoretical or formal measures. We discuss the promise and the limitations of neuro-symbolic models as candidate explanatory accounts of human pragmatic language use.

</details>


### [7] [Using Fine-Tuned LLMs to Identify Indicators of Vulnerability in UK Police Incident Logs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18446)
*Sam Relins,Daniel Birks*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether a locally hosted open-weight LLM can be used, with extra methodological steps, to estimate how often indicators of vulnerability (mental ill health, substance misuse, alcohol dependence, homelessness) appear in UK police incident narratives.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Routine police work involves many vulnerable people, but existing administrative fields capture this poorly. Better measurement could guide training, resource allocation, and multi-agency support. Since incident records are largely unstructured text, the authors investigate whether an LLM-based classifier—initially built for US data—can be adapted for UK police narrative data and under what conditions its outputs can be trusted as measurement rather than rough prediction.

Method: They analysed ~3,000 de-identified incident logs from a UK police force with a multi-stage pipeline: repeated LLM inferences for each incident, aggregation of those outputs into provisional labels, structured human review of a subset, and statistical bias correction to adjust LLM-derived prevalence estimates. The system uses a locally hosted open-weight LLM to meet security constraints. They then compare LLM-derived prevalence and label stability against human judgements, quantifying over-assignment and uncertainty.

Result: The LLM can generate large-scale, approximate prevalence estimates of vulnerability indicators, suggesting mental ill health indicators in around 20% of incidents and lower rates for substance misuse, alcohol dependence, and homelessness. However, single-run classifications are unstable, and even aggregated predictions show systematic over-labelling compared with human reviewers. Achieving more defensible prevalence estimates required substantial human involvement and post-hoc statistical adjustment, and even then, estimates retained notable uncertainty.

Conclusion: LLMs are useful for extracting signals from unstructured police narratives, but their raw outputs are not valid measurements. With careful pipeline design, human oversight, and bias correction, one can obtain somewhat defensible population-level prevalence estimates, though at significant resource cost. For individual-level or operational decision-making, the error rate and unpredictability of misclassifications make current LLM-based approaches unsuitable. The work underscores both the promise and the methodological limitations of using LLMs as measurement tools in sensitive, real-world domains.

Abstract: Purpose: Understanding how much of routine policing involves vulnerable people could inform resourcing, training, and multi-agency response, yet administrative data provide limited insight. We explore whether an LLM-based classification pipeline, developed on open-source US police data, can be adapted to estimate the prevalence of four vulnerability indicators - mental ill health, substance misuse, alcohol dependence, and homelessness - in UK police incident narratives, and when outputs can be treated as defensible measurements.
  Methods: We analyse nearly 3,000 de-identified incident logs from a UK police force, using a multi-stage pipeline combining repeated model inference, label aggregation, structured human review, and statistical correction. The pipeline runs on a locally hosted open-weight LLM, reflecting the secure environments police must work in.
  Results: LLMs can produce meaningful, if imperfect, prevalence estimates at scale. Mental ill health indicators are present in approximately one in five incidents, with lower prevalence for other indicators. However, naive LLM deployment is unreliable: single-pass classifications are unstable, and aggregated outputs systematically over-assign indicators relative to human judgement. Correcting these biases required substantial human input and statistical adjustment, leaving considerable uncertainty.
  Conclusions: While LLMs can extract information from unstructured police data, their outputs cannot be treated as valid measurements without careful methodological support. At the population level, defensible estimates are achievable but resource-intensive; at the individual level, errors remain frequent and unpredictable, limiting suitability for operational decisions. This study highlights both the potential and the constraints of LLM-based measurement in applied settings.

</details>


### [8] [PathReportEval: A Systematic Benchmark for Pathology Report Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18448)
*Suryakant Singh,Sejuti Majumder,Beatrice Knudsen,Joel Saltz,Prateek Prasanna*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a standardized benchmark and a clinically grounded evaluation metric (CRQS) for assessing pathology report generation from whole-slide images, showing that traditional text-similarity metrics poorly reflect clinical correctness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work on pathology report generation uses inconsistent datasets, models, encoders, and evaluation protocols, making fair comparison difficult. Moreover, standard natural language generation metrics like BLEU, ROUGE, and METEOR focus on surface lexical overlap and fail to capture clinically critical issues such as missing diagnoses, hallucinations, or incorrect tumor attributes. There is a need for a reproducible benchmark and a clinically meaningful metric to evaluate factual and clinical correctness of generated pathology reports.

Method: The authors build a standardized benchmark and plug-and-play evaluation framework that unifies preprocessing, feature extraction, training, decoding, and evaluation for pathology report generation from whole-slide images. They evaluate four representative generation methods across three datasets (TCGA, HistAI, REG 2025) and three pathology-focused visual foundation encoders (CONCHv1.5, UNI2-h, H-Optimus-1). The key methodological innovation is the Clinical Report Quality Score (CRQS), which converts both reference and generated reports into structured clinical attributes and computes four dimensions: clinical fact coverage, key information recall, hallucination rate, and clinical discordance. CRQS outputs an overall score plus interpretable sub-scores.

Result: Using the benchmark, the authors show that conventional NLG metrics (BLEU, ROUGE, METEOR) correlate weakly with clinical correctness and tend to overestimate the quality of generated pathology reports. In contrast, CRQS is sensitive to clinically important differences between models and encoders, revealing variations in factual correctness, omissions, hallucinations, and discordances that lexical metrics miss. The experiments validate that the choice of encoder and generation method leads to measurable differences under CRQS even when lexical metrics suggest similar performance.

Conclusion: The work provides a reproducible, standardized foundation for evaluating pathology report generation, consisting of a benchmark, a public modular framework, and the CRQS metric. These tools enable fair comparison across models and encoders and, crucially, assess clinical factual correctness rather than mere lexical overlap. The authors conclude that future research in pathology report generation should adopt clinically grounded metrics like CRQS, as standard text similarity metrics are insufficient for ensuring safe and accurate clinical reporting.

Abstract: Pathology report generation from whole-slide images (WSIs) is a rapidly growing multimodal learning problem, yet progress is difficult to measure because existing studies use heterogeneous datasets, model settings, visual encoders, and evaluation protocols. Moreover, commonly used natural language generation metrics, including BLEU, ROUGE, and METEOR, primarily reward lexical similarity and often fail to detect clinically consequential errors such as omitted diagnoses, hallucinated findings, or discordant tumor attributes.
  We present a standardized benchmark and evaluation framework for pathology report generation. The benchmark evaluates four representative methods across three datasets (TCGA, HistAI, and REG 2025) using three pathology foundation encoders (CONCHv1.5, UNI2-h, and H-Optimus-1). Our framework standardizes preprocessing, feature extraction, training, decoding, and evaluation, enabling fair comparison across models while providing a modular platform for integrating new methods, datasets, and encoders.
  A central contribution is the Clinical Report Quality Score (CRQS), a clinically grounded metric for evaluating factual correctness. CRQS maps reference and generated reports into structured clinical attributes and measures four complementary dimensions: clinical fact coverage, key information recall, hallucination rate, and clinical discordance, producing both an overall score and interpretable sub-scores.
  Experiments demonstrate that conventional language-generation metrics are weakly aligned with clinical correctness and frequently overestimate report quality. In contrast, CRQS reveals clinically meaningful differences between models and encoders that lexical metrics fail to capture. Together, the benchmark, public plug-and-play framework, and CRQS establish a reproducible foundation for rigorous evaluation of pathology report generation.

</details>


### [9] [Structured Output Collapses Answer Diversity Across 44 Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18476)
*Tapan Parikh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that simply asking language models to respond in JSON format, without enforcing any schema, makes their answers significantly more homogeneous and closer to the global majority choice across models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models are increasingly used through structured-output interfaces (like JSON) in tools and software, but most evaluation focuses on free-form chat. The authors want to know whether the output format request itself systematically changes what answer a model gives, especially in tasks where many answers are equally valid.

Method: They replicate the One-Word Census experiment with 31 broad, open-ended one-word category prompts across 44 language models, but now add a natural-language instruction to answer in JSON (and other formats). They compare distributions of answers between plain chat and JSON (and XML, YAML, CSV, arbitrary brackets), compute convergence metrics such as modal-answer frequency, number of distinct answers, and surprisal, and run statistical tests (e.g., BH-FDR and p-values) to see which models and formats significantly shift. They also test within-run resampling to track model-specific default answers and examine the effect of enforcing schemas via decoder-level constraints (response_format) vs. just requesting a format in the prompt.

Result: Requesting JSON induces markedly stronger convergence toward a few common answers: for the unconstrained “Pick a word” prompt, the most common answer’s share increases from 41% to 64%, distinct answers drop from 52 to 36, and mean surprisal falls from 1.80 to 1.58 bits. Six of 44 models show individually significant shifts and all move toward the global mode, with stronger effects on previously distinctive models. The pre-existing chat modal answer usually remains the mode (28/31 categories), but JSON sharpens its dominance. Within-model resampling shows JSON changes 53% of a model’s stable chat defaults, typically moving them toward the crowd and sometimes creating new JSON-only defaults (e.g., Claude Fable 5 always answers “cerulean” for colour in JSON but never in chat). Across output formats, compression is strongest and statistically significant for JSON and XML, absent for YAML and CSV, and reversed (more diversity) for an arbitrary bracket wrapper. Enforcing structured-output schemas at the decoder adds almost no extra compression beyond the JSON request itself.

Conclusion: The way we ask models to format their answers is not neutral: requesting structured output, especially in formats like JSON and XML that models are heavily trained to use with tools, makes their behavior more homogeneous and shifts their internal defaults toward widely shared patterns. This effect arises from the model’s learned association between certain “registers” (tool-oriented formats) and particular default responses, not from decoding constraints. Because most real-world applications consume models through structured formats, but evaluations are performed in plain chat, current benchmarks underestimate how convergent and less diverse models may be in actual tool-use settings.

Abstract: When a language model must choose one answer from a large space of equally valid options, a format clause -- "Reply with JSON only" -- changes which answer it chooses. We re-run the One-Word Census (arXiv:2607.12796): 31 wide-answer-space category prompts asked of 44 models, now with the reply requested in JSON -- no schema enforcement, no constrained decoding, only the request. Convergence deepens sharply: on the unconstrained "Pick a word" prompt the modal answer rises from 41% to 64% of the pool and distinct answers fall from 52 to 36; mean answer-choice surprisal drops from 1.80 to 1.58 bits. The tax is progressive: six of 44 models move individually (BH-FDR q=.10), all toward the mode, led by the most distinctive models, while the conformist floor is immobile. It is a sharpener, not a re-indexer -- the plain-chat modal answer survives in 28 of 31 categories. Defaults are register-indexed: a within-run re-sample (n=20) finds JSON shifts 53% of a model's stable chat defaults, mostly back to the crowd, and installs defaults absent from chat (Claude Fable 5 answers "cerulean" for colour 0% of the time in chat, 100% in JSON). Full-battery controls reveal a register gradient: compression is significant and specific to the answer-delivery formats models are trained to speak (JSON -0.22 bits, p=.0002; XML -0.19, p=.002), absent for YAML and CSV, and reversed for an arbitrary bracket wrapper (+0.13, p=.009) -- weighing the mechanism toward tool-use post-training. Enforcing the schema at the decoder (response_format) compresses no further than the request (-0.03 bits): the collapse lives in the model's response to the register, not the decoder. Structured output is how software consumes language models, and that surface is served by a measurably more homogeneous model than the chat surface on which models are evaluated, compared, and chosen.

</details>


### [10] [Search-on-Graph-R1: Training Large Language Models to Search Knowledge Graphs with Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18481)
*Jia Ao Sun,Hao Yu,Fengran Mo,Zhan Su,Yuchen Hui,Bang Liu,Jian-Yun Nie*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Search-on-Graph-R1, an 8B-parameter model for knowledge graph question answering that learns to navigate knowledge graphs efficiently via supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning, outperforming larger frontier-LLM-based systems while being cheaper to deploy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing KGQA methods often rely on large frontier LLMs that use retrieval tools to explore knowledge graphs, which is computationally expensive and difficult to deploy at scale. There is a need for smaller models that can internalize the graph navigation process while remaining grounded to the knowledge graph and achieving or surpassing the performance of frontier LLM systems.

Method: The authors propose Search-on-Graph-R1, an 8B model trained in two stages: (1) supervised fine-tuning using trajectories generated by a frontier LLM teacher that is scaffolded with each question’s gold SPARQL query to traverse a known answer-bearing path using a live Search tool against Freebase; (2) reinforcement learning to further refine the model’s ability to navigate the graph, including optimizing for fewer Search calls. The trajectories are grounded because they are executed against a live Freebase server, and the model ultimately learns to perform KG navigation without auxiliary modules or LLM judges at inference or training time.

Result: On the WebQSP, CWQ, and GrailQA benchmarks, the 8B Search-on-Graph-R1 model surpasses all compared frozen frontier-LLM-based KGQA systems, achieving state-of-the-art results on CWQ among the systems considered. Ablation and stage-wise analysis show that both SFT and RL contribute complementary performance improvements, the method transfers across model families, and RL specifically helps the model reach answers with fewer Search calls than the SFT-only initialization.

Conclusion: Search-on-Graph-R1 demonstrates that an 8B model can internalize knowledge graph navigation behaviors previously delegated to large frontier LLMs, yielding better accuracy and efficiency in KGQA without relying on auxiliary inference-time modules or LLM judges. The combination of scaffolded teacher trajectories for SFT and subsequent RL not only improves performance but also makes graph exploration more efficient, suggesting a practical path toward deployable, compact KGQA systems.

Abstract: Knowledge graph question answering (KGQA) requires navigating from topic entities to an answer several relations away. Recent methods prompt a frontier LLM to explore the graph through a retrieval tool, but their reliance on frontier-scale inference makes them costly to deploy. We present Search-on-Graph-R1 (\sogrone{}), which internalizes this navigation into a compact 8B model through supervised fine-tuning (SFT) followed by reinforcement learning (RL). Our central idea is to scaffold a frontier teacher with each question's gold SPARQL query, so the teacher traverses a known answer-bearing path with a live \texttt{Search} tool rather than having to discover the path itself. Since every call executes against a live Freebase server, the resulting trajectories are grounded in the knowledge graph by construction. On WebQSP, CWQ, and GrailQA, \sogrone{} at 8B surpasses every frozen frontier-LLM system in our comparison and posts the strongest results on CWQ of any system we compare against. It does so using no auxiliary module at inference and no LLM judge during training. Isolating each training stage shows that SFT and RL contribute complementary gains, our approach transfers across model families, and RL learns to reach answers in fewer \texttt{Search} calls than its SFT initialization.

</details>


### [11] [Reasoning Fine-Tuning Induces Persistent Latent Policy States](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18532)
*Abir Harrasse,Michael Lan,Hunar Batra,Fateme Hashemi Chaleshtori,Chaithanya Bandi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how reasoning-focused fine-tuning changes the internal dynamics of LMs, modeling chain-of-thought as a switching dynamical system to uncover discrete latent reasoning states and their transitions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although reasoning-tuned models perform much better than base models on multi-step reasoning, it is unclear *how* their internal computation changes: is it just better local token prediction, or a global reorganization of inference over time? The authors want a mechanistic, temporally aware view of reasoning processes instead of only input-output evaluation.

Method: They treat chain-of-thought reasoning as a switching dynamical system where internal activations evolve under a small number of discrete latent 'policy states.' They combine (1) time-aware contrastive representation learning to get good trajectory embeddings, with (2) discrete regime discovery to infer latent states and their transition structure from activation trajectories. They apply this to multiple reasoning benchmarks and models from 1.5B–32B parameters, then validate with controls and causal interventions such as state-swap ablations and transplanting learned reasoning dynamics into base models.

Result: Reasoning-fine-tuned models show substantially richer and more structured latent policy organization than base models: clearer transition graphs, distinct patterns of state usage, persistence, and mixing. The inferred states align with different stages of reasoning rather than just correctness or representation artifacts. Causal experiments show these states are functional: swapping states harms predictive performance, while inserting reasoning dynamics into base models improves their performance. Using the discovered regimes to prune failure-prone reasoning prefixes yields better accuracy than self-consistency in most model-dataset combinations, with improvements up to 12.5 percentage points.

Conclusion: Reasoning fine-tuning does more than locally improve token predictions; it globally reorganizes the latent dynamics of how models reason over time. Modeling internal computation as a switching dynamical system provides a useful mechanistic lens and enables process-level control, such as improving or pruning reasoning trajectories based on inferred latent states.

Abstract: Reasoning-specialized language models show large performance gains over base models, yet the internal changes responsible for improved multi-step reasoning remain poorly understood. It is unclear whether reasoning fine-tuning improves local token-level competence or globally reorganizes how models structure inference over time. We address this question by modeling Chain-of-Thought reasoning as a switching dynamical system (SDS), in which internal representations evolve under discrete latent policy states. Our framework combines time-aware contrastive representation learning with discrete regime discovery to recover latent policies from activation trajectories. Across four benchmarks and model scales from 1.5B to 32B parameters, reasoning-fine-tuned models exhibit richer latent-policy organization than their base counterparts, characterized by more differentiated transition structure and model-dependent changes in state utilization, persistence, and mixing. The recovered regimes exhibit functional specialization aligned with distinct reasoning stages, and extensive controls confirm that their structure is not explained by correctness, representation learning, or modeling priors, but depends on the coherent temporal organization of reasoning trajectories. Causal interventions further show that the regimes are functionally meaningful: state-swap ablations reduce one-step predictive fit, while transplanting reasoning dynamics into base models improves performance on challenging reasoning problems. Finally, SDS-guided pruning of failure-prone reasoning prefixes outperforms self-consistency in 11 of 12 model-dataset settings, with gains of up to 12.5 percentage points. Together, our results suggest that reasoning fine-tuning globally reorganizes latent dynamics, offering a new lens for mechanistic analysis and process-level control of reasoning models.

### [12] [The Story Shapes the Agent: Narrative Priors in LLM Behavior](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18566)
*Yixuan Wang,James Lester,Shashank Srivastava*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how the narrative/story framing of a task versus assigned personas shape LLM agent behavior, finding that narrative priors (from the story) dominate personas and often hurt performance unless personas are grounded in concrete actions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Persona prompting is popular for steering LLM agents, but models often behave differently when the same decision structure is wrapped in different stories (e.g., medicine vs. tech support vs. mystery). The authors want to disentangle whether behavior comes from the narrative framing or the explicit persona instructions, and understand what kinds of persona descriptions actually transfer across tasks.

Method: They design three structurally isomorphic text-based investigation games (disease investigation, IT troubleshooting, murder mystery) with the same action space, progression, and resource constraints, varying only the narrative. They run 1,890 sessions across three LLMs and ten personas, quantifying how much behavioral variance is explained by narrative vs. persona. They identify narrative priors (systematic action tendencies induced by story framing) and test cross-narrative transfer. They analyze persona descriptions to find behavioral anchors—words mapping directly to concrete actions—and perform causal interventions by removing these anchors. They also test a fourth held-out narrative and derive a method for selecting personas that transfer better across narratives.

Result: Narrative priors strongly shape LLM agent behavior and explain 5–31 times more variance than personas, largely independent of model architecture. In two of three domains, these narrative priors correlate negatively with task success. Persona effects that do generalize across narratives are tied to behavioral anchors—concrete, action-linked words in the persona description. Removing anchor terms from a high-transfer persona reduces cross-narrative consistency by 95%. Their analytical framework extends to a new, held-out narrative and supports a persona-selection method that improves behavior transfer across narratives.

Conclusion: The dominant driver of LLM agent behavior is the narrative framing of the task, not the nominal persona, and these narrative priors can hinder performance. To achieve behavior that is robust across different stories, personas should be grounded in explicit, concrete actions rather than abstract character traits. Careful design of personas and narratives—and attention to behavioral anchors—can improve cross-narrative consistency and task success.

Abstract: Persona prompting is widely used to steer LLM agent behavior, yet the narrative framing of a task can matter more than the assigned persona. We isolate this effect through structural isomorphism, constructing three text-based investigation games that share the same action space, stage progression, and resource constraints while varying only task narrative: disease investigation, IT troubleshooting, and murder mystery. Across 1,890 sessions spanning 3 models and 10 personas, we identify narrative priors: systematic action tendencies activated by a task's story framing, independent of its decision structure. Narrative priors explain 5-31x more behavioral variance than persona, are consistent across model architectures, and in two of three domains are negatively associated with task success. Persona effects that do transfer across narratives arise from behavioral anchors, persona descriptions whose language maps directly onto shared actions. Causal interventions confirm this: removing anchor words from a high-transfer persona reduces cross-narrative consistency by 95%. Our framework also generalizes to a held-out fourth narrative and yields a persona-selection method that improves cross-narrative transfer. These results suggest that LLM behavior that survives narrative changes should be grounded in concrete actions rather than abstract descriptions.

  <summary>Details</summary>

They inspect layerwise token contributions and decision dynamics across the sequence to see when and where the model commits to a discourse relation and how that signal propagates through the network.

Result: They find that some early layers make preliminary predictive decisions at mid-sequence tokens, while certain mid-level layers finalize these decisions closer to the final token, with many remaining layers mostly propagating previously formed decisions instead of reshaping them. They also observe that some layers systematically favor one discourse label over alternatives, indicating an asymmetric internal representation of discourse-based reasoning.

Conclusion: Instruction-tuned Transformers encode discourse relations in a temporally and structurally non-uniform way: specific early and mid layers are crucial decision points, while others largely act as carriers of existing signals. Moreover, the internal representations are asymmetric across discourse types, with some layers biased toward particular relations. These insights refine our understanding of how LMs perform discourse reasoning and point to targeted layers for potential intervention or improvement.

Abstract: Discourse relations provide document structure, critical to language understanding and enabling language model performance and ethicality. In this work, we investigate how instruction-tuned Transformer models (LLaMA and Mistral) encode discourse relations in English, with a particular focus on the contrasting relations of causation and antithesis. Framing the task as a next-token prediction task and applying a suite of interpretability techniques to test model internals, our findings show that certain early layers make predictive decisions at mid-sequence tokens, while some mid-level layers finalize their decisions closer to the last token. Most of the remaining layers primarily propagate earlier decisions rather than actively influencing them. Additionally, we observe that some layers exhibit a preference for one answer over alternatives, suggesting asymmetric representation of discourse-based reasoning.\footnote{Our code is available at https://github.com/abhidipbhattacharyya/causation_vs_antithesis}

### [14] [Stochastic Meta-Unlearning: Bridging Language Backbone and Multimodal Unlearning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18615)
*Zijie Liu,Jinhao Duan,Gaowen Liu,Sijia Liu,Tianlong Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Stochastic Meta-Unlearning (SMU), a bilevel framework to make machine unlearning effective and transferable for vision-language models (VLMs) by using VLM-level feedback while only updating the language backbone.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing machine unlearning work mainly focuses on single-modality (often text-only) models. For VLMs, simply unlearning on the language backbone with text-only signals is insufficient, because the visual components can help the full VLM recover information that appears forgotten by the text model alone. This gap shows that reliable unlearning for VLMs needs to account for the full multimodal behavior rather than just the language part in isolation.

Method: The authors introduce Stochastic Meta-Unlearning (SMU), a bilevel optimization framework. In the inner loop, they run a few unlearning steps on the language backbone using text data (simulating various unlearning tasks). In the outer loop, they plug this updated backbone back into the frozen VLM, then evaluate both forgetting of the target data and retention of general utility at the VLM level. The outer-loop feedback then shapes the backbone’s initialization so that future unlearning (performed with only text) will lead to desirable behavior when combined with vision modules.

Result: On two different VLM architectures and two multimodal meme datasets, and against three baseline unlearning strategies, SMU achieves the best trade-off between forgetting targeted content and preserving overall performance. Quantitatively, relative to the strongest baseline per metric, SMU lowers Forget-set accuracy by 10.52 points (better forgetting) while increasing Retain and Test accuracies by 20.10 and 17.01 points, respectively (better utility).

Conclusion: Text-only unlearning on the language backbone is not sufficient for VLMs because visual features can reintroduce supposedly forgotten information. Using VLM-level feedback during training, as in SMU, yields a backbone that supports more reliable and more transferable unlearning: it better forgets targets while maintaining performance, and it generalizes to new forgetting targets and different downstream unlearning procedures.

Abstract: Machine unlearning for vision-language models (VLMs) remains underexplored. Unlike language models, VLMs combine a language backbone with visual components, which makes unlearning more complex. There is a surprising phenomenon when moving from single-modality unlearning to VLM unlearning: a target forgotten by the standalone language backbone can still be recovered when image information is given to the full VLM. This shows that text-only feedback is not enough for reliable VLM unlearning. Motivated by this observation, we propose Stochastic Meta-Unlearning (SMU), a bilevel framework that uses VLM-level feedback to learn an unlearning-ready initialization. In the inner loop, SMU applies a few unlearning steps to the language backbone using text data. In the outer loop, SMU recomposes the updated backbone with the frozen VLM and evaluates forgetting and utility at the VLM level. This design makes the unlearning update aware of the final multimodal behavior, while still keeping the update local to the language backbone. Experiments on two VLMs, two multimodal meme datasets, and three baselines show that SMU achieves the best overall forget-retain trade-off. Compared with the strongest baseline for each metric, SMU reduces average Forget accuracy by 10.52 points and improves average Retain and Test accuracy by 20.10 and 17.01 points, respectively. More importantly, SMU also transfers to new forgetting targets and to different meta-test unlearning methods. These results suggest that VLM-level feedback can make language-backbone unlearning more reliable and more transferable for VLMs.

### [15] [LatentMT: Machine Translation with Latent Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18618)
*Wei-Rui Chen,Samar M. Magdy,Chiyu Zhang,Wenhui Zhu,Zhipeng Wang,Muhammad Abdul-Mageed*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents LatentMT, a machine translation system that uses latent recurrent reasoning in hidden states instead of larger models or explicit chain-of-thought, achieving comparable or better performance with less compute.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern machine translation systems typically scale by increasing model size or by adding explicit chain-of-thought tokens, both of which are computationally expensive. The authors seek an alternative scaling route that improves translation quality and efficiency without greatly increasing parameter count or token-level computation. They want to understand whether additional reasoning done purely in latent space (hidden states) can provide a better compute–performance tradeoff, especially for mid- and low-resource languages.

Method: The authors build LatentMT by adapting a relatively small 2.6B-parameter backbone language model with a looped, latent-reasoning architecture (LoopLM). This architecture performs multiple recurrent reasoning steps entirely in the hidden states before emitting tokens. They evaluate LatentMT on 32 translation directions spanning high-, mid-, and low-resource language pairs, and systematically vary the number of recurrent reasoning steps to study scaling behavior. They also conduct mechanistic analyses of hidden representations across recurrent steps and perform efficiency comparisons (training and inference compute) against larger, non-latent-reasoning MT models with similar quality.

Result: LatentMT, despite being based on a compact 2.6B-parameter model and trained with lightweight adaptation, matches or approaches the performance of models that are three to five times larger across 32 translation directions. It is competitive on high-resource language translation and achieves state-of-the-art results on mid-resource and low-resource language settings. Increasing the number of recurrent reasoning steps improves translation quality for the first few steps but then quickly saturates. Hidden-state analyses reveal that representations become more similar across later recurrent steps, aligning with the observed performance saturation. Efficiency measurements show that LatentMT uses less training and inference compute than larger non-latent-reasoning systems of comparable quality.

Conclusion: Latent recurrent reasoning in hidden states is an effective and efficient scaling strategy for machine translation. A relatively small LoopLM-based system, LatentMT, can rival or surpass much larger models, particularly in mid- and low-resource scenarios, while saving compute. The rapid saturation of gains with more recurrent steps, together with shrinking differences in hidden representations, suggests that only a modest number of latent reasoning iterations is needed. Overall, the work positions latent recurrent computation as a promising direction for building compact, strong, and compute-efficient MT systems.

Abstract: Latent-reasoning looped language models (LoopLMs) offer a different scaling path for machine translation (MT): instead of increasing parameter count or emitting explicit chain-of-thought tokens, they spend additional recurrent computation inside hidden states. We introduce LatentMT, the first systematic study of latent-reasoning LoopLMs for machine translation. LatentMT adapts a small 2.6B-parameter backbone model with lightweight training. Across 32 translation directions spanning high-, mid-, and low-resource languages, LatentMT achieves performance comparable to models three to five times larger. It is competitive in a high-resource language and achieves state-of-the-art performance on both mid-resource and low-resource languages. Studying the behavior of scaling the number of recurrent reasoning steps, we find that recurrent computation consistently improves translation quality in early steps, then saturates quickly afterwards. Our mechanistic analysis shows that hidden-representation differences shrink along the recurrent reasoning-step axis, supporting the observed saturation in performance. Finally, our efficiency analysis shows that LatentMT requires lower training and inference compute than much larger non-latent-reasoning models with similar performance, making latent recurrent computation a promising path toward compact, efficient, and strong machine translation.

### [16] [Fusion Embedding: A Unified Embedding Space for Text, Image, Video, and Audio](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18666)
*Abdul Basit Tonmoy,Kazi Fardinul Hoque,Md. Shahrier Islam Arham,Arman Luthra*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Fusion Embedding, a family of models that extend a frozen vision-language embedding base to include audio, enabling a single shared embedding space for text, images, video, and audio, trained efficiently with small adapters and without modifying the base model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current state-of-the-art embedding models for multimodal retrieval either focus on vision-language (text, images, video) without audio, or on audio-text only, resulting in fragmented systems that cannot support all modalities in a unified way. The authors aim to create a single embedding space spanning all four modalities that (1) reuses strong existing vision-language backbones, (2) keeps those backbones unchanged for stability and compatibility, and (3) can be trained efficiently and cheaply. They also want to understand design and training choices that help or hurt performance when extending frozen decoder-LM–based embedding models.

Method: They start from a strong frozen vision-language backbone that already aligns text, images, and video in a shared embedding space. Generation 1 (fusion-embedding-1) attaches a frozen audio tower and trains only a lightweight 16.4M-parameter connector to map audio features into the base embedding space, keeping all backbone parameters frozen. Generation 2 (fusion-embedding-2) adds modality-gated deep adapters (44.2M parameters) whose additional computation is activated only for audio inputs; for text, image, and video, the model’s outputs are enforced to be bit-identical to the original base. Training aligns audio to text embeddings only, relying on the existing text–image–video binding to induce cross-modal behavior like audio–image retrieval without any audio–visual paired data. They systematically explore architectural and training-setup variants, including LLM-rewritten captions, stronger alternative audio towers, and wider connectors, and evaluate their impact on retrieval performance.

Result: Fusion Embedding achieves strong retrieval performance across text, image, video, and audio, matching or improving on existing systems while adding audio capability to a vision-language base without modifying its parameters. The models are compact (16.4M–44.2M trainable parameters on top of the backbone) and train in hours on a single GPU. Remarkably, aligning audio only to text in the shared space makes audio–image retrieval emerge without any direct audio–image training data. Their ablation and negative-result study shows that several intuitive modifications—such as LLM-rewritten captions, swapping in a leaderboard-stronger audio tower, or widening the connector—actually degrade retrieval, and they identify training protocol choices that are important to performance.

Conclusion: A single unified embedding space over text, images, video, and audio can be built efficiently by extending a frozen vision-language backbone with small, audio-specific components, preserving the original model’s behavior for non-audio modalities. Audio–text alignment alone is sufficient to induce broader cross-modal retrieval capabilities such as audio–image matching, avoiding the need for expensive audio–visual datasets. The approach is practical—trainable on a single GPU in hours—and the authors expect the design and training insights to generalize to other frozen decoder-LM embedding backbones. They release model weights, code, and evaluation tools to facilitate adoption and further research.

Abstract: A single embedding space that covers text, images, video, and audio lets one index serve every query a user can pose. Embedding models built on vision-language backbones now lead text/image/video retrieval benchmarks but lack audio entirely, while audio-text retrieval is led by specialist systems that serve no other modality. We present the Fusion Embedding family, which adds audio to a frozen vision-language embedding base whose parameters are never updated: generation 1 (fusion-embedding-1) trains only a 16.4M-parameter connector between a frozen audio tower and the frozen base, and generation 2 (fusion-embedding-2) adds modality-gated deep adapters (44.2M parameters) whose branch never executes on text, image, or video inputs: their outputs are bit-for-bit those of the released base, verified after every training run. Because the base already binds text, images, and video, aligning audio to text alone makes audio-image retrieval emerge, with zero paired audio-visual training data. Alongside the recipe we map its design space with controlled negative results (rewriting training captions with an LLM, substituting a leaderboard-stronger audio tower, and widening the connector each reduce retrieval) and with training-protocol findings that we expect to transfer to any frozen decoder-LM embedding backbone. Both generations train in hours on a single GPU. Weights, code, and the evaluation harness are openly released.

Method: The authors use Chain-of-Thought prompting with a Large Language Model to generate rationales that explain stance decisions. Cross-lingual stance detection alleviates this problem by transferring stance knowledge from resource-rich languages to low-resource languages. ### [18] [Find Before You Fine-Tune: A Diagnostic Study of Small LLMs for Cybersecurity QA](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18725)
*Shaswata Mitra,Subash Neupane,Trisha Chakraborty,Himanshu Tripathi,Sudip Mittal,Aritran Piplai,Shahram Rahimi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces FiT, a diagnostic framework to evaluate small LLMs for cybersecurity QA before fine-tuning, showing that fine-tuning can harm vocabulary and parametric knowledge and that pre-tuning FiT scores can predict these effects.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fine-tuning small LLMs for specialized domains like cybersecurity is expensive and risky: it may improve domain alignment but can also degrade existing knowledge, instruction-following, and hallucination behavior, especially when labeled data are limited or fast-changing. Practitioners lack a principled way to choose which base model to fine-tune for critical-domain QA. The authors aim to provide a task-oriented diagnostic to evaluate and compare candidate models prior to fine-tuning, reducing wasted effort and avoiding harmful deployments.

Method: The authors propose FiT (Find before Fine-Tune), a diagnostic framework that decomposes performance on cybersecurity QA into three measurable capabilities: (1) vocabulary recognition (understanding domain-specific terms), (2) parametric knowledge (what the model already “knows” in its weights), and (3) contextualization of retrieved information (using external retrieval effectively). They then apply FiT to five open-weight 7B-parameter LLMs under two fine-tuning regimes: knowledge-focused tuning and instruction-focused tuning. They evaluate how each regime affects the three capabilities and compare pre- and post-fine-tuning rankings using rank-correlation analysis.

Result: Empirically, fine-tuning small LLMs for cybersecurity QA does not provide uniform benefits. Both fine-tuning regimes consistently degrade vocabulary recognition and parametric knowledge, but in different patterns: knowledge-focused tuning leads to moderate degradation while largely preserving the ranking between models; instruction-focused tuning induces abstention behavior that makes models appear to “forget” knowledge, inverting the prior knowledge ranking. Despite this, retrieval-based contextualization remains largely unaffected by instruction-focused tuning. The authors also find that FiT scores obtained before fine-tuning are predictive of the direction and nature of performance changes after fine-tuning.

Conclusion: Task-oriented diagnosis via FiT can be used to screen small LLMs before fine-tuning, helping practitioners identify unsuitable models, avoid unnecessary or harmful fine-tuning, and design safer cybersecurity QA pipelines. Fine-tuning regimes impact different capabilities in distinct ways, so blindly fine-tuning for domain alignment can erode valuable knowledge and instruction-following. Pre-tuning diagnostics should therefore be an integral part of deploying small LLMs in safety-critical domains like cybersecurity.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly fine-tuned for critical-domain Question-Answering (QA), yet choosing which small model to adapt, before paying the cost of adaptation, remains difficult. Fine-tuning can improve domain alignment, but it may also erode prior knowledge, weaken instruction-following, or increase hallucination, especially when labeled data are scarce or rapidly evolving as in cybersecurity. We present FiT (Find before Fine-Tune), a task-oriented diagnostic framework that characterizes small LLMs along three capabilities required for cybersecurity QA: vocabulary recognition, parametric knowledge, and contextualization of retrieved information. Using FiT, we conduct an empirical study of five open-weight 7-billion-parameter models under two fine-tuning regimes. We find that fine-tuning does not uniformly help: it consistently degrades vocabulary and parametric knowledge in small models, and the two regimes trade off differently. Knowledge-focused tuning causes moderate, rank-preserving degradation, whereas instruction-focused tuning collapses measured knowledge through induced abstention, inverting the knowledge ranking while leaving retrieval-grounded contextualization essentially intact. We quantify these regime-specific patterns with rank-correlation analysis and show that pre-fine-tuning FiT scores anticipate the direction of post-tuning change. Our results suggest that task-oriented diagnosis can screen out unsuitable models, avoid unnecessary fine-tuning, and support safer deployment of small LLMs in cybersecurity QA pipelines.

### [19] [Dual Attention Residuals](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18730)
*Xingda Yu,Yining Li,Xinzhang Liu,Zhihao Yang,Haowei He,Chao Wang,Yongxiang Li,Shuangyong Song*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Dual Attention Residuals (DAR), a multi-stream historical retrieval mechanism for Transformers that lets each residual stream choose which depths to retrieve based on the other stream’s states, improving validation loss and preserving diverse depthwise representations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing extensions of Transformer residual pathways fall into two mostly separate lines: (1) historical retrieval, where layers can attend to earlier depths in the network, and (2) multi-stream architectures, where multiple residual streams are maintained in parallel. These have largely been studied in isolation; simply assigning an independent retriever to each stream prevents depth selection in one trajectory from being informed by the other. This can limit representational diversity or lead to redundancy/imbalance across streams. The motivation is to integrate cross-stream interaction directly into the historical retrieval process so that one stream can guide where in depth the other should read from, potentially yielding richer, more complementary representations and better performance without fundamentally changing the Transformer branch structure.

Method: The authors introduce Dual Attention Residuals (DAR), a two-stream residual design with reciprocal cross-stream depth selection. For a given target stream, DAR normalizes states in the opposite stream and uses them to compute depth weights over the target stream’s own historical residual states. These weights define a historical retrieval over the target’s depth-wise history, but the addressing is conditioned on the other stream. The retrieved historical states are then merged with the current state within an otherwise standard Transformer block, and the history is updated using constrained gated writes to control how much new information is stored. A block-form DAR variant aggregates histories at the block level to reduce computational and memory overhead. The method is evaluated in dense models ranging from 0.1B to 1B parameters and in a 7B-parameter sparse Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) model, with ablations that remove or alter routing components (extra stream, value projection, etc.) to isolate which aspects are responsible for performance gains. Representation and causal intervention analyses are used to study how depth-wise diversity and cross-stream interactions behave under DAR versus alternative two-stream designs.

Result: Across all tested dense model scales (0.1B–1B parameters) and a 7B sparse-MoE setting, DAR yields consistent improvements in validation loss compared to both a standard residual Transformer baseline and a prior Attention Residuals architecture. Routing ablations show that simply adding an additional stream or value projections does not recover the performance seen with DAR’s reciprocal cross-stream selection, indicating the specific addressing mechanism is critical. Representation analyses reveal that DAR maintains diverse depth-wise representations across streams, avoiding collapse into redundant features. Intervention experiments further indicate that DAR mitigates the functional redundancy or imbalance that arises in alternative two-stream setups, where one stream may dominate or become underutilized.

Conclusion: Dual Attention Residuals provide an effective way to couple multi-stream architectures with historical retrieval in Transformers by letting each stream determine depth selection for the other. This reciprocal cross-stream addressing leads to better validation loss across a range of model sizes and architectures without altering the main Transformer branch. The gains cannot be attributed to mere architectural size or simple value projections, but rather to the specific cross-stream routing scheme. DAR preserves and exploits depth-wise diversity between streams, avoiding redundancy and functional imbalance that can plague two-stream designs. Overall, the work suggests that structured cross-stream depth selection is a promising direction for improving Transformer residual pathways and scaling performance efficiently.

Abstract: Recent work extends Transformer residual pathways along two complementary axes: historical retrieval selects information from earlier depths, whereas multi-stream methods maintain multiple residual trajectories. These capabilities have largely been studied in isolation, and assigning an independent retriever to each stream still prevents one trajectory from influencing depth selection in another. We propose Dual Attention Residuals (DAR), which brings multi-stream interaction into historical retrieval through reciprocal cross-stream addressing. For each target stream, DAR computes depth weights from normalized states in the opposite stream and applies them to values from the target stream's own history. The retrieved states are combined for an unchanged Transformer branch and updated through constrained gated writes; a block-form variant operates on block-level histories to control overhead. Across dense models from 0.1B to 1B parameters and a 7B sparse-MoE model, DAR consistently improves validation loss over standard residual Transformers and Attention Residuals. Routing ablations show that the gain cannot be explained by an additional stream or value projection alone. Representation and intervention analyses further show that reciprocal cross-stream selection preserves depth-wise diversity and avoids the redundancy or functional imbalance observed in alternative two-stream designs.

</details>

*Yueqi Xing,Houbo He,Jolie Wang,Erin Ni,Shikai Wang,Qiufeng Li,Weidong Cao,Taiyun Chi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces RF-Agent, a textbook-driven multi-agent system that builds an RF-domain reasoning dataset and benchmark to improve LLM performance in RF circuit design, evaluating SFT and RAG adaptation strategies.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Result: The resulting RF-domain dataset and benchmark enable systematic evaluation of RF reasoning. Domain-specific supervised fine-tuning substantially boosts RF reasoning performance, particularly for small and mid-sized models, while among RAG methods, semantic retrieval outperforms keyword and hybrid approaches.

Conclusion: RF-Agent offers the first reusable RF-domain reasoning dataset and benchmark, showing that domain-specific SFT and semantic RAG are effective for RF reasoning, and laying a foundation for future research on LLM-assisted RF circuit design.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have driven rapid progress in electronic design automation (EDA), yet their application to radio-frequency (RF) circuit design remains limited by the scarcity of domain-specific datasets and standardized benchmarks. We present RF-Agent, which addresses this gap through textbook-driven knowledge distillation. On this benchmark we study two adaptation strategies: supervised fine-tuning (SFT) and three retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) configurations (semantic, keyword, hybrid). Across multiple LLM families, domain-specific SFT significantly improves RF reasoning, especially for small and medium-sized models; among RAG configurations, semantic retrieval performs best, indicating embedding-based context alignment suits RF reasoning better than naive fusion. The dataset and benchmark provide a reusable foundation for future work on LLM-aided RF circuit design.
