### [21] [CASE: Causal Alignment and Structural Enforcement for Improving Chain-of-Thought Faithfulness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18820)
*Ziming Wang,Yinghua Yao,Changwu Huang,Ke Tang,Xin Yao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CASE, a framework to make chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning more causally faithful to answers by removing direct shortcuts from instructions to answers and enforcing that answers depend on the generated reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although CoT improves performance and interpretability of LLMs, the produced reasoning often does not causally support the final answer; models can exploit instruction-to-answer shortcuts, hurting faithfulness. The authors aim to formally characterize and mitigate this gap so that CoT genuinely mediates between instructions and answers.

Method: They model faithful reasoning as a causal chain Z→X→Y, where Z is the instruction, X the CoT, and Y the answer. They propose CASE, which has two components: (1) training-time causal alignment that constructs counterfactual-CoT, biased-instruction, and empty-instruction datasets and uses selective-loss fine-tuning to increase CoT-to-answer dependence while suppressing instruction-to-answer shortcuts; (2) inference-time structural enforcement that masks direct attention from instruction tokens to answer tokens so the model must use the generated CoT. They complement this with an information-theoretic analysis of how these mechanisms promote faithfulness.

Result: Across three LLMs and four CoT benchmarks, CASE yields on average a 37% relative improvement in CoT faithfulness compared to strong baselines, transfers better across datasets in terms of faithfulness, and preserves competitive task accuracy.

Conclusion: Enforcing a causal structure where instructions influence answers only via the chain-of-thought leads to more faithful reasoning without sacrificing much performance. CASE demonstrates that combining causal-aligned training with architectural constraints at inference can significantly improve the trustworthiness of CoT explanations in LLMs.

Abstract: Chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning is widely used to improve both the performance and interpretability of large language models (LLMs), yet the generated reasoning may not faithfully support the final answer. We study this problem from a causal perspective, where a faithful CoT process should follow the chain $Z\rightarrow X\rightarrow Y$, with $Z$, $X$, and $Y$ denoting the instruction, reasoning chain, and final answer, respectively. In this process, the instruction should affect the answer only through the reasoning chain. However, conventional autoregressive LLMs condition answer generation on both the instruction and the CoT, which still allows a direct instruction-to-answer shortcut. To address this issue, we propose CASE, a framework that combines training-time causal alignment and inference-time structural enforcement. During training, CASE builds counterfactual-CoT, biased-instruction, and empty-instruction datasets, and applies selective-loss fine-tuning to strengthen CoT-to-answer dependence while suppressing instruction shortcuts. During inference, CASE masks direct attention from instruction tokens to answer tokens, preventing the model from bypassing the generated CoT. We provide an information-theoretic analysis showing how these components promote faithful chains. Experiments on three models and four benchmarks show that CASE achieves a 37\% average per-setting relative improvement in overall CoT faithfulness over the strongest baselines, exhibits stronger cross-dataset faithfulness transfer, and maintains competitive average accuracy. Code is available at https://github.com/oddwang/CASE.

</details>

Additionally, we assessed performance on standardized tests such as the All India Bar Examination (AIBE), thereby providing a robust benchmark for practical applications. Under the study's evaluation protocol, some AI-generated responses received higher ratings than the available reference answers, particularly when they contained accurate and relevant supporting details. This finding is specific to the evaluated dataset and rating criteria and should not be interpreted as evidence that the models generally outperform qualified legal professionals. We also discuss the challenges encountered, such as the need for precise context and the risks of model hallucination, and propose directions for future research to further refine AI capabilities in the legal field. This study aims to pave the way for enhanced legal decision-support systems, making them more accessible and effective for legal professionals and the public alike.

### [23] [HPD-Parsing: Hierarchical Parallel Document Parsing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18839)
*Shu Wei,Jingjing Wu,Lingshu Zhang,Qunyi Xie,Hao Zou,Le Xiang,Xu Fan,Yangliu Xu,Manhui Lin,Xiaolong Ma,Cheng Cui,Tengyu Du,YY*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes HPD-Parsing, a hierarchical parallel decoding paradigm for VLM-based document parsing that removes the full-page autoregressive bottleneck, significantly boosting throughput while keeping accuracy competitive.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Unified VLM-based document parsers currently decode an entire page via a single autoregressive token stream, making inference latency grow with document length. This design ignores that document layout requires global reasoning, but once blocks are identified, their content can be parsed independently and in parallel. The motivation is to exploit this inherent structure to overcome the sequential decoding bottleneck and make unified document parsing substantially more efficient without sacrificing accuracy.

Method: The authors design HPD-Parsing, a Hierarchical Parallel Decoding framework. A main layout branch first analyzes the page globally and predicts a high-level document structure, segmenting it into blocks (e.g., paragraphs, tables, figures). For each block, the model dynamically spawns concurrent decoding branches that parse the block content in parallel instead of in a single long sequence. Within each branch, they introduce progressive multi-token prediction (P-MTP), which predicts multiple tokens per decoding step, further shortening the decoding trajectory. The system is implemented on top of a VLM-based unified document parser so that the only major change is the decoding paradigm rather than the core perception or representation modules.

Result: On public document parsing benchmarks, HPD-Parsing yields a decoding speed of 4,752 tokens per second. This corresponds to a 2.62× throughput improvement over the fastest existing document parsing model and a 3.06× improvement over a vanilla autoregressive baseline. Importantly, these gains are achieved while preserving competitive parsing accuracy compared with state-of-the-art unified parsers, demonstrating that efficiency can be improved markedly without notable loss in quality.

Conclusion: The study shows that full-page autoregressive generation is not the only viable paradigm for unified document parsing and that exploiting the natural hierarchy in documents enables substantial efficiency gains. Hierarchical Parallel Decoding, combining a global layout branch with parallel block-level content branches and P-MTP, offers a scalable and accurate alternative. This opens a new research direction focused on structured, parallel decoding strategies for vision-language document understanding systems.

Abstract: Efficient teamwork typically combines global coordination with parallel execution, a principle not yet fully reflected in unified Vision-Language Model (VLM)-based document parsers. Existing unified parsers process an entire page jointly but generate its output through a single token-by-token autoregressive trajectory, creating a sequential bottleneck that grows with document length. Such full-page sequential generation overlooks a key property of document parsing: layout must be analyzed globally, whereas block content can be parsed in parallel. Based on this observation, we introduce HPD-Parsing, which replaces full-page autoregressive generation with a Hierarchical Parallel Decoding paradigm. A main layout branch organizes the overall document structure and dynamically assigns block-level content decoding to concurrent branches, while progressive multi-token prediction (P-MTP) further reduces the decoding steps within each branch. Experiments on public benchmarks show that HPD-Parsing achieves 4,752 tokens per second, delivering $2.62\times$ the throughput of the fastest existing document parsing model and $3.06\times$ that of the vanilla autoregressive baseline, while maintaining competitive parsing accuracy. These results establish hierarchical parallel decoding as an effective alternative to full-page autoregressive generation, opening a new direction for efficient unified document parsing.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: African-language ASR suffers from messy data (inconsistent spelling, artifacts, missing audio), speaker/domain imbalance, and evaluations that don’t match real streaming deployment. There is also a lack of transparent, end‑to‑end engineering reports on how to adapt large multilingual streaming models to specific low‑resource languages while preserving streaming constraints.

Method: Starting from an existing Kenyan Swahili–adapted checkpoint of NVIDIA Nemotron 3.5 ASR Streaming 0.6B, they fully fine‑tune all parameters for Kikuyu, Dholuo, and Kalenjin while keeping the underlying FastConformer RNN‑T architecture, cache‑aware streaming setup, prompt conditioning, and streaming decoder. Starting from a Kenyan Swahili-adapted checkpoint, we retain its cache-aware FastConformer RNN-T, prompt conditioning, and streaming decoder during full-parameter fine-tuning. The study covers corpus auditing, Unicode normalization, split checks, duration filtering, low-rate continuation, validation-based checkpoint selection, true-streaming evaluation, artifact preservation, and isolated serving.
  On internal, adaptively consulted evaluation sets excluded from gradient updates at context [56,13], selected Kikuyu and Dholuo models achieve 42.97% and 33.98% WER, respectively. Dholuo records 9.59% CER and 8.13% no-space CER under its frozen historical label policy; Kikuyu records 7.79% no-space CER. Kalenjin remains a work in progress: v1-v reaches 68.74% WER on a 2,411-row clean-v3 diagnostic subset excluding long-pause annotations, digit-bearing references, and targets shorter than three tokens. Its checkpoint selection used a mixed-source validation manifest containing test-origin rows, so the score is not an independent generalization estimate. We also report negative findings involving non-speech labels, short-utterance over-generation, boundary-sensitive WER, and cloud job-lifecycle failures. We make no state-of-the-art claim because the internal sets, repeated consultation, and normalization differ from public benchmarks. This work provides an auditable account of adapting a multilingual streaming model into language-specific systems without discarding streaming constraints.

### [25] [Reasoning Error from Known Fact: Step-Level Self-Consistency Group Relative Policy Optimization for LLM](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18915)
*Xiaomeng Hu,Jiaqi Hu,Hao Chen,Qi Zhang,Zhanming Shen,Wentao Ye,Junbo Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes and mitigates hallucinations that emerge inside long reasoning traces of LLMs and introduces SSC-GRPO, a step-level self-consistency RL method that improves both reasoning accuracy and hallucination robustness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs perform longer, multi-step reasoning, they increasingly generate hallucinated content within their internal reasoning traces. Many of these errors are not due to lack of knowledge but arise from context-sensitive interference during reasoning, and they are hard to detect with existing methods. There is a need for fine-grained analysis and targeted training techniques that operate at the step level to reduce such hallucinations without harming reasoning ability.

Method: The authors first conduct a fine-grained empirical analysis of hallucinations in LLM reasoning and identify a dominant class they term Context-Sensitive Factual Hallucinations, where the model has access to correct knowledge but still makes step-wise factual errors because of misleading context. To mitigate this, they propose Step-level Self-Consistency Group Relative Policy Optimization (SSC-GRPO), an RL-style training approach. SSC-GRPO generates multiple reasoning rollouts for the same query, computes a self-consistency score for each individual reasoning step across rollouts, and converts these scores into step-level rewards. These rewards are then used in a group relative policy optimization framework to adjust the model’s policy so that frequently self-consistent, factually sound steps are reinforced while inconsistent, hallucination-prone steps are penalized.

Result: SSC-GRPO achieves state-of-the-art results on mathematical reasoning benchmarks as well as on hallucination leaderboards, outperforming previous methods designed for reasoning enhancement or hallucination reduction. The experiments indicate that applying self-consistency at the step level during training significantly reduces context-sensitive factual hallucinations without sacrificing, and in fact improving, overall reasoning performance.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that a major source of hallucinations in LLM reasoning comes from context-sensitive factual errors within reasoning traces, even when the underlying knowledge is present. By using step-level self-consistency as a reward signal within an RL framework (SSC-GRPO), LLMs can be trained to produce more reliable intermediate reasoning, leading to better final answers and fewer hallucinations. This step-wise perspective offers a promising direction for future work on training and evaluating LLM reasoning processes.

Abstract: With the rapid advancement of large language models (LLMs), modern systems not only possess strong foundational capabilities and extensive knowledge, but can also solve complex problems via long, multi-step reasoning. However, as reasoning traces become longer, LLMs may produce a substantial amount of hallucinated content during the reasoning process, which is often difficult to detect. In this work, we conduct a fine-grained analysis of hallucinations arising in LLM reasoning and find that the reasoning traces are particularly prone to Context-Sensitive Factual Hallucinations: cases where the model actually has the relevant knowledge, yet makes factual errors due to contextual interference during reasoning. To address this issue, we propose Step-level Self-Consistency Group Relative Policy Optimization (SSC-GRPO), which assigns step-level rewards to reasoning traces by computing self-consistency scores of individual steps across multiple rollouts. Compared with prior methods, SSC-GRPO achieves state-of-the-art performance on both mathematical reasoning benchmarks and hallucination leaderboards. Our results offer a new perspective for detecting and mitigating hallucinations in the reasoning process of large language models.

### [26] [Transcription Policy as a Latent Variable: Activating Controllable Verbatim ASR with Word-Level Timing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18934)
*Laurin Wagner,Mario Zusag,Bernhard Thallinger*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Paper tackles instability in ASR caused by mixed transcription styles and introduces mechanisms to explicitly control and exploit verbatim vs. intended modes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: ASR systems are trained on data with inconsistent transcription styles (verbatim with disfluencies vs. cleaned-up intended text). Because models are not given explicit control over style, this latent factor harms decoding stability, inflates or confounds WER metrics, and yields poor word-level timing, especially around disfluencies. There is also a lack of scalable methods to generate high-quality verbatim annotations for large corpora.

Method: 1) Empirically show that existing ASR models implicitly encode both transcription styles. 2) Introduce coverage-aware decoder task tokens that explicitly steer decoding toward verbatim or intended style, trained using parallel pairs of verbatim vs. intended transcripts. 3) Evaluate zero-shot transfer to German disfluency detection using English-only training. 4) Perform full English-only fine-tuning for both styles and compare to baselines on verbatim accuracy, disfluency detection, and intended-mode transcription quality in English and German. 5) Propose supervised cross-attention fine-tuning for better alignment between acoustic frames and word outputs, improving timestamp quality on disfluent speech beyond forced alignment. 6) Define a new ASR task, “verbatimize,” for generating canonical verbatim transcripts to enrich existing speech corpora.

Result: 1) Show that style (verbatim vs. intended) is a latent variable already present in models but previously uncontrolled. 2) With coverage-aware style tokens, achieve a jump in German disfluency F1 from 10% to 79% in a zero-shot setting despite training only on English data. 3) English-only fine-tuning with explicit style control outperforms all baselines in verbatim transcription, disfluency detection, and intended-style transcription for both English and German. 4) Supervised cross-attention fine-tuning yields better word-level timestamps on disfluent speech than traditional forced-alignment methods. 5) Demonstrate feasibility of the proposed “verbatimize” task for scalable creation of high-quality verbatim transcriptions.

Conclusion: Transcription style should be treated as an explicit, controllable dimension in ASR rather than an uncontrolled latent variable. By introducing style-specific decoder tokens and cross-attention fine-tuning, ASR systems can achieve more stable decoding, more accurate disfluency detection, better cross-lingual transfer, and improved word-level timing. The new “verbatimize” task enables scalable enrichment of speech corpora with canonical verbatim annotations, paving the way for more reliable training and evaluation of ASR models across styles and languages.

Abstract: Modern ASR models trained on heterogeneously annotated data treat transcription style (verbatim vs. intended) as an uncontrolled latent variable, causing measurable decoding instability, evaluation confounding (up to 60% of reported WER attributable to style mismatch), and unreliable word-level timing. We show that models already encode both styles; the challenge is controlled activation. Using coverage-aware decoder task tokens trained on parallel verbatim/intended transcript pairs, we raise German disfluency F1 from 10% to 79% zero-shot, despite English-only training. Full English-only fine-tuning surpasses all baselines in verbatim accuracy, disfluency detection, and intended-mode quality across both languages. We further introduce supervised cross-attention fine-tuning that improves word-level timestamps on disfluent speech beyond forced-alignment baselines. Finally, we propose verbatimize, a new task enabling scalable creation and enrichment of speech corpora with high-quality canonical verbatim transcriptions.

### [27] [Constrained CTC Decoding for Efficient Diacritic Restoration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18946)
*Rufael Marew,Amr Keleg,Hanan Aldarmaki*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an efficient CTC-based, non-autoregressive speech-to-text model for restoring Arabic diacritics, using a lattice of valid diacritizations as hard constraints during decoding, and shows it outperforms a more complex multimodal baseline in accuracy and efficiency on Classical and Modern Standard Arabic datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Arabic speech transcripts are typically written without diacritics, which encode key phonological and sometimes morphological information. This lack of diacritics hampers precise modeling of pronunciation and downstream speech and language processing tasks. While text-only diacritic restoration has been studied extensively, it cannot fully leverage the phonetic cues present in speech, and existing multimodal approaches can be computationally heavy. There is a need for a speech-aware, computationally efficient method to restore diacritics accurately in Arabic transcripts.

Method: The authors use a non-autoregressive speech-to-text diacritization model based on Connectionist Temporal Classification (CTC). Given an undiacritized transcript and its corresponding speech, they build a character-level diacritization lattice encoding all valid diacritized forms of the transcript. During CTC decoding, they impose hard constraints by restricting output hypotheses to paths that are consistent with this lattice, effectively pruning invalid diacritized sequences while leveraging the acoustic signal to choose among valid options.

Result: On Classical Arabic and Modern Standard Arabic test sets (ArVoice and ClArTTS), the proposed CTC-based constrained decoding system achieves statistically significant reductions in diacritic error rate compared to a stronger, more computationally expensive multimodal baseline. This demonstrates that the method not only improves restoration accuracy but is also more efficient computationally.

Conclusion: Incorporating hard, lattice-based diacritization constraints into a CTC speech-to-text model provides an effective and efficient solution for Arabic diacritic restoration. The approach successfully exploits speech information while limiting outputs to linguistically valid diacritized forms, leading to better performance than a more complex multimodal baseline. This suggests that non-autoregressive, constraint-aware decoding is a promising direction for diacritic restoration in Arabic and potentially other under-diacritized languages.

Abstract: In this work, we address diacritic restoration for Arabic speech transcripts. Most speech data are undiacritized, limiting the ability of modeling fine-grained phonological distinctions. The speech modality has recently been explored as a way to complement text-based diacritic restoration efforts. We propose an efficient non-autoregressive approach for speech-to-text diacritization based on Connectionist Temporal Classification (CTC). Our method incorporates hard constraints during decoding by constructing a character-level diacritization lattice from an undiacritized transcript and restricting hypotheses to valid diacritized realizations. We evaluate on Classical Arabic and Modern Standard Arabic test sets (namely, ArVoice and ClArTTS) against a more computationally-complex multi-modal diacritic restoration baseline, and show statistically significant reductions in diacritic error rates in both, demonstrating that the proposed approach offers both performance and efficiency gains.

### [28] [Verifiable Self-Evolution for Open-Ended Dialogue Skills via Future-Feedback Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18973)
*ChaoJin Zhao,Xuan Jiang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a way to let frozen language-model agents improve themselves offline by learning to predict future user feedback from logged conversations, turning moving conversational feedback into a stable, verifiable training signal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing self-improvement methods for language-model agents rely on stable, automatically verifiable signals (like checking math or code), but open-ended dialogue lacks such fixed validation because altering an answer would change the user’s subsequent reaction, so past logs cannot directly evaluate counterfactual responses. The authors want a method for reliable, reproducible offline skill evolution for dialogue agents without exposing every candidate skill to live traffic.

Method: They introduce “future-feedback skill evolution,” where instead of directly optimizing the current answer, the model first learns a textual skill that predicts whether a given assistant response (observed in a log) will lead to positive or negative future user feedback. This prediction can be validated on fixed logged (context, response, subsequent user signal) tuples and used with validation-gated textual optimization to refine the feedback-prediction skill. Once learned, this feedback skill—capturing interpretable quality criteria—then guides the diagnosis and optimization of answer-generation skills, all done offline on a proprietary, privacy-preserving sales-assistant dataset with carefully filtered and balanced resolved/unresolved conversations.

Result: On the curated sales-assistant dataset, the learned feedback-prediction skill achieves over 75% accuracy at predicting whether a response will lead to resolved versus unresolved outcomes, indicating that the model can reliably infer future user satisfaction signals from static logs.

Conclusion: By reframing conversational feedback prediction as an offline, verifiable task, the method enables reproducible, validation-gated evolution of textual skills for dialogue agents without requiring each candidate skill to be deployed in live traffic. This serves as an effective offline optimization stage that complements, but does not replace, final human or online evaluation, and clarifies the limits between what can be observationally verified from logs and what requires counterfactual or live assessment.

Abstract: Textual skills provide a lightweight way to improve frozen language-model agents, but their self-evolution normally requires a stable validation signal. Such signals are natural in mathematics or code, where an answer can be checked after it changes, yet are problematic in open-ended dialogue: changing the assistant response also changes the user's next reaction, so a logged reaction cannot directly evaluate a counterfactual response. We propose future-feedback skill evolution, which first redirects self-evolution from prescribing the current answer to predicting whether the observed answer will lead to a positive or negative subsequent user signal. This prediction task is verifiable on fixed logged tuples and therefore supports validation-gated textual optimization. The evolved feedback skill captures interpretable criteria for response quality and can subsequently serve as a diagnostic and optimization target for answer skills. On a proprietary, privacy-preserving sales-assistant dataset, careful quality filtering and a balanced resolved/unresolved split yield more than 75% prediction accuracy. Beyond this result, the central contribution is a formulation that converts otherwise moving conversational feedback into a fixed offline learning target, enabling reproducible skill evolution without placing every candidate skill in live traffic. We discuss the boundary between observational verification and counterfactual validity, and position the method as an offline optimization stage rather than a replacement for final human or online evaluation.

</details>


### [29] [AutoJourn: Multi-Perspective Summarisation, Bias Detection and Bias Neutralisation for LLM-Generated News in Automated Journalism](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18983)

The pipeline integrates advanced prompt engineering with optional retrieval augmentation to produce semantically diverse perspective sets, a multi-perspective summarisation module that merges conflicting viewpoints into balanced summaries, and a bias analysis suite supporting sentence-level bias detection and type classification in the generated news article, and automatic neutralisation. Users can inspect perspective clusters, compare stance-specific summaries, generate news articles, and apply bias-aware rewrites directly in the interface. We evaluate each component with intrinsic metrics -- semantic diversity, summary quality, and bias reduction and show improvements over strong baselines while maintaining content fidelity. A live, publicly accessible demo accompanies the paper to facilitate reproducibility and further research on socially responsible automated journalism.

### [30] [Disentangling Curriculum Learning in NLP: Towards a Unifying Taxonomy](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18984)
*Vanessa Toborek,Florian Seiffarth,Sebastian Müller,Tamás Horváth*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a taxonomy for curriculum learning (CL) in NLP that cleanly separates how difficulty is defined from how training is scheduled, exposing why prior CL results are hard to compare and offering a framework to design and evaluate CL methods more systematically.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although curriculum learning has been studied in NLP for over a decade, researchers lack clear guidance on which difficulty metrics and scheduling strategies work best, because existing work mixes different definitions and implementations under the same CL label. This ambiguity makes empirical results incomparable and obstructs cumulative progress. The paper is motivated by the need for a principled, unified framework that clarifies the components of CL and their roles, so that evidence can be shared, compared, and built upon.

Method: The authors construct a fine-grained conceptual taxonomy that decomposes curriculum learning into two orthogonal components: difficulty evaluation and training scheduling. For difficulty evaluation, they categorize methods by the source of difficulty attribution (e.g., model-based, data-based, or task-based signals) and by whether difficulty is task-dependent or task-agnostic, emphasizing that difficulty is inherently perspectival. For scheduling, they propose a formalization based on the notion of expected training contribution of examples across time. Using this, they define concepts such as retention regimes (how long examples remain in the curriculum) and monotonicity properties (whether difficulty exposure is non-decreasing, cyclical, etc.). They then apply this taxonomy to a broad set of existing CL works in NLP, systematically classifying and comparing them to uncover patterns and mismatches.

Result: Using the taxonomy, the authors show that many prior CL papers in NLP are systematically incomparable: they rely on different and often incompatible notions of difficulty and scheduling while all claiming to use 'curriculum learning'. This conflation masks the fact that these works may optimize for different objectives (e.g., stability, speed, generalization) and differ in what they mean by 'hard' examples and how they are presented over time. The formalization of schedulers via expected training contribution also exposes commonalities between superficially different implementations and clarifies how retention and monotonicity shape learning dynamics.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that progress in curriculum learning for NLP has been hampered by conceptual ambiguity and lack of standardization in defining difficulty and scheduling. Their taxonomy provides a principled language to separate and analyze these components, enabling fairer comparisons, clearer reporting, and more targeted design of CL methods. They argue that future work should explicitly specify and justify both difficulty evaluation and scheduler choices, and adopt evaluation protocols that disentangle which part of a CL strategy is responsible for observed gains, thereby supporting a more coherent and cumulative evidence base.

Abstract: Despite more than a decade of curriculum learning (CL) research in NLP, the field lacks a principled account of which difficulty function or scheduler to use for a given problem. To understand what has hindered progress towards this account, we propose a fine-grained taxonomy separating difficulty evaluation from training scheduling to enable systematic analysis of CL strategies. For difficulty evaluation, we distinguish attribution source and task dependence, revealing difficulty as a perspectival concept encoding different assumptions about what makes an instance hard to learn. For scheduling, we provide the first formalisation of CL schedulers in terms of expected training contribution, enabling comparison across implementations by introducing retention regimes and monotonicity properties. Applied in a dedicated analysis of CL works in NLP, our taxonomy reveals a systematic incomparability problem: prior works conflate distinct notions of difficulty and scheduling, often pursuing different objectives under the same CL label -- hindering comparison and the accumulation of a coherent evidence base. Beyond diagnosis, the taxonomy supports the design, analysis, and comparison of CL strategies, and motivates evaluation practices that disentangle the sources of observed improvement.

</details>


### [31] [MedDDC-Eval: Diagnosis-Decoupled Evaluation of Multi-Turn Medical Consultation Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18999)
*Guofeng Zhang,Yizeng Quan,Huaiyi Fang,Jianwei Lv,Jinyao Liu,Xunxu Duan,Lening An,Yu Ouyang,Junfeng Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose MedDDC-Eval, a benchmark that evaluates medical dialogue agents by the quality of the patient history they elicit, decoupled from the final diagnosis generation, and show it enables clearer measurement and training of evidence-gathering policies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of multi-turn medical consultation agents entangle two factors: how well the agent elicits relevant medical history (its questioning policy) and how well it maps that history to a diagnosis (its generation capability). Strong generators can mask poor questioning by still getting diagnoses right, and weak generators can make good questioning look worse than it is. This makes it hard to fairly compare or improve evidence-acquisition policies. The authors want a setup where the elicited history itself is the central object of comparison, and where diagnosis is controlled so that policy quality is measured cleanly and can guide learning.

Method: They build MedDDC-Eval, a diagnosis-decoupled evaluation framework. First, all policies’ elicited histories are fed into a single frozen diagnostic reader so the history-to-diagnosis mapping is held constant. They design a grounded interface and a diagnosis-trajectory-efficiency (D/T/E) evaluation harness to separately quantify diagnostic usefulness, information acquisition, and efficiency over multi-turn trajectories. For open-ended items, they use directional semantic coverage with deterministic one-to-one matching so each prediction and reference contributes at most one true positive, yielding coherent precision–recall statistics. They also run interactive multi-turn rollouts and apply Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) to post-train a Qwen3-32B-based consultation policy, using both diagnosis outcomes and trajectory-level feedback as learning signals, and perform ablations removing each signal in turn.

Result: When keeping the elicited histories fixed but swapping the diagnostic reader, diagnosis F1 varies by 2.2–19.0 points and 18–36% of policy pairwise rankings are reversed across Record and Dialogue splits, demonstrating that conventional, coupled evaluation is highly sensitive to the chosen generator. Under MedDDC-Eval, they post-train Qwen3-32B on 100 Record and 70 Dialogue cases; the learned policy improves its total evaluation score over initialization by 9.7 and 4.6 points respectively. Ablation studies show that removing either diagnosis-result feedback or trajectory feedback reduces held-out joint performance, indicating that both types of signals contribute meaningfully to policy improvement.

Conclusion: MedDDC-Eval cleanly separates the evaluation of evidence-acquisition policies from diagnosis generation by standardizing the reader, enabling controlled attribution of performance differences to the elicitation policy itself. Its grounded interface, D/T/E metrics, and semantic matching scheme provide interpretable, auditable measures of the diagnostic value and efficiency of elicited histories. The experiments show that diagnosis readers substantially affect apparent policy quality in traditional setups, and that using MedDDC-Eval with GRPO can effectively guide the development of better questioning strategies in multi-turn medical consultation agents.

Abstract: Multi-turn medical consultation agents must decide what to ask, adapt to patient responses, and determine when the collected evidence is sufficient. However, coupled evaluation conflates the quality of the policy-elicited history with policy-specific terminal diagnosis generation: strong generation can compensate for a thin history, while weaker generation can obscure a rich one. We introduce MedDDC-Eval, a diagnosis-decoupled testbed that treats elicited history as the comparison object and holds the history-to-diagnosis mapping constant through a shared frozen reader. Across two held-out sources, a grounded interface and an auditable diagnosis-trajectory-efficiency (D/T/E) harness measure diagnostic usefulness, information acquisition, and efficiency. Directional semantic coverage followed by deterministic one-to-one assignment yields coherent precision-recall counts for open-ended items, with at most one credited match per prediction or reference. Holding histories fixed, changing only the diagnostic reader shifts diagnosis F1 by 2.2-19.0 points and reverses 18% and 36% of pairwise policy orderings on the Record and Dialogue splits. We further apply standard Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) over interactive multi-turn rollouts to post-train Qwen3-32B using diagnosis-result and trajectory feedback. On the 100-case Record and 70-case Dialogue splits, the trained policy improves over its initialization by 9.7 and 4.6 total-score points; removing either primary signal lowers held-out joint performance. These results show that MedDDC-Eval supports controlled attribution, interpretable elicited-history measurement, and evaluation-guided evidence-acquisition policy development.

### [33] [Content is What Remains: Invariant Speech Tokenization from Parallel Utterances](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19033)
*Laurin Wagner,Bernhard Thallinger,Miroslav Stankovic,Mario Zusag*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce PINT, a method to produce discrete speech tokens that strip away speaker and channel variation while preserving linguistic content and frame timing, leading to lower entropy and better performance on several benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing discrete speech tokenizers built from SSL models (e.g., HuBERT) still encode non-linguistic information such as speaker identity, prosody, and channel conditions. This leakage raises token entropy and makes learning with these tokens less efficient for downstream models like language models or codecs. The authors want a representation that is invariant to nuisance factors but still temporally grounded, unlike pure ASR text.

Method: They propose PINT (Parallel INvariant Tokenization), which fine-tunes an SSL speech encoder using alignment losses across parallel utterances and data augmentations. The core idea is to have many different speakers saying the same words under varied conditions; by enforcing alignment of their internal representations, the model is driven to retain only what is shared—linguistic content—while discarding speaker, prosody, and channel characteristics. These refined representations are then quantized into discrete tokens that can replace standard semantic targets in audio codec pipelines while staying frame-synchronous.

Result: PINT tokens significantly reduce non-linguistic information: speaker probe accuracy drops from 93.1% to 1.2% (a 98.7% relative reduction), indicating strong speaker invariance. They also improve linguistic discriminability and modeling efficiency: ABX error rate is 42% lower and language model perplexity on token sequences is 27–30% lower than baselines using standard SSL-derived tokens.

Conclusion: The study shows that enforcing the right invariances—here, collapsing parallel pronunciations of the same words—produces discrete speech tokens that are much more linguistically pure and lower entropy while preserving temporal alignment. Such tokens are better suited as semantic targets for audio codecs and for downstream modeling, as evidenced by improved probe, ABX, and LM perplexity metrics.

Abstract: Discrete speech tokenizers aim to disentangle semantic from acoustic information, yet targets from self-supervised learning (SSL) models like HuBERT retain non-linguistic variation: speaker identity, prosody, and channel conditions leak into the tokens, inflating entropy. Our key insight is that when enough speakers utter the same words under varying conditions, linguistic content is the only shared factor. We propose PINT (Parallel INvariant Tokenization), which fine-tunes an SSL encoder with alignment losses across parallel utterances and augmentations to distill this shared residual. PINT collapses identical words onto consistent token sequences, drastically reducing conditional entropy. Unlike ASR text, PINT tokens preserve frame-level temporal grounding and serve as drop-in semantic targets for audio codecs. Experiments show a 98.7% relative reduction in speaker probe accuracy (93.1% to 1.2%), a 42% lower ABX error rate, and 27-30% lower LM perplexity versus baselines, confirming that the right invariance is key to efficient learning.

</details>

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors want to understand how close current ASR comes to human performance on such diverse speech and to identify conditions where ASR is weaker or stronger than humans, in order to guide future improvements and clarify how evaluation test sets influence conclusions about ASR versus human performance.

Overall, the ASR systems performed similarly to native Dutch human listeners and, in certain conditions, even outperformed them. The detailed analysis revealed that the small performance differences between humans and ASR were systematically related to speaker age, regional accents, and utterance length. The comparison between performance on the specific experimental test stimuli and on the full Jasmin-CGN test sets showed that the choice of test material can significantly influence the apparent relationship between human and ASR performance.

Conclusion: The study concludes that current state-of-the-art ASR can reach, and in some cases exceed, the performance of human native listeners on challenging “diverse” Dutch speech, challenging the assumption that humans always form a strict upper bound for ASR performance. However, both ASR and human performance are sensitive to speaker-related factors such as age, regional accent, and utterance length. The authors emphasize the need for future research to improve ASR robustness to acoustic variability tied to aging and regional accents and warn that benchmarking results depend strongly on the specific test sets used, so careful test set design and reporting are crucial when comparing human and ASR performance.

Abstract: Humans are often considered to be the best listeners and seen as the upper-bound performance of automatic speech recognition (ASR) systems. We present a preliminary comparison of the performances of state-of-the-art ASR systems and Dutch native listeners on the recognition of "diverse" speech, specifically Dutch child and older adults' speech and Flemish. Google Telephony outperformed the other ASR systems. Importantly, the ASR systems showed similar performance to the listeners, and in specific cases even outperformed them. Slight performance differences between the listeners and ASR systems were found related to speaker's age and regional accents and utterance length. Future research should focus on making ASR systems more robust to acoustic variability related to aging and regional accents. A comparison of ASR recognition performances on the test stimuli and the full Jasmin-CGN test sets showed the influence of the specific test sets on the conclusions regarding benchmarking human and ASR performance.

### [35] [DAIS: Dependency-Aware Intermediate QA Supervision for Complex Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19088)
*Yu Wang,Ming Fan,Xicheng Zhang,Zhiyong Li,Zhihu Wang,Caiyue Xu,Dahai Hu,Ting Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes DAIS, a dependency-aware intermediate QA supervision framework that turns teacher chain-of-thought into stage-level QA records to better align local reasoning steps with final answers, improving downstream accuracy over several baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing chain-of-thought (CoT) supervision typically optimizes a single flat reasoning sequence and offers weak guidance on how intermediate conclusions should be structured and connected to later decisions. This limits the effectiveness of intermediate supervision and may fail to capture the true dependency structure of multi-step reasoning tasks.

Method: DAIS converts filtered teacher rationales into a set of dependency-aware stage-level QA records. For each reasoning stage, it defines an intermediate QA pair: a local question whose answer depends on specific prior states needed for that step. The intermediate records predict local answers conditioned only on the relevant previous states, while the final-answer record preserves the original task format. Training uses these structured intermediate QA targets; evaluation, however, relies only on the original input and optional context, without requiring intermediate supervision or extra inputs at test time.

Result: Across multiple datasets (GDPR, AIACT, MedQA, and FOLIO) and with various Qwen backbones, DAIS improves final-answer accuracy compared with answer-only training, flat CoT supervision, and independent intermediate QA baselines. On policy-compliance benchmarks, DAIS yields up to a 5.6% absolute accuracy gain and an average 4.2% gain over the strongest non-DAIS baseline, demonstrating more effective use of intermediate supervision.

Conclusion: Structuring intermediate supervision as dependency-conditioned QA records—rather than as a single flat chain-of-thought—provides a more informative and efficient training signal. Valid previous-state conditioning, not just longer or more intermediate text, is key to the performance gains. DAIS thus offers a lightweight, training-time-only auxiliary supervision method that improves standard final-answer inference without changing the evaluation format.

Abstract: Chain-of-thought (CoT) supervision exposes intermediate rationales, but flat rationale targets usually optimize a single reasoning sequence and provide limited supervision on how local conclusions should support later decisions. We introduce Dependency-Aware Intermediate QA Supervision (DAIS), a training-time framework that converts filtered teacher rationales into stage-level QA records. Each intermediate record predicts a local answer conditioned on the previous states needed for that decision, while the final-answer record keeps the original task format; evaluation therefore uses only the original input and optional context. Across GDPR, AIACT, MedQA, and FOLIO with multiple Qwen backbones, DAIS improves average final-answer accuracy over answer-only, flat chain-of-thought, and independent-QA baselines. On policy-compliance benchmarks, it achieves a largest gain of 5.6% and an average gain of 4.2% over the strongest non-DAIS baseline. Controlled ablations show that valid previous-state conditioning contributes beyond longer targets or additional intermediate text, supporting dependency-conditioned intermediate QA as a lightweight auxiliary supervision signal for standard final-answer inference.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing NMT and domain-adaptation methods struggle to fully meet legal accuracy standards. With the advent of reasoning-capable LLMs, there is an opportunity to leverage their reasoning abilities and improved training paradigms (SFT, RL, etc.) to boost translation quality, especially for smaller models that are more efficient and practical to deploy. The authors aim to understand which enhancement strategies are most effective and how far small models can be pushed toward frontier performance in a demanding real-world setting (Swiss law).

Small base models, once enhanced, achieve translation performance close to that of frontier reasoning models, though a gap remains. The study also finds that as model size increases, the marginal benefit from additional re-training paradigms decreases, indicating diminishing returns. The authors release their code and models for reproducibility and further research.

Conclusion: Re-training paradigms, especially RL with verifiable rewards, can substantially upgrade small language models for legal-domain NMT, narrowing the performance gap to frontier reasoning models while maintaining efficiency advantages. However, enhanced small models still lag behind top-tier reasoning models, and the gains from sophisticated re-training shrink as base model size grows. This suggests a trade-off between model scale and the value of complex adaptation methods, and positions RL-based approaches as a promising direction for high-precision domain-specific NMT such as Swiss legal translation.

Abstract: Neural machine translation (NMT) in the legal domain is a linguistically and conceptually demanding task, primarily due to the complexity of legal language and the high level of precision it requires. The recent emergence of reasoning-capable language models opens new possibilities for tackling such challenges. They add to a set of other previously proposed techniques to enhance the translation quality, which includes supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning.
  In this work, we perform a comparison between these various approaches. More particularly, we evaluate small language models such as Qwen3.5 4B, Qwen3.5 9B, and Gemma 3 12B enhanced with various re-training paradigms and compare their performances against frontier reasoning models. We focus on the Swiss legal system, which -- with its unique multilingual statutes -- offers a particularly challenging testbed for reasoning-augmented models. Our results show that the quality of small ``base'' models can be greatly enhanced, and that reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards can be applied to NMT in the legal domain and surpasses the translation quality of supervised fine-tuning. The performance of enhanced small models is close to the one of state-of-the-art reasoning models yet remains inferior. We also note that re-training paradigms yield diminishing returns as model size increase. The code and models are publicly available at https://github.com/aixiuxiuxiu/Legal-MT-SFT-RL.

### [40] [Beyond Score Prediction: LLM-Based Essay Scoring and Feedback Generation via Reinforcement Learning with Rubric Rewards](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19219)
*Xuefeng Jin,Jiashuo Zhang,Teng Cao,Bin Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces RLAES, a reinforcement-learning-based framework that jointly improves essay scoring and feedback generation for large language models using a detailed rubric and an LLM judge.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work in automated essay scoring and feedback generation mainly uses prompt engineering or supervised fine-tuning. There is little systematic study of reinforcement learning post-training for these tasks and no robust, automated way to evaluate feedback quality, especially in a fine-grained, interpretable, and training-usable manner. The authors aim to fill this gap by creating an RL-based approach that can jointly optimize both scoring accuracy and feedback quality with a reliable automatic evaluation mechanism.

Method: The authors propose RLAES, a unified LLM framework trained with reinforcement learning to perform both essay scoring and feedback generation. They design Rubric-based Feedback Evaluation (RFE), which consists of 166 fine-grained binary rubric items and an LLM-as-judge to automatically assess feedback along multiple dimensions, including consistency with the essay. On top of RFE, they introduce Adaptive Gated Feedback Optimization (AGFO), which selectively turns on rubric-based feedback rewards during RL to control evaluation cost while still improving feedback quality. They also develop Adjacent Contrastive Reasoning (ACR), which explicitly contrasts adjacent score levels during training to better calibrate ordinal scores. The system is evaluated on the ASAP benchmark and through preference studies aligned with expert judgments.

Result: RFE is shown to capture consistency between essays and feedback, has strong ability to discriminate between pairs of feedback, and aligns closely with expert preferences. On the ASAP benchmark, the RLAES model with AGFO achieves a quadratic weighted kappa of 0.803, outperforming other LLM-based methods in scoring, while maintaining feedback quality that is comparable to GPT-5.5. It avoids the degradation in feedback quality typically seen when RL is applied only to scoring without considering feedback.

Conclusion: Joint RL-based optimization of essay scoring and feedback generation is effective when supported by a detailed, rubric-based automatic evaluation framework. The proposed RFE and AGFO components enable scalable, interpretable feedback assessment and training, while ACR improves score calibration. Overall, RLAES-AGFO advances the state of the art in LLM-based automated essay scoring on ASAP and delivers high-quality feedback without sacrificing evaluation efficiency.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have been widely applied to automated essay scoring (AES) and automated feedback generation (AFG). However, existing studies rely primarily on prompt engineering or supervised fine-tuning, while systematic research on reinforcement learning (RL) post-training and automated evaluation of feedback quality remains limited. We propose RLAES, a unified LLM framework that jointly optimizes essay scoring and feedback generation through RL. To make feedback quality measurable, interpretable, and usable for training, we introduce Rubric-based Feedback Evaluation (RFE), an essay-grounded feedback evaluation framework comprising 166 fine-grained binary rubric items and an LLM-as-judge. Building on RFE, we propose Adaptive Gated Feedback Optimization (AGFO), which activates rubric-based feedback rewards on demand during RL, reducing evaluation overhead while improving feedback quality. We also propose Adjacent Contrastive Reasoning (ACR) to improve ordinal score calibration by explicitly contrasting adjacent score levels. Experimental results show that the RFE framework captures essay-feedback consistency, exhibits strong pairwise discriminative power, and closely aligns with expert preferences. On the ASAP benchmark, RLAES-AGFO achieves the best scoring performance among LLM-based methods (QWK = 0.803), while maintaining feedback quality comparable to GPT-5.5 and avoiding the feedback degradation observed under score-only RL. ### [41] [The Price of Reasoning: Cost-Quality Tradeoffs in Reinforcement Learning for Neural Machine Translation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19226)
*Michael Jungo,Aixiu An*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper examines how including a model’s explicit reasoning steps (reasoning trace) during training and/or inference affects translation quality in RL-with-verifiable-rewards for legal document translation, and analyzes the quality vs. computational cost tradeoff.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RL with verifiable rewards has proven effective for post-training LLMs on tasks like NMT, and recent work suggests it may be especially good for legal translation because it enhances reasoning. The authors want to disentangle whether the gains come specifically from explicit reasoning traces or simply from the RLVR paradigm, and to understand if requiring reasoning at inference is worth the extra token and compute cost.

Method: They use an RLVR setup for neural machine translation of legal documents and systematically vary whether the model outputs its reasoning trace during training, inference, both, or neither. By ablation—omitting the reasoning trace in one phase at a time—they measure the impact on translation quality. They also quantify how reasoning traces increase token count and compute, then relate this to the observed quality improvements.

Result: The experiments show that including explicit reasoning, particularly at inference time, improves overall translation quality compared to not using reasoning traces. They also document that reasoning traces substantially increase output length and thus computational cost.

Conclusion: Reasoning traces during inference are beneficial for translation quality in RLVR-based legal NMT, indicating that explicit reasoning—not just the RLVR paradigm alone—contributes to performance gains. However, this comes at a cost in token usage and compute, so practitioners must weigh the improved quality against higher resource requirements.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) has been established as a viable paradigm for the post-training of Large Language Models (LLMs), including downstream tasks, such as Neural Machine Translation (NMT). With the latest research indicating that RLVR could be the preferred training method for translating legal documents due to the induced reasoning capabilities, it raises the question whether it is really attributed to the reasoning or more generally to the training paradigm. We investigate the importance of including the model's reasoning trace in the generated responses during both training and inference by systematically omitting it from one of the phases. Our experiments show that including the reasoning, specifically during inference, has a positive effect on the overall translation quality. Furthermore, we recognise that the reasoning leads to an increase in output tokens, hence we study the cost-quality tradeoff between the increased computational demands and the improved translation quality.

### [42] [Selection Shapes the Boundary: A Preregistered Replication of Monotonicity and Label Agreement in Unselected NLI Populations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19231)
*Haram Choi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether earlier findings about lower label agreement for certain logical operators in NLI generalize, and finds they do not.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Previous work on human label variation in natural language inference used datasets that were pre-selected for disagreement, finding that hypotheses with non-upward monotonicity operators had lower label agreement. It was unclear whether this effect was a true population-level phenomenon or an artifact of selecting only low-agreement items. The authors aim to test whether that previously reported effect replicates in the original, unfiltered NLI data.

Method: They preregister a replication study on the SNLI and MultiNLI development sets, from which the ChaosNLI dataset was derived. Using the same automatic operator tagger as the prior study, they categorize hypotheses by whether they contain non-upward monotonicity operators. They then measure annotation agreement on a four-level ordinal scale and compute Cliff's delta for several planned contrasts, with predefined smallest effect size of interest (0.10). They also perform robustness checks including simulated tagger misclassification and a manual re-tagging audit on a new 200-item sample to validate the tagging and measurement approach.

Result: Contrary to the preregistered prediction, all seven planned contrasts show a positive Cliff's delta, meaning that items with non-upward monotonicity operators actually have slightly higher agreement, not lower. Only one confirmatory contrast is statistically significant, and it has the opposite sign from the preregistered hypothesis. All observed effects are smaller than the smallest effect size of interest. Robustness checks show that plausible tagger misclassification would shrink effects rather than produce them, and manual re-tagging achieves high agreement (0.875) across four classes, supporting the reliability of the operator labels.

Conclusion: The previously reported negative association between non-upward monotonicity operators and label agreement appears to be conditional on the low-agreement selection used in ChaosNLI, rather than a stable property of the broader NLI data. Therefore, structural claims about human label variation that are based on re-annotation datasets selected for disagreement should explicitly acknowledge their selection conditions, as such effects may not generalize to the full population of NLI examples.

Abstract: Prior work on human label variation (HLV) in natural language inference (NLI) has often relied on re-annotation resources that select items by disagreement level. An earlier study (arXiv:2607.15870) found that hypotheses containing non-upward monotonicity operators showed lower label agreement in ChaosNLI (Cliff's delta = -0.284), which is restricted to items whose majority label carries exactly three of five votes. We preregistered a replication of this boundary in the unselected populations that ChaosNLI was drawn from: the SNLI and MultiNLI development sets, using the same operator tagger and a four-level ordinal agreement outcome. The registered prediction fails. All seven contrasts return a positive Cliff's delta (non-upward items agree slightly more, not less), the only significant confirmatory contrast has the opposite sign to the registration, and every effect is far below our smallest effect size of interest (0.10). Robustness checks support the measurement: simulated tagger misclassification shrinks the effects rather than manufacturing them, and a manual re-tagging audit reaches four-class agreement of 0.875 on a fresh 200-item sample. We conclude that the earlier negative boundary is plausibly a structure conditional on low-agreement selection rather than a population-level property, and that HLV structure claims built on selected re-annotation resources should state their selection conditional explicitly.

Abstract: Theory of Mind (ToM), the ability to infer other's beliefs, intentions, and states of knowledge, is central to social interaction, yet remains challenging for current Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs), especially in multi-party meetings where cues are distributed across speech and behavior. Existing multimodal ToM benchmarks mainly focus on video-grounded question answering over overt, externally verifiable signals, and provide limited coverage of latent social states and group dynamics. We introduce MeetingToM, a benchmark for complex social behavior reasoning in naturalistic multi-party meetings. MeetingToM targets meeting-specific phenomena such as \textbf{pseudo-consensus}, where apparent agreement masks private dissent under social pressure. The benchmark is hierarchically organized to evaluate ToM at increasing levels of social granularity, including (i) subject-level mental state prediction, (ii) dyadic-level addressee understanding, and (iii) group-level consensus reasoning. We provide a unified evaluation protocol and conduct systematic analyses of representative MLLMs, revealing persistent limitations in integrating non-verbal cues, inferring hidden attitudes, and distinguishing genuine consensus from pseudo-consensus. Our results highlight key challenges and establish MeetingToM as a testbed for advancing meeting-grounded ToM in multimodal models.

### [44] [Inference-Time Steering for Cross-Lingual Factual Consistency in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19243)
*Alexander Manev*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how to reduce cross-lingual factual inconsistency in LLMs—where answers change just because the prompt language changes—by comparing four inference-time and lightweight training interventions, finding that simple persona-style prompting works best overall.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are multilingual but their internal knowledge is biased toward high-resource languages like English. As a result, the same factual question can yield different answers depending on the query language, which is problematic for fairness, reliability, and culturally appropriate behavior. The authors want to know if this inconsistency can be fixed at inference time (without retraining the whole model) and whether such fixes generalize from factual benchmarks to broader, culturally grounded preferences in multiple target languages.

Method: The authors evaluate four strategies on Gemma 3 12B Instruct to make an English-prompted model behave as if queried in target languages (German, Spanish, Bulgarian). (1) Zero-shot contextual steering via persona prompting: instructing the model to answer as a native or target-language–centric assistant. (2) Contrastive Activation Addition (CAA): manipulating internal activations to push the model toward representations associated with the target language. (3) Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) adapters trained on factual data derived from benchmarks. (4) DPO adapters trained on more conceptual, culturally grounded data to test generalization. They curate a multilingual factual dataset and a new generalization benchmark with culturally rooted questions, then measure shifts in answer distributions, safety, and transfer to out-of-domain tasks.

Result: Persona prompting produces the best overall balance: it significantly reduces cross-lingual factual inconsistency, maintains safety, and generalizes well to out-of-domain, culturally rooted queries. CAA can strongly shift behavior on inconsistency benchmarks but is fragile—performance and knowledge quality depend heavily on configuration and can degrade. DPO-based adapters create more permanent behavior changes but tend to be narrower in scope and less transferable to new domains or broader cultural preferences.

Conclusion: Cross-lingual inconsistency in LLMs appears to be largely a selection issue—how knowledge is accessed—rather than purely a lack of non-English knowledge. Lightweight, contextual methods such as persona prompting can rival or surpass more invasive interventions like activation editing and DPO finetuning, especially when robustness, safety, and transfer to new domains matter. This suggests that, for practical multilingual alignment, carefully designed prompts may often be preferable to heavier-weight architectural or training modifications.

Abstract: Although Large Language Models (LLMs) demonstrate remarkable multilingual fluency, their internal knowledge representations remain disproportionately biased toward high-resource languages. This leads to cross-lingual factual inconsistency, where they shift their empirical answer distributions based solely on the prompt language. We investigate whether these biases can be mitigated at inference time, forcing an English-prompted model to answer as if it were queried in target languages (German, Spanish, Bulgarian), and evaluate four intervention strategies: zero-shot contextual steering (persona prompting), internal representation manipulation via Contrastive Activation Addition (CAA), and lightweight weight modification via Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) trained on benchmark-derived factual data as well as conceptual generalization data. To assess alignment, we curate a multilingual factual dataset alongside a novel generalization benchmark comprising culturally rooted queries to determine whether factual interventions transfer to broader target-centric preferences. Experiments on Gemma 3 12B Instruct reveal persona prompting to be the strongest overall intervention, balancing efficacy, safety, and out-of-domain generalization. While CAA yields sharp inconsistency benchmark shifts, it is configuration-sensitive and risks knowledge degradation. DPO-based adapters offer permanent, yet narrower and less transferable gains. These findings suggest that cross-lingual inconsistency is at least partly a selection problem, and that simple contextual interventions may outperform more invasive methods for robust, transferable alignment.

### [45] [Prompt Design at Scale: How Format, Instruction Count, and Context Length Shape Instruction Adherence and Hallucination in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19257)
*Netanel Eliav*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They empirically test how instruction formatting, number of rules, and context length affect LLM instruction-following, recall, and honesty, using a synthetic, contamination-free corpus and standardized benchmark across several models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prompt engineers and practitioners make key choices about format (markdown vs plain text vs tables vs prose), number of instructions in a prompt, and context length, but there is little controlled, model-comparative evidence about how these factors actually affect performance, instruction-following, and failure modes like hallucination or sycophancy. The authors want to replace folk wisdom with systematic measurements.

Method: They create a synthetic, contamination-free knowledge base called the “Book of Veyra” containing 8,780 uniquely named entities that can be deterministically regenerated from a seed, and build a benchmark (VeyraBench). They run two controlled experiments across four formatting styles and five models. Experiment 1 runs 960 calls per model, varying rule-count N from 10 to 160, crossing format types and whether instructions live in the system prompt or user turn, and measures instruction-following (perfect response rate). Experiment 2 runs 5,520 calls per model, varying context length from 2k to 512k tokens and format, and measures recall accuracy, false-premise sycophancy, fabrication of absent facts, and outright refusal, analyzing how these evolve near each model’s context window ceiling. They also track token overhead of each format.

Result: In Experiment 1, as the number of rules increases, perfect-response rates drop for all models and settings, collapsing to zero by 80 rules regardless of format or prompt placement. Prompt placement (system vs user) produces effects comparable to or larger than formatting at high rule counts, but the direction (which is better) depends on the model. No consistent performance advantage is found for markdown; one 35B model even prefers plain text. In Experiment 2, recall remains high up to around 64–128k tokens but then drops steeply in a way that depends on the formatting choice; one model shows up to a 48-point accuracy spread between formats at 128k. The models never fabricate answers in the probes and show only low levels of sycophancy, but near their context limits they begin refusing to answer instead, with refusal rates climbing as high as ~79–90%. Pre-registered hypotheses about which format would work best are not supported, and because markdown-like formats introduce 22–37% extra tokens compared to plain text, they sometimes push prompts closer to context ceilings and change which format is optimal.

Conclusion: LLM prompt performance is strongly constrained by the number of simultaneous instructions and proximity to the model’s context limit, and folk beliefs about markdown as a generally superior format or about system vs user placement do not hold reliably across models. Models can maintain high recall and low hallucination and sycophancy over very long contexts but eventually fail by refusing to answer rather than fabricating, and formatting both affects accuracy and indirectly harms performance via added token overhead. Practitioners should treat formatting, instruction load, and context length as model-specific, empirical tuning parameters rather than rely on simple best-practice rules, and VeyraBench provides an open, reproducible way to study these effects across future models.

Abstract: Practitioners make three prompt-design decisions with almost no controlled evidence behind them: how to format instructions and context (markdown, plain text, prose, or tabular), how many simultaneous instructions a system prompt can carry before compliance degrades, and how much context a model can hold before recall and honesty degrade. We report two controlled experiments crossing all three factors on one held, contamination-free synthetic corpus (the "Book of Veyra," 8,780 uniquely-named entities, deterministically regenerable from a fixed seed), evaluated across five models.
  Experiment 1 (960 calls/model) measures instruction-following decay as rule count N grows from 10 to 160, crossed with four formats and system-prompt vs. user-turn placement. Perfect-response rate collapses to zero by N=80 for every model, format, and placement. Placement produces effects at least as large as format at N=160 in most models, but the direction is model-specific. No model shows a reliable markdown advantage; one 35B model favors plain text instead.
  Experiment 2 (5,520 calls/model) measures recall accuracy, false-premise sycophancy, and absent-fact fabrication across a 2k-to-512k-token context ladder in the same four formats. Recall stays near ceiling through 64-128k tokens, then degrades sharply and format-dependently: one model's accuracy spread reaches 48 points at 128k tokens. Fabrication never occurs (0/5,760 probes), and sycophancy stays negligible (<=8.3%). What rises sharply near each model's context ceiling is outright refusal to answer (0% to 79-90%), distinct from sycophancy or fabrication. Neither pre-registered format ordering holds, and token overhead (+22% to +37% over plain text) further changes which format is preferable where accuracy spread is genuine.
  We release the full harness, corpus generator, and raw results (VeyraBench): https://github.com/iNetanel/veyrabench

### [46] [Two-Level Meta-Rubrics for Evaluating Open-Ended Generation: GAMUT, a Benchmark for Factual Completeness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19322)
*Xilun Chen,Zhaleh Feizollahi,Ross Goodwin,Seungwhan Moon,Scott Yih,Pinar Donmez,Babak Damavandi,Luna Dong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Gamut, a benchmark and framework to evaluate factual completeness—not just correctness—of long-form, often multimodal, model outputs using a two-level rubric that is automatically converted into binary checks judged by LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing factuality evaluation for long-form generation mainly measures precision (are stated facts correct?) via decompose-search-verify pipelines, which miss whether models omit important information. Factual completeness is harder to assess because correct answers may involve open-ended, structured sets of facts, processes, and relationships that cannot be captured by flat lists of independent boolean checks. There is a need for a systematic, scalable way to define and automatically grade what a fully complete answer should contain, especially in multimodal settings like wearable imagery.

Method: The authors propose a two-level meta-rubric framework. First, experts construct a structured meta-rubric that encodes the organization, hierarchy, and relative importance of required content for a question, reflecting processes, coverage requirements, and relationships among facts. This meta-rubric is mechanically compiled into a flat checklist of binary rubrics suitable for automatic grading. An LLM judge then scores each item in this checklist, enabling reliable machine grading of factual completeness. Using this framework, the authors build Gamut: 1,813 questions grounded in real wearable imagery from 10 diverse domains, each with an evidence-backed rubric validated by expert annotators. They also create a text-only variant to show the modality-agnostic nature of the approach, and evaluate 14 frontier and open-weight models under this benchmark.

Result: Gamut proves to be a challenging benchmark for current models: the best-performing model, Gemini 3.1 Pro, achieves only 58.7% completeness. The benchmark discriminates well across 14 evaluated models and yields consistent rankings irrespective of the specific LLM judge used, indicating robustness of the rubric design and scoring pipeline. The evidence-backed rubrics and expert verification allow reliable measurement of whether model outputs cover the necessary information, not just avoid incorrect claims.

Conclusion: The paper demonstrates that factual completeness is a distinct and under-measured dimension of factuality in long-form generation, and that it can be systematically evaluated via a structured meta-rubric compiled into machine-gradable binary checks. Gamut establishes a challenging, discriminative, and modality-agnostic benchmark for this purpose, showing that even state-of-the-art models perform far from complete. This framework offers a scalable path toward more thorough evaluations of long-form, especially multimodal, model outputs and can guide the development of models that not only avoid errors but also provide comprehensive answers.

Abstract: Evaluating the factuality of long-form generations has focused predominantly on precision, measuring whether the claims a model makes are correct. The dominant decompose-search-verify pipeline catches incorrect claims well but says little about whether a response contains all the information it should. Measuring factual completeness, the missing half of factuality, is harder: it requires enumerating the full set of facts a complete answer should contain, and these facts rarely form a flat list. They often involve open-ended sets where coverage is what matters, ordered processes, and relationships among facts that a list of independent boolean checks fails to capture. We introduce a two-level meta-rubric framework for evaluating open-ended generation, and instantiate it as Gamut (Grounded Assessment of Multimodal Factuality), a benchmark for factual completeness in long-form generation. The framework rests on a two-level rubric representation: a structured meta-rubric captures the organization and importance of the required content, which is then mechanically compiled into a flat checklist of binary, machine-gradable rubrics that an LLM judge scores reliably. We construct 1,813 questions grounded in real wearable imagery across 10 diverse domains, each paired with an evidence-backed rubric verified by expert human annotators. Because the framework is modality-agnostic, we also release a text-only variant. Evaluating 14 frontier and open-weight models, we find the benchmark genuinely challenging (best score 58.7% from Gemini 3.1 Pro), highly discriminative, and robust to the choice of judge.
