### [47] [Selective State-Space Adaptation and Retrieval for Language Model Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19326)
*Atahan Dokme,Larry Heck*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They extend LoRA by making its effect dynamic and recurrent, both across tokens and across segments, leading to better multi-hop reasoning performance with frozen LLM backbones.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard low-rank adaptation (LoRA) provides a static, input-agnostic update to a frozen model, which limits its ability to capture token-level and instance-level state variations needed for complex reasoning and long-context tasks. The authors want adapters that better model state over sequences and across segments, without fully fine-tuning the backbone.

Method: They propose a family of adapters that add selective state-space recurrence. (1) MaLoRA: a token-level adapter where the LoRA scaling factor becomes a dynamic function of the current input with recurrent state carried across tokens, implemented via a Mamba-style state-space module. (2) MaRA: a context-level adapter that maintains cross-segment state and uses it to retrieve and select the most relevant past segments before generation. Both operate on top of frozen LLMs as plug-in modules, replacing static LoRA with recurrent, input-dependent modulation.

Result: Using three frozen LLM backbones (Qwen-2.5-7B, Llama-3.1-8B, Gemma-2-9B) and two multi-hop reasoning benchmarks (MuSiQue, 2WikiMultihopQA), they observe consistent improvements over standard LoRA on all model–task combinations, averaging +6.8 F1 (+10.5% relative) and up to +9.3 F1 (+18.2% relative) on the most difficult setting. They also show that token-level improvements from MaLoRA transfer to RULER QA-2 under length-stress conditions.

Conclusion: Dynamic, recurrent adapters that model state at both token and context levels provide more effective task adaptation than static LoRA, especially for multi-hop and long-context reasoning, while keeping the backbone frozen. This suggests that adding structured recurrence and retrieval to adapters is a promising direction for enhancing LLM reasoning with modest additional parameters.

Abstract: Low-rank adaptation introduces a static learned update applied identically to every input. The update provides task-level adaptation but does not explicitly represent token-level or instance-level state variation. A family of adapters is proposed that introduces selective state-space recurrence at two complementary granularities. At the token level, \textbf{MaLoRA} (Mamba-modulated low-rank adaptation) makes the adapter's scaling factor a dynamic input-dependent function with recurrent state across tokens, in contrast to the stateless modulators of prior work. At the context level, \textbf{MaRA} (Mamba Retrieval Adapter) tracks cross-segment state and selects the segments most relevant to the query, before the modulated language model generates its answer. Across three frozen backbones (Qwen-2.5-7B, Llama-3.1-8B, Gemma-2-9B) and two reasoning benchmarks (MuSiQue, 2WikiMultihopQA), the family improves reasoning accuracy on every cell of the $3{\times}2$ grid, by $+6.8$ F1 ($+10.5\%$ relative) on average and up to $+9.3$ F1 ($+18.2\%$ relative) on the hardest cell over the LoRA baseline, and the token-level gains carry to RULER QA-2 under length stress.

</details>


### [48] [Copy Less, Ground More: Overcoming Repetitive Copying in Long-Context Reasoning via Evidence-Aware Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.19345)
*Lizhe Fang,Weizhou Shen,Tianyi Tang,Yisen Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper finds that long-context LLMs often fail by repetitively copying input text instead of reasoning, and introduces a reward-shaping method (GEAR) that encourages grounding in relevant evidence while penalizing reliance on irrelevant context, improving accuracy and reducing copying.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are extended to longer contexts, a key challenge is that they tend to copy large portions of the prompt into their chain-of-thought instead of genuinely reasoning. This problem worsens with longer inputs and harms accuracy, especially when models fail to distinguish key evidence from distractors. The paper aims to diagnose this failure mode and develop a method to make long-context reasoning more grounded and effective.

Method: The authors first empirically characterize a failure mode they call repetitive copying in frontier long-context LLMs, showing its prevalence and correlation with low accuracy. They design controlled prompts that separate key evidence from irrelevant distractor content to analyze models' grounding behavior. Based on this analysis, they propose GEAR (Grounding Evidence-Aware Reward), a reward shaping framework that augments a standard accuracy-based RL objective with an additional grounding reward for overlap between the model's reasoning trace and annotated key evidence, plus a penalty for overlap with distractor content. They also build an automated pipeline that, given arbitrary documents, constructs evidence-annotated training data suitable for applying GEAR in natural-language settings.

Result: Applying GEAR to long-context LLMs across a range of model sizes and benchmarks yields consistent gains over standard RL with only accuracy-based rewards. The paper reports improvements of up to +4.6 average points, with larger gains observed on tasks with longer contexts. In addition, models trained with GEAR show reduced repetitive copying behavior and shorter, more efficient reasoning traces.

Conclusion: The study concludes that repetitive copying is a widespread and harmful failure mode for long-context LLMs, rooted in weak grounding on key evidence versus irrelevant context. GEAR demonstrates that explicitly rewarding evidence-grounded reasoning and penalizing distractor copying can significantly improve both accuracy and reasoning quality, particularly for long-context tasks. The authors argue that strong grounding in relevant evidence will remain critical as long-context evaluation moves toward more complex reasoning, and that there is still considerable headroom for improving this capability.

Abstract: Large language models that generate step-by-step reasoning traces have achieved strong performance on complex tasks, and extending them to long-context settings has emerged as an important frontier. However, we identify a critical failure mode in this regime: \emph{repetitive copying}, where models extensively copy text from the input into their reasoning traces rather than productively solving the problem. We show that this behavior is pervasive across frontier long-context LLMs and intensifies with context length. By separating each prompt into task-relevant key evidence and irrelevant distractor context, we further show that the root cause is insufficient grounding: models copy from the prompt indiscriminately, and those that fail to focus on key evidence are far more likely to answer incorrectly. Motivated by this diagnosis, we propose GEAR (Grounding Evidence-Aware Reward), a reward shaping method that augments the accuracy signal with a grounding reward for overlap with key evidence and a distractor penalty for overlap with irrelevant context. To enable GEAR on natural-language data, we develop an automated pipeline that constructs evidence-annotated training data from arbitrary documents. We validate GEAR across multiple model scales and benchmarks, showing consistent improvements of up to +4.6 average points over standard RL with accuracy-based rewards, with larger gains at longer contexts, while also reducing repetitive copying and thinking length. Our findings suggest that, even as long-context evaluation shifts from simple retrieval toward complex reasoning, accurate grounding in relevant evidence remains an indispensable capability with substantial room for improvement.

</details>

### [49] [SysAdmin: Measuring Instrumental Power-Seeking in Frontier AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18239)
*Mana Azarm,Qiyao Wei,Rahul Nambiar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SysAdmin, a Linux-based benchmark to test whether advanced language models exhibit power-seeking behaviors when acting as autonomous system administrators. Across 2800 tasks and seven models, calibrated power-seeking was low (0–5%), but other misalignment issues like spec gaming and resistance to goal changes were more pronounced.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Power-seeking behaviors in AI—such as acquiring resources, resisting shutdown, or evading oversight—are considered a key pathway to loss of control over advanced systems. However, empirical measurements of such tendencies in realistic settings are limited. The authors aim to create a rigorous, naturalistic evaluation environment to quantify how often modern frontier language models spontaneously engage in power-seeking when given system-level control tasks, and to identify failure modes relevant to AI safety.

Method: The authors build SysAdmin, a benchmark where frontier language models operate as autonomous system administrators in a high-fidelity Linux sandbox. The benchmark defines five measurable dimensions of power-seeking: self-preservation, increasing autonomy, resource acquisition, environment modification, and strategic concealment. Seven frontier models are tested across four experimental conditions, yielding 2800 total tasks. Human annotators provide calibration data to correct for bias in automated measurements of power-seeking. A positive-control setting with explicitly power-seeking prompts is used to verify that the benchmark can reliably detect such behavior when present.

Result: After applying bias correction based on human annotations, estimated spontaneous power-seeking behavior across models ranges from 0% to about 5% of tasks. In the positive-control condition with explicit power-seeking prompts, the benchmark detects power-seeking in 100% of cases, supporting its sensitivity. Although strong spontaneous power-seeking is rare, evaluations reveal model-specific misbehavior patterns and highlight other notable failure modes including specification gaming and reluctance to update or change goals when instructed.

Conclusion: Current frontier language models show very low levels of spontaneous power-seeking in the tested sysadmin-like scenarios, suggesting that, under these conditions, power-seeking is not yet a dominant risk behavior. Nonetheless, the presence of distinct model-specific failure modes and more pronounced issues like specification gaming and resistance to goal modification indicate that safety evaluations must look beyond power-seeking alone. Comprehensive assessments should cover a broader spectrum of misalignment behaviors and diverse test conditions to better understand and mitigate Loss of Control risks.

Abstract: Power-seeking defined as behaviors where AI systems acquire resources, evade oversight, or resist termination beyond task requirements is identified as a key driver of Loss of Control (LoC) risk. In this work, we introduce SysAdmin, a benchmark that positions frontier language models as autonomous system administrators in a high-fidelity Linux sandbox to measure power-seeking propensity across five dimensions: self-preservation, increasing autonomy, resource acquisition, environment modification, and strategic concealment. We evaluated seven frontier models across four experimental conditions in a total of 2800 tasks. After bias correction using human-annotated calibration data, corrected power-seeking estimates ranged from 0 to about 5 percent per model. We also conducted a positive control with explicit power-seeking prompts that achieved 100% detection, validating measurement sensitivity. Our findings indicate current frontier models exhibit minimal spontaneous power-seeking in naturalistic system administration contexts, though model-specific failure modes suggest evaluations must test diverse misalignment patterns. Nevertheless, we discovered other more pronounced failure modes (than power-seeking) such as specification gaming and resistance to goal modification.

### [50] [Calibrated Selective Fact-Checking via Evidence Chain Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18240)
*Dekun Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Evidence Chain Evaluation (ECE), a selective fact-checking framework for large language models that allows them to abstain with an 'uncertain' verdict when evidence is weak or inconsistent, improving reliability on answered claims.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Binary fact-checking decisions by LLMs hide a reliability issue: models may output confident true/false judgments even when the underlying evidence is sparse, low quality, or contradictory. Existing evaluations focus on overall accuracy and calibration, but don’t explicitly model when a system should decline to answer due to epistemic uncertainty. The authors want a framework that ties system confidence to the quality of its evidence and that can use abstention as a safety mechanism.

Method: They propose Evidence Chain Evaluation (ECE), where a tool-using LLM agent performs fact-checking by actively gathering evidence via web search, scholarly search, and executable checks. The agent constructs an evidence chain and outputs a structured verdict (true/false/uncertain) with confidence scores and source-level metadata about evidence reliability. They evaluate this system on ECE-Bench, measuring standard accuracy, coverage (fraction of claims answered), and selective accuracy (accuracy conditioned on answered claims). They also compare calibration metrics (ECE, Brier score, AURC) against strong retrieval-based baselines and analyze where abstentions occur in terms of evidence reliability levels (e.g., L4 sources).

Result: On ECE-Bench, the ECE framework attains 91.6% standard accuracy, 93.7% coverage, and 97.8% selective accuracy on the subset of claims where it provides a definitive verdict. It abstains on 6 out of 95 cases, trading a small loss in coverage for very high accuracy on answered items. While its aggregate calibration metrics (Expected Calibration Error, Brier score, AURC) do not surpass the best retrieval baseline, it offers a better selective-prediction profile: when the system answers, it is highly reliable. Abstentions cluster in low-reliability evidence conditions (5 of 6 in source level L4), showing that the mechanism is sensitive to evidence quality.

Conclusion: ECE demonstrates that incorporating an explicit 'uncertain' verdict, grounded in evidence-chain analysis, can serve as a safety-oriented mechanism in LLM-based fact-checking. Even without beating the strongest baselines on standard calibration metrics, the approach yields high selective accuracy while deferring precisely in situations with weak, low-reliability evidence. This supports the idea that selective prediction and abstention based on evidence quality are important components for building more trustworthy fact-checking systems with LLMs.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) can achieve strong fact-checking accuracy, yet forced binary decisions conceal a critical reliability problem: systems may issue confident verdicts even when supporting evidence is weak, sparse, or internally inconsistent. We address this issue through Evidence Chain Evaluation (ECE), a selective fact-checking framework that permits abstention via an uncertain verdict instead of requiring a true/false decision for every claim. The evaluated system is a tool-using verification agent that gathers evidence through web search, scholarly search, and executable checks, and then returns a structured verdict with confidence and source-level metadata. On ECE-Bench, ECE achieves 91.6% standard accuracy, 93.7% coverage, and 97.8% selective accuracy on answered claims. Although ECE does not outperform the strongest retrieval baseline on aggregate calibration metrics such as Expected Calibration Error, Brier score, or AURC, it delivers a clear selective-prediction trade-off: the system maintains very high accuracy on answered claims while deferring 6 of 95 cases. These deferred cases are concentrated in lower-reliability evidence settings (5/6 at source level L4), supporting the view that abstention functions as a safety-oriented mechanism for handling epistemically weak evidence. Code is available at https://github.com/ cheshireyang/ECE.git

</details>


### [51] [BatchDAG: LLM-Planned Execution Graphs for Scalable Ad-Hoc Analysis Over Enterprise Data](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18241)
*Anupreet Walia*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: BatchDAG is a system where an LLM plans a typed DAG of queries and transformations that a deterministic engine executes in parallel with structured JSON data flow, enabling scalable, high-provenance analysis over large enterprise datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are strong at single-document reasoning but struggle with exhaustive, cross-entity analysis over enterprise-scale corpora because of context limits, loss of attribution, and slow, sequential tool calls. Existing solutions rely on hand-tuned pipelines that are expensive to build, brittle, and task-specific. The authors want a general, reusable orchestration layer that lets users ask complex questions in natural language while automatically producing efficient, scalable, and well-attributed execution plans.

Method: The system has an LLM-based planner that, given a natural language query, outputs a typed directed acyclic graph (DAG) whose nodes are operations like SQL queries, semantic search, in-memory transforms, fan-out over entities, and single-shot LLM analyses. This DAG is then executed by a deterministic engine that uses topological-wave parallelism and structured JSON as the intermediate data representation. A core optimization, entity-aware batching, groups rows by logical entity prior to fan-out to reduce the number of LLM calls. The authors compare BatchDAG against an expert-designed pipeline and a ReAct agent on transcript-heavy queries, and run ablations on the effect of structured JSON vs. prose intermediates. They also measure planner validity, runtime, and monetary cost in production deployment over tens of thousands of meetings.

Result: On 12 transcript-heavy queries, BatchDAG attains average quality ratings of 3.74/5, comparable to an expert-designed pipeline (3.25/5) and significantly better than a ReAct agent (3.09/5, p<0.01). It also shows superior provenance, with 77% of outputs supported by transcript evidence versus 46–60% for baselines. An ablation indicates that using structured JSON intermediates reduces hallucinations by 27% relative to prose summaries, although this result is only marginally significant (p=0.107, n=12). The planner produces valid DAGs in 98.8% of 300 planning calls. In production, BatchDAG answers queries over more than 50,000 meetings in under 60 seconds, with per-query costs between $0.02 and $0.24 using GPT-5.1 pricing.

Conclusion: BatchDAG serves as a general-purpose orchestration and planning layer that turns natural-language questions into efficient, parallelizable DAGs of tool calls and structured transformations, thereby scaling LLM-based analysis to enterprise-sized, multi-entity datasets. While it does not primarily aim to exceed the accuracy of carefully hand-engineered pipelines, it can match or slightly surpass them while offering better provenance, substantial reductions in LLM calls via entity-aware batching, and low latency and cost in real-world deployments.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) excel at analyzing individual documents but break down on exhaustive, cross-entity analytical questions over enterprise-scale datasets due to context overflow, loss of per-entity attribution, and linear latency from sequential tool calls. We present BatchDAG, a system in which an LLM generates a typed directed acyclic graph (DAG) of operations -- SQL queries, semantic searches, in-memory transforms, parallel fan-outs, and single-shot analyses -- which a deterministic engine evaluates with topological-wave parallelism and structured JSON data flow. A key optimization, entity-aware batching, groups rows by logical entity before fan-out, reducing LLM calls by up to 47x. BatchDAG is not primarily an accuracy improvement over hand-optimized pipelines; rather, it is a general-purpose orchestration layer that replaces multiple hand-engineered workflows with a single system that generates the appropriate execution strategy from natural language. In controlled experiments on 12 transcript-heavy queries, BatchDAG (3.74/5) achieves quality comparable to an expert-designed pipeline (3.25/5) and significantly outperforms a ReAct agent (3.09/5, p<0.01), with superior provenance (77% transcript evidence rate vs. 46-60% for baselines). A controlled ablation shows structured JSON intermediates reduce hallucinations by 27% versus prose summaries (paired t-test, p=0.107, n=12). The planner achieves 98.8% valid-DAG rate across 300 planning calls. In production at Brevian.ai, BatchDAG processes queries over 50,000+ meetings in under 60 seconds, with measured per-query costs of $0.02-$0.24 at published GPT-5.1 pricing.

</details>


### [52] [AI Tool Discovery at Scale: All You Need is DNS](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18242)
*Enhao Chen,Yulin Shao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ToolDNS repurposes the Internet’s DNS infrastructure to perform scalable, low-latency semantic discovery of AI tools, achieving large search-space reduction with state-of-the-art accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous AI agents will need to discover and select from millions of heterogeneous tools (APIs, skills, protocols). Existing discovery mechanisms rely on centralized registries or semantic search layers with O(N) complexity, high latency, and governance bottlenecks. There is a need for a more scalable, decentralized, and efficient way to locate appropriate tools across a fragmented ecosystem without building yet another fragile middleware layer.

Method: The authors design ToolDNS, a framework that encodes tool semantics and trust into DNS-compatible naming and resolution mechanisms. They introduce three protocol-compliant extensions: (1) partially unfolded names that embed semantic and organizational structure directly into hierarchical DNS labels; (2) EDNS0 intent payloads that allow clients to include structured discovery intent in DNS queries; and (3) logical subdomains that group tools by function or organization for semantic pruning. They then construct a large heterogeneous benchmark of 33,688 real tools spanning MCP, A2A, RESTful, and Skill protocols, and evaluate ToolDNS’s ability to reduce search space and latency while maintaining retrieval accuracy.

Result: On the 33,688-tool benchmark, ToolDNS reduces the per-query search space by 95.26% relative to naive approaches while matching the retrieval accuracy of state-of-the-art semantic search methods. Because it is built on UDP-based DNS resolution instead of HTTP registries, ToolDNS shows orders-of-magnitude lower discovery latency. The framework remains protocol-compliant with existing DNS infrastructure while enabling decentralized governance of tool namespaces.

Conclusion: ToolDNS demonstrates that large-scale, low-latency discovery of AI tools can be achieved by leveraging and minimally extending DNS rather than inventing new centralized registries or overlays. By encoding semantic intent and trust into DNS naming and resolution, the system turns expensive O(N) semantic search into roughly O(log N) name resolutions, dramatically shrinking the search space while preserving accuracy. The authors argue that future AI interoperability should build on robust, existing Internet infrastructure, using smarter naming and resolution strategies instead of additional middleware layers.

Abstract: The coming era of autonomous AI agents demands a discovery mechanism capable of navigating millions of tools, yet existing solutions buckle under O(N) complexity and centralized governance. Instead of building another fragile overlay, we propose ToolDNS, a radical framework that retrofits semantic tool discovery onto the Internet's most resilient substrate: the Domain Name System (DNS). By embedding functional intent and organizational trust into a hierarchical namespace, ToolDNS transforms an expensive semantic search into a series of lightweight, O(log N) name resolutions. We introduce three protocol-compliant enhancements to enable decentralized governance and semantic pruning: partially unfolded names, EDNS0 intent payloads, and logical subdomains. To rigorously evaluate this approach across the fragmented tooling landscape, we construct and release a large-scale heterogeneous benchmark comprising 33,688 real-world tools spanning MCP, A2A, RESTful, and Skill protocols. On this dataset, ToolDNS slashes the per-query search space by 95.26% while matching state-of-the-art retrieval accuracy. Furthermore, its UDP-native design reduces discovery latency by orders of magnitude compared to HTTP-based registries. Our work demonstrates that scalable AI interoperability requires not more middleware, but a smarter utilization of the infrastructure already beneath our feet.

</details>


### [53] [From Agent Failure Paths to Quantified Residual Risk: A Compositional Framework for Resilient Agentic AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18243)
*Hassan Karim,Sai Sitharaman,Deepti Gupta,Danda B. Rawat*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes a structured, quantitative framework (CPSAINT + FRIESA-K) to model and measure residual risk in agentic/embodied AI systems across domains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current risk models for agentic AI either (1) explain how failures happen without giving a reusable numerical residual-risk estimate, or (2) give a numerical risk estimate while treating internal failure mechanisms as a black box. This gap prevents rigorous, transferable, and composable trust assessments as AI systems cross traditional trust boundaries and operate in complex physical and financial environments.

Method: Introduce CPSAINT, a seven-layer integrity decomposition that breaks an agentic/embodied AI system into Physical state, Sensors, Data, Compute, Actuators, Environment, and Time, so that failure paths can be explicitly traced across layers. Pair this with FRIESA-K, a residual-risk functional that maps each specific failure path to a quantitative risk instance. The resistance term K in FRIESA-K is grounded in a controlled absorbing Markov model, such that the effectiveness of controls is derived from system state dynamics, not ad hoc scoring. Governance observability is captured via a separate additive penalty rather than embedding governance directly into K. The authors also formalize structural composability, linking valid failure paths to their corresponding risk instances, and demonstrate the approach on two distinct use cases.

Result: A concise, mechanism-to-magnitude pipeline that connects concrete failure mechanisms in agentic/embodied AI systems to quantitative residual risk, preserving the same layer grammar, variable semantics, and dynamic-resistance construction across domains. The framework is instantiated on (1) a hard real-time warehouse robot and (2) a governance-instrumented financial-services agent, showing that identical structural elements can be reused while capturing their very different operational contexts and risks.

Conclusion: The CPSAINT + FRIESA-K framework yields a compact, cross-domain kernel for reasoning about trust in agentic AI. It supports explicit assumptions, structural composability of failure paths into risk instances, and quantitatively grounded residual-risk estimates based on system dynamics. This composable, formally specified approach enables more rigorous and transferable trust assessments for resilient, agentic, and embodied AI systems operating across diverse domains.

Abstract: Agentic AI is crossing trust boundaries faster than current risk models can represent. Existing approaches provide one of two partial views. They either describe failure mechanisms without producing a transferable residual-risk estimate, or they produce a risk estimate while treating the internal failure path as a black box. We couple those two views by proposing CPSAINT, a seven-layer integrity decomposition over Physical state, Sensors, Data, Compute, Actuators, Environment, and Time, paired with FRIESA-K, a residual-risk functional that maps each failure path to a quantified risk instance. FRIESA-K grounds the resistance term K in a controlled absorbing Markov model so that control effectiveness is derived from state dynamics rather than assigned as an informal score. The result is a concise mechanism-to magnitude pipeline for resilient agentic and embodied AI. We report governance observability through a separate additive penalty instead of inserting governance as a new variable in the resistance functional. We formalize structural composability linking valid failure paths to well-defined risk instances and show the framework on two contrasting scenarios a hard real-time warehouse robot and a governance-instrumented financial-services agent. Across both cases, the same layer grammar, variable semantics, and dynamic-resistance construction remain intact. Thus, we obtain a compact kernel that supports cross-domain reasoning, explicit assumptions, and quantitatively grounded formalism of composable trust.

</details>


### [54] [SAAG: Structured Agent Assessment and Grounding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18245)
*Ritvik Garimella,Vedant Khandelwal,Anvi Kohli,Amit Sheth*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose SAAG, a three-stage diagnostic framework to evaluate and iteratively improve LLM agent-calling beyond a single exact-match score.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current evaluations of agent-/function-calling use a coarse exact-match metric that merges distinct failure modes (wrong tool choice vs. hallucinated arguments vs. shallow schema-matching). This prevents practitioners from understanding why calls fail and from giving targeted feedback to fix them.

Method: Define a cascaded three-stage evaluation for agent calls: (1) registry conformance – does the model pick a valid agent from the available registry; (2) structural completeness – are the required arguments present and schema-compliant; (3) argument grounding – are argument values supported by the input rather than hallucinated. Each stage yields explicit diagnostics that can be fed back to the model as structured, stage-specific feedback to drive iterative self-repair. They test this on a controlled benchmark based on Glaive’s function-calling dataset, with varying registry sizes and three local (<4B) models, comparing structured-feedback multi-step inference to single-pass inference and to binary feedback baselines.

Result: Structured, stage-specific feedback improves argument precision and reduces hallucinated values compared to both single-pass generation and using only a binary success/failure signal. However, overall end-to-end F1 improvements are modest and vary across models and registry sizes.

Conclusion: A decomposed, stage-wise diagnostic evaluation of agent-calling reveals and helps address qualitatively different failure modes that exact-match metrics obscure. Even when global F1 gains are modest, such diagnostics are important for understanding and improving agent-calling reliability across different model families and tool registries.

Abstract: Exact-match evaluation of agent-calling obscures qualitatively different failure modes: a model may select the right function yet hallucinate argument values, or satisfy a schema while choosing a agent for the wrong reason. Existing benchmarks collapse these distinctions into a single binary score, leaving practitioners unable to diagnose where agent calls fail. We propose SAAG a cascaded diagnostic framework that decomposes agent-calling evaluation into three sequential stages: registry conformance, structural completeness, and argument grounding, each producing interpretable stage-specific diagnostics. These diagnostics additionally enable iterative self-repair: on prediction failure, the stage-specific signal guides targeted correction without leaking ground-truth values. We evaluate this framework on a controlled benchmark derived from Glaive's function-calling dataset across registry sizes of 5, 10, and 15 agents using three local sub-4B-parameter models. Structured feedback consistently improves argument precision and reduces value hallucination relative to single-pass inference and uninformative binary feedback, while end-to-end F1 gains are modest and model-dependent. These results suggest that stage-decomposed diagnostic evaluation is a necessary lens for understanding and improving agent-calling reliability across model families and registry scales.

</details>


### [55] [Phionyx: A Deterministic AI Runtime Architecture with Structured State Management and Pre-Response Governance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18246)
*Ali Toygar Abak*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Phionyx is a deterministic AI runtime that treats LLM outputs as noisy sensor inputs and uses a governed state machine to ensure reproducible, auditable behavior with built‑in safety and memory control.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM-based agents are typically probabilistic and opaque, which complicates governance, auditability, reproducibility, and safety. The authors want an architecture where AI systems behave like deterministic, controllable software systems with clear state evolution, making them suitable for regulated or high‑assurance environments.

Method: They design Phionyx, a runtime with: (1) a deterministic evaluation kernel built as a fixed 46-block processing pipeline that ingests noisy LLM outputs as sensor measurements; (2) a unified safety layer that applies pre-response controls and enforces privacy at the architectural level; and (3) a semantic time-based memory system that performs impact-weighted cache eviction. They then run single-instance experiments to measure compute overhead, memory retention characteristics, determinism of execution, and reliability of runtime under a defined cost and test model.

Result: On simulated workloads, Phionyx reduces computational overhead by about 31% versus post-hoc filtering when 30% of inputs are unsafe; its impact-weighted memory improves retention of high-value data by up to 24% compared to LRU and 72% compared to FIFO under equal cache capacity; it shows deterministic execution across 100 repeated runs with zero variance in control signals (verified via hashing); and exhibits zero unplanned restarts in single-instance deployment tests.

Conclusion: Phionyx demonstrates that a governance-first, deterministic runtime for LLM-based systems is feasible and can provide reproducible control signals, integrated safety, and more efficient, impact-aware memory management compared with naive baselines. However, evidence is limited to single-instance settings, and extending the architecture to distributed or multi-tenant deployments is left for future work.

Abstract: We present Phionyx, a deterministic AI runtime architecture derived from the broader Echoism interaction framework that introduces a governance-first approach to AI engineering: treating large language model (LLM) outputs as noisy sensor measurements rather than direct decisions. Unlike probabilistic agents, Phionyx enforces deterministic state evolution via a structured state vector governed by deterministic state-evolution equations, enabling reproducible behavior in applications requiring auditability and governance. The architecture integrates three layers: (1) a deterministic evaluation kernel processing noisy sensor measurements through a canonical 46-block pipeline, (2) a unified safety layer providing pre-response control and architectural privacy enforcement, and (3) a semantic time-based memory system implementing impact-weighted cache eviction. Experimental validation on single-instance deployments demonstrates approximately 31% reduction in computational overhead vs. post-hoc filtering (at 30% unsafe input ratio, simulated cost model) and up to 24% improvement in high-value data retention vs. LRU (72% vs. FIFO, same cache capacity, benchmark-verified), deterministic execution verified across 100 repeated runs with zero variance in control signals (hash-verified), and zero unplanned restarts in single-instance deployment testing (see Appendix C for methodology and scope). This paper presents the architecture, its analytic structure, and scoped experimental evidence; generalization to distributed or multi-tenant deployments remains future work.

</details>


### [56] [Integro-differential equations in angular stabilization of drone motion by distributed feedback control](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18251)
*Alexander Domoshnitsky,Oleg Kupervasser,Anatoly Polonsky*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They design and analyze a feedback control method with integral (memory-based) terms to stabilize a drone’s angular motion, even when the control relies on the entire past (unbounded memory). They reduce the resulting integro-differential equations to systems of ODEs and derive conditions for exponential stability, then validate these ideas on drone flight stabilization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conventional feedback control usually considers only current or short-term past states. However, using longer memory of a system’s past behavior can improve stabilization, particularly for complex systems like drones. Standard methods for analyzing integro-differential equations assume bounded memory kernels and do not directly apply when the integral operator has unbounded memory. There is thus a need for a general, mathematically rigorous approach to study stability and design stabilizing controllers in this unbounded-memory regime, especially for practical applications such as drone attitude control.

Method: They introduce a distributed feedback control in the form of an integral operator whose kernel may have unbounded support in time (unbounded memory). To analyze the resulting integro-differential equations, they develop a universal reduction technique that transforms these equations into equivalent systems of ordinary differential equations (ODEs). In the linearized drone angle stabilization problem, they specialize to exponential kernels for the memory term, which yield a finite-dimensional ODE system. They then extend this to more complex kernels (e.g., linear combinations of exponentials) and derive conditions for exponential stability of the closed-loop system. Finally, they apply these theoretical results to the drone’s angular motion model.

Result: 1) A general reduction framework that converts integro-differential equations with unbounded-memory feedback kernels into (possibly infinite) systems of ODEs. 2) For the linearized angle stabilization problem with exponential kernels, a finite-dimensional ODE representation is obtained, enabling explicit stability analysis. 3) They show that more complex kernels built as linear combinations of exponentials can significantly improve stabilization performance. 4) They prove new exponential stability results for such integro-differential systems and demonstrate their effectiveness on a drone flight stabilization problem.

Conclusion: The paper establishes a systematic way to analyze and design memory-based feedback controls with unbounded memory by reducing integro-differential equations to ODE systems. This yields new exponential stability criteria and shows that carefully chosen integral kernels (especially combinations of exponentials) can enhance the angular stabilization of drones. The proposed approach is broadly applicable to other systems governed by integro-differential equations with long-memory feedback.

Abstract: In this paper, we propose angular stabilization of drone motion using distributed feedback control in the form of an integral operator. It should be stressed that the memory of this integral operator could be unbounded. It is intuitively clear that large length of the observation time open new possibilities to construct better control based on previous states of the control object. Unbounded memory in control requires the creation of a certain approach different from standard ones to the study of integro-differential equations. One of the goals of this article is to propose a certain universal approach that allows us to study the stability of integro-differential equations in the case of unbounded memory in the integral operator specifying the feedback control in stabilization. The approach we propose allows us to reduce the study of integro-differential equations to the analysis of systems of ordinary differential equations. In general, such systems can consist of an infinite number of equations. In relation to the so-called linear approximation in the problem of angle stabilization manages to limit itself to relatively simple exponential kernels in the integral control and arrive at a system with a finite number of equations. The examples explain that more complex kernels, for example, linear combinations of the exponential kernels, can enhance the stabilization capabilities. We obtain new unexpectable results on the exponential stability of integro-differential equations. Then we apply them to stabilization of drone flight.

</details>

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM query routers focus on selecting models based on quality and monetary cost but largely ignore latency. In real deployments, latency is handled separately via simple load balancing (e.g., round-robin, shortest-queue) that is unaware of model accuracy or cost, leading to suboptimal overall efficiency and user experience. Latency is hard to integrate into routing because it depends on dynamic system-level factors like concurrent workloads, batching, and scheduling, not just prompt length.

Method: They design a lightweight latency estimator that simulates autoregressive token batch processing within the serving framework to predict time-to-first-token (TTFT) for each query on each model instance. This estimator accounts for prompt length, current prefill and decode loads, and batching/scheduling behavior. They then build a latency-aware router that uses these latency predictions alongside model accuracy and monetary cost to decide where to send each query, effectively solving a joint optimization over latency, accuracy, and cost instead of treating these axes separately.

Result: Experiments show that their router achieves up to 40% higher accuracy–cost utility compared with baselines, while keeping latency comparable to standard load-balancing policies like round-robin or join-the-shortest-queue. This demonstrates that explicitly modeling latency in the router can yield substantial utility gains without harming responsiveness.

Conclusion: Incorporating a realistic, lightweight latency estimator into query routing allows LLM serving systems to jointly optimize latency, accuracy, and cost. This approach outperforms traditional latency-agnostic routers and naive load balancers, delivering significantly better accuracy–cost trade-offs at similar latency, and highlights the importance of system-level latency modeling for efficient LLM deployment.

Abstract: Modern language query routers improve inference efficiency by assigning each query to a model that balances response quality and monetary cost. However, current query routers are largely latency-agnostic and do not consider the generation latency experienced by queries at model instances. In practice, latency is often controlled by load-balancing policies such as round-robin or join-the-shortest-queue, which do not account for model accuracy or inference cost. Incorporating query latency into routing is challenging as it depends not only on the query's prompt length, but also on the current prefill and decode workload at the model instance and the scheduling and batching policy of the serving framework. We design a lightweight latency estimator that simulates autoregressive token batch processing in the serving framework and estimates the time-to-first-token (TTFT) of queries. We incorporate this latency estimator into a latency-aware router that jointly optimizes latency, accuracy, and cost when assigning queries to model instances. Our experimental results indicate that this joint optimization yields up to 40% improvement in accuracy--cost utility while maintaining the same latencies as standard load-balancing approaches.

### [59] [Cross-Dialect Generalization Without Retraining: Benchmarks and Evaluation of Schema-Derived Constrained Decoding for MLIR](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18254)
*Plawan Kumar Rath*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether mechanically derived, inference-time structural constraints from MLIR dialect specifications can replace fine-tuning for generating valid MLIR from natural language, releasing new benchmarks and showing that a small code model plus schema-derived constraints can match or outperform much larger models on structurally dominated dialects.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MLIR is central to modern ML compiler stacks but is underrepresented in code-pretraining corpora and is highly extensible via new dialects, making per-dialect fine-tuning impractical. The authors want to know if one can exploit the formal Operation Definition Specifications (ODS) of each dialect at inference time to guide code generation, instead of relying on data- and compute-intensive gradient-based adaptation. This addresses scalability of MLIR support across many dialects and the need for accurate NL-to-MLIR translation without retraining.

Method: They first construct and release several NL-to-MLIR benchmarks across multiple dialects: MLIR-Spec-150, Linalg-Spec-30, StableHLO-Spec-30, StableHLO-Held-Out-200, plus additional stress and functional reference sets. Then they design a three-layer, schema-derived constraint system obtained from each dialect's ODS: (C1) a control-flow graph over operation signatures, (C2) type-domain constraints derived from an extracted type lattice, and (C3) an SSA scope validator that is used inside a rejection-sampling loop (up to five retries). These constraints are layered on top of an existing small code model (SmolLM2-1.7B) at decoding time, and they demonstrate portability of the constraint stack when moving from one dialect bundle (arith+func+memref+linalg) to StableHLO without changing core constraint logic.

Result: On dialects where verifier semantics are mostly structural (e.g., linalg), the constrained decoding system using SmolLM2-1.7B achieves high rates of verifier-valid MLIR, reaching 80% verify-valid on linalg (three-seed mean, n=125). This performance surpasses larger code models such as CodeLlama-34B, Granite-Code-34B, and StarCoder2-15B by 21–44 percentage points, with non-overlapping confidence intervals, while also being 8–25× faster per generation. However, on dialects where correctness depends more on attribute values than on structural constraints (arith+func and parametric StableHLO-Held-Out-200), the larger baselines match or outperform the small model with constraints, which the authors classify as non-win cells. They also show that adding StableHLO support did not require new constraint-layer code, supporting the claim of portability.

Conclusion: Mechanically derived, schema-based constraints from MLIR dialect specifications can substantially improve the quality and efficiency of NL-to-MLIR generation, enabling a small language model to match or exceed far larger models on structurally governed dialects without additional fine-tuning. The constraint stack is portable across dialects, suggesting a scalable way to support many MLIR dialects via inference-time priors rather than per-dialect training. Nonetheless, for dialects whose correctness hinges on non-structural attributes, this approach alone is insufficient to outperform strong large code models, indicating that complementary techniques are needed there. The authors provide datasets, tooling, and reproducibility artifacts to support further research in schema-guided MLIR generation.

Abstract: Multi-Level Intermediate Representation (MLIR) underlies modern ML compiler infrastructure (TensorFlow, JAX/StableHLO, PyTorch Inductor, IREE), yet appears only in trace amounts in code-LM pretraining corpora. MLIR is also extensible by design: new dialects ship per application domain, so a fine-tuned model per dialect does not scale. We ask whether inference-time priors derived mechanically from each dialect's Operation Definition Specification (ODS) can substitute for gradient-based adaptation. First, we release four natural-language-to-MLIR benchmarks across three dialects - MLIR-Spec-150, Linalg-Spec-30, StableHLO-Spec-30, and StableHLO-Held-Out-200 - totaling 410 in-scope NL-to-MLIR pairs, plus a 25-program out-of-grammar stress set and a hand-authored n=30 functional reference set, shipped under Apache-2.0 with Gebru datasheets and Croissant 1.0 metadata. Second, we build a three-layer schema-derived constraint stack: a CFG over op signatures(C1), type-domain splits from an ODS-extracted type lattice (C2), and an SSA-scope validator driving five-retry rejection sampling (C3). Porting from arith+func+memref+linalg to StableHLO required no new constraint-layer code. On dialects whose verifier semantics are dominated by structural constraints, schema-derived priors let SmolLM2-1.7B match or exceed 15B-34B code LMs at 8-25x the per-generation speed: on linalg, SmolLM2 reaches 80.0% verify-valid (three-seed mean, n=125), beating CodeLlama-34B, Granite-Code-34B, and StarCoder2-15B by 21-44 percentage points with non-overlapping CIs. On arith+func and on the templated parametric StableHLO-Held-Out-200, where verifier semantics turn on attribute values rather than structure, the same baselines match or beat the SLM; we scope these as non-win cells. We release benchmarks, decoder, all per-prompt generations, and a reproducibility Docker image.

### [60] [Semantic Cooperative Games for Contribution Attribution in LLM-Based Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18255)
*Pengyi Jiang,Xiaoguang Zhu,Quanyan Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce a fast, single-run method (SLIC) to attribute contributions of different agents in LLM-based multi-agent workflows by analyzing the semantic information flow instead of rerunning counterfactual subsets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In LLM-based multi-agent systems, many agents exchange messages and depend on each other to produce a final answer. We want to know which agents actually contributed what. Existing attribution approaches use counterfactual experiments (removing or modifying agents and re-running the system). This is computationally expensive, noisy, and ignores the rich intermediate semantics carried in messages, especially in language-mediated workflows. A more efficient, semantically grounded, and interpretable attribution method is needed.

Method: They model the realized run of a multi-agent system as a semantic generation hypergraph that represents how pieces of information are produced, preserved, and transformed through agents and messages. On top of this, they define a cooperative game in the semantic space, inducing an agent-level semantic value function. They then introduce the Semantic Shapley Value (SSV) to allocate value across agents based on semantic support logic rather than direct counterfactual reruns. The SLIC algorithm builds the semantic hypergraph from a single trajectory, identifies minimal semantic supports for the final output, applies Boolean absorption to simplify overlapping supports, and computes SSV contributions without re-executing any agent subsets.

Result: They theoretically show that when the workflow is fully observable, order-independent, and can be represented as a standard set-based game, SSV collapses to the classical Shapley value. On a medical benchmark that satisfies these assumptions, SLIC yields agent attributions highly consistent with a Monte Carlo Shapley baseline, while reducing computation cost by 93.3%. In more complex multi-role workflows that violate these assumptions, SSV’s scores track perturbation-induced score drops but also highlight cases where an agent’s semantic contribution is large even if its removal does not produce a proportional performance drop, revealing deeper structure in how information flows and fails.

Conclusion: Semantic Cooperative Games and the SLIC algorithm provide a counterfactual-free, efficient, and interpretable way to attribute contributions in LLM-based multi-agent systems. By operating on a semantic generation hypergraph and defining the Semantic Shapley Value, they preserve desirable Shapley properties in simple settings, achieve dramatic computational savings, and offer richer diagnostics of semantic contribution versus failure impact in complex workflows.

Abstract: Contribution attribution has become a central problem in LLM-based multi-agent systems, where final outputs are produced through multiple agents, message exchanges, and ordered workflow dependencies. Existing attribution methods often rely on counterfactual valuation, such as removing agents or comparing score changes across altered agent subsets. In language-mediated workflows, these methods require repeated model calls, introduce high variance, and do not explicitly capture the intermediate semantic states through which agents produce, preserve, and transform task-relevant information. We propose Semantic Cooperative Games (SCG), a framework that represents a realized language flow as a semantic generation hypergraph and induces an agent-level semantic value function on this structure. We define the Semantic Shapley Value (SSV) to allocate contribution over semantic support logic, and introduce SLIC, a single-trajectory algorithm that constructs the semantic hypergraph, recovers minimal semantic supports, applies Boolean absorption, and computes SSV without rerunning agent subsets. We prove that SSV reduces to the classical Shapley value under standard set-based, fully observable, and no-order-dependence conditions. On a medical benchmark satisfying these conditions, SLIC reduces computation cost by 93.3% while remaining highly consistent with a Monte Carlo Shapley baseline. In more general multi-role workflows, SSV aligns with perturbation-induced score-drop profiles and exposes cases where semantic contribution and failure impact diverge. Overall, SLIC provides a fast, counterfactual-free, and interpretable attribution method for complex LLM-based multi-agent systems.

By learning when and how to use solver and execution feedback to iteratively refine models, PEARL delivers better-verified optimization formulations and solver code, and demonstrates that careful integration of tools and iterative reasoning can enable smaller models to outperform much larger ones on realistic optimization modeling tasks.

Abstract: Optimization modeling is the process of translating real-world decision problems, often described in natural language, into formal mathematical formulations and executable solver code. While recent advances in large language models have shown promise in automating this process, most existing approaches remain one-shot: a model produces a formulation once, without executing it, conditioning on solver feedback, or iteratively revising errors. This stands in sharp contrast to real-world optimization modeling, which is inherently interactive and proceeds through repeated solve-debug-revise cycles. We introduce PEARL, a system for interactive optimization modeling that uses Python execution and mathematical programming solvers inside this loop. Rather than relying on a fixed repair workflow, PEARL learns when to test partial models, how to revise from solver diagnostics, and when to stop. It operates in a multi-turn tool-integrated setting where intermediate execution results, feasibility signals, and solution checks are used to improve both formulations and solver code before finalization. Across diverse optimization benchmarks, PEARL substantially improves verified solve rates over strong one-shot and tool-augmented baselines; notably, our PEARL-Qwen3-\textbf{4B} model outperforms the much larger DeepSeek-V3.2-\textbf{685B} in both macro- and micro-averaged accuracy on optimization modeling tasks.

*Wei Chen,Guanghui Zhu,Yafei Li,Limin Wang,Yihua Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes S2T-RLHF, a sentence-to-token reward decomposition method that improves stability and robustness in RLHF by using sentence-level intermediate rewards instead of purely sequence- or token-level rewards.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RLHF uses a single scalar reward at the sequence level, which leads to ambiguous token-level credit assignment and unstable optimization, especially when preference feedback is noisy. Recent attempts to make rewards denser at the token level can actually worsen instability by amplifying uncertainty. The authors aim to find a principled way to assign credit hierarchically that balances granularity and stability.

Method: They introduce a granularity-aware principle for hierarchical credit assignment that prioritizes stability over maximal precision in reward allocation. Concretely, they treat sentences as the key intermediate unit: sequence-level preference rewards are first distributed across sentences, then a bounded refinement is performed from sentences to tokens. This S2T-RLHF procedure decomposes rewards without retraining the reward model and without needing token-level supervision, effectively structuring the reward signal across sentence and token levels.

Result: Across multiple datasets and RL optimization setups, S2T-RLHF yields more stable training dynamics and greater robustness to noise compared with standard RLHF and other dense-reward approaches, while keeping preference alignment performance competitive with baselines.

Conclusion: Finer-grained rewards are not always better when preference feedback is noisy and response-level; too much refinement can hurt stability. Using sentences as an intermediate granularity for hierarchical credit assignment offers a better trade-off between stability and precision. S2T-RLHF operationalizes this idea and empirically improves RLHF training stability and robustness without sacrificing alignment quality.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF) with preference-based reward models often exhibits unstable training dynamics. A key contributing factor is that standard RLHF relies on a single sequence-level scalar reward, which is propagated to token-level policy updates and leaves credit assignment within a response inherently ambiguous. Recent work has attempted to address this issue by refining rewards into denser token-level supervision, often relying on the implicit assumption that finer-grained credit assignment improves optimization. We argue that this assumption is incomplete: when preference signals are noisy and only defined at the response level, overly fine-grained reward refinement can amplify reward uncertainty and destabilize learning. To address this problem, we propose a granularity-aware principle for hierarchical credit assignment, emphasizing stability-oriented reward design rather than maximal allocation precision. Under this principle, sentences serve as a natural intermediate granularity, balancing semantic coherence with robustness to token-level noise. Guided by this view, we introduce S2T-RLHF. This sentence-to-token reward decomposition framework first allocates sequence-level preference rewards across sentences and then applies bounded token-level refinement within each sentence, without reward-model retraining or token-level supervision. Experiments across multiple datasets and optimization settings show that S2T-RLHF improves training stability and robustness while maintaining competitive preference alignment.

TL;DR: A new inference-time steering method for LLMs that is probabilistic and concept-aware, aiming for safer and more coherent control without hurting task performance.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This involves modeling semantic alignment along a continuous spectrum and adjusting how strongly the concept vector is injected into intermediate activations during inference so that behavior remains coherent and controllable.

Result: PCS enables controllable, safety-oriented semantic biasing of LLM outputs while better preserving the model’s original capabilities compared with prior steering vector methods. The probabilistic calibration yields more representation-coherent behaviors and finer-grained control over concept expression than existing binary or discretely evaluated techniques.

Conclusion: Probabilistic, concept-aware steering offers a more interpretable and reliable way to control LLM behavior at inference time. By focusing on concept-based vector retrieval and probabilistic strength calibration, PCS mitigates representation-incoherent behaviors of prior methods, supports fine-grained semantic control, and maintains task performance, making it promising for safety-critical applications.

Abstract: Steering vectors (SVs), an inference-time intervention technique for large language models (LLMs), guide the generation process by adding a concept-specific direction vector to intermediate activations during inference. However, existing SV methods frequently yield representation-incoherent behaviors that undermine interpretability and fine-grained control, largely because prior work has focused on binary positive-negative steering evaluation while employing discrete clustering metrics that fail to capture the continuous spectrum of semantic alignment. In this work, we present the Probabilistic Concept-Aware Steering (PCS) framework for LLM inference. PCS preserves original task competence while providing controllable, safety-oriented semantic bias through concept-driven steering-vector retrieval and probabilistic strength calibration.

Method: They build FindStatBench from the FindStat database with 2,329 tasks spanning 24 collections and 5.52M hidden instances. Each task provides a mathematical description and up to five public examples, and models must output a single Python solve() function without any tools, retrieval, or interactive feedback. Performance is measured via exact execution on hidden test instances. They benchmark eleven LLM systems (four closed-source, seven open-weight) to characterize accuracy patterns and failure modes.

Result: Top open- and closed-source models achieve similar instance accuracy (within 1 percentage point). Certain failures are due to long reasoning that runs out of output budget before producing code. Statistic synthesis tasks are solved much more reliably than map synthesis; several collections remain almost unsolved, and long prompts cause a sharp drop in accuracy.

Conclusion: FindStatBench exposes current LLM limitations in exact symbolic rule induction for combinatorial mathematics, particularly in map synthesis and under longer prompts. While leading open and closed models perform similarly, example-based prompting and output-budget constraints can unexpectedly hurt performance, and many structured combinatorial tasks remain challenging, indicating substantial headroom for future model and prompting advances.

Abstract: We introduce FindStatBench, an execution benchmark for evaluating large language models on combinatorial code synthesis. Built from FindStat, it contains 2,329 tasks across 24 collections and 5.52M hidden instances, covering statistic synthesis, which maps objects to integers, and map synthesis, which maps objects to objects. Each task gives a mathematical description and at most five public input-output examples; a model must emit one Python solve function with no retrieval, tool use, execution feedback, voting, or reranking. Submissions are scored by exact sandboxed execution on held-out combinatorial objects. We evaluate eleven systems: four closed-source production models and seven open-weight models served through one inference provider. FindStatBench reveals three main patterns. First, the strongest open- and closed-source systems converge within 1 pp instance accuracy, and both an oracle over all systems and five-way sampling from one mid-tier model yield only limited task-accuracy gains. Second, examples can hurt: several classical bijections are solved perfectly with zero examples but fail under five-example prompts. Third, some failures reflect output-budget mechanics, as reasoning can exhaust the visible response before code is emitted. Overall, statistic synthesis is much easier than map synthesis, some collections remain near-zero, long prompts cause a sharp accuracy cliff, and exact symbolic rule induction remains brittle.

While JSON Schema and structured-output modes ensure syntactic and schema correctness, they do not guarantee that the transaction faithfully and safely represents the user’s intent. There is a need for a benchmark that isolates and measures these deeper semantic and safety aspects beyond mere parse and schema validity.

Conclusion: Structured-output mechanisms like JSON Schema are helpful interface layers that reduce parsing failures but are insufficient for guaranteeing semantic faithfulness or safety of LLM-generated transactions. Robust systems must add explicit domain-level verification and enforce fail-closed execution, treating schema validation as a first step rather than a complete safety solution.

Abstract: LLM agents are increasingly used as transaction compilers: a user states an intent in natural language, and the model emits a structured object that an API can execute. JSON Schema and provider-level structured-output modes are useful because they remove a large class of parse failures, but they do not by themselves decide whether the object is a safe, faithful transaction. We introduce OrderBench, a deterministic benchmark for restaurant ordering agents that separates syntactic validity, schema validity, status decisions, exact item semantics, constraint preservation, and unsafe acceptances. Across 2,400 Nebius Token Factory calls to four open models in prompt-only and JSON-schema modes, we find that schema-valid output can still have large semantic error rates. In the strongest model, both modes achieve 100% schema validity, yet semantic success remains near 80%; in weaker models, schema-valid unsafe acceptances occur in double digits. The result is a concrete engineering warning: structured output is a necessary interface layer, not a substitute for domain verification and fail-closed execution.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Circular-factory applications need to integrate and query large amounts of sensor-derived measurement data (e.g., from returned products) that are multi-dimensional and uncertain. Existing RDF/SPARQL stacks treat numbers as precise values and push any uncertainty handling and probabilistic reasoning into application code, which hinders reuse, optimization, and interoperability. The authors want a query language that can natively represent and operate on distributions so downstream modules (triage, validation, reliability modeling, planning) can use uncertainty-aware queries over a shared knowledge graph.

Method: They design ProbSPARQL, an upward-compatible extension of SPARQL where numeric literals can encode probability distributions via new probabilistic RDF datatypes. The extended language supports (1) distribution-aware expressions, (2) probabilistic filters that reason over uncertainty, and (3) joins based on statistical divergence between distributions instead of only exact value equality. They implement ProbSPARQL on top of Apache Jena ARQ and provide a Fuseki-compatible endpoint. They then run two evaluations: (a) a real-data case study using project data with Gaussian Mixture Model (GMM) encoded uncertainty and histogram-based roughness distributions, and (b) synthetic, ontology-conformant benchmarks with up to 5,000 product instances and 1.5M triples to test scalability and different divergence-join strategies.

Result: Their prototype shows that probabilistic reasoning can be executed directly inside the SPARQL engine with acceptable performance. Querying with uncertainty-aware filters and joins is feasible, and performing these operations in the engine yields speedups compared to doing them as post-processing in the application layer, largely due to filter-pushdown. Different strategies for implementing divergence-based joins exhibit trade-offs between query latency and the accuracy of the probabilistic matching, but all remain practically usable at the tested scale.

Conclusion: ProbSPARQL demonstrates that RDF/SPARQL infrastructures can be extended to natively represent and query uncertain numeric sensor data in a scalable way. By treating numeric values as random variables with explicit distributions and adding probabilistic filters and divergence-based joins, they enable more principled, efficient, and reusable uncertainty-aware analytics over circular-factory knowledge graphs. This provides a viable query-layer foundation for the SFB 1574 Circular Factory and suggests a path toward standardized probabilistic extensions of SPARQL for other domains that rely on uncertain measurement data.

Abstract: The SFB 1574 Circular Factory is building a shared knowledge graph infrastructure for integrating data about returned products. A central challenge is that circular-factory data include numeric measurements that (i) originate from sensors or are derived from sensor-based measurements, (ii) are frequently multi-dimensional, and (iii) are inherently uncertain, while downstream triage, validation, reliability-modeling, and reassembly-planning modules require queryable uncertainty representations. Current RDF and SPARQL technologies lack native support for harmonized querying and analysis of such uncertain numeric measurement data. To address this gap, we present ProbSPARQL, an upward-compatible SPARQL extension developed as an early-stage query-layer pilot for this infrastructure. ProbSPARQL models uncertain numeric values as random variables whose distributions are encoded by probabilistic RDF literal datatypes, and supports distribution-aware expressions, probabilistic filters, and divergence-based joins. We implement ProbSPARQL on Apache Jena ARQ and expose it through a Fuseki-compatible execution layer. We assess real-data applicability using project-derived measurement fragments covering GMM-encoded uncertainty and histogram-based empirical roughness distributions, and evaluate scalability separately on controlled ontology-conformant benchmarks with up to 5,000 angle-grinder instances and 1.5M triples. The results show feasible in-engine execution, filter-pushdown speedups over application-layer post-processing, and latency-accuracy trade-offs among divergence-join decision strategies.

</details>


### [67] [Position: AI/ML Deepfake Research is Misaligned with AI-Generated Non-Consensual Intimate Imagery (AIG-NCII)](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18263)
*Li Qiwei,Wells Lucas Santo,Sarita Schoenebeck,Eric Gilbert*

TL;DR: The paper argues that current deepfake research focuses too narrowly on detecting inauthentic media and neglects AI-generated non-consensual intimate imagery (AIG-NCII), which causes serious dignity harms to depicted subjects.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To highlight a mismatch between the focus of AI/ML deepfake research—mainly on epistemic harms like misinformation—and the real-world prevalence and severity of subject-centric harms caused by AI-generated sexualized abuse, particularly AIG-NCII.

Method: The authors conduct a landscape analysis of highly cited deepfake-related works to examine what kinds of harms and threat models they address, showing that most focus on authenticity detection and viewer-centric risks, with little to no attention to AIG-NCII. They then offer a conceptual and normative argument about the limitations of this focus.

Result: The analysis reveals that the technical deepfake research ecosystem overwhelmingly prioritizes authenticity verification tools and epistemic harms (fraud, scams, misinformation), with minimal engagement with subject-centric dignity harms such as AIG-NCII. The authors show that recognizing an image as synthetic does not eliminate or necessarily reduce harms to the person depicted and can sometimes worsen them.

Conclusion: The field of deepfake and generative AI research should broaden its threat models and interventions to include subject-centric harms, particularly AIG-NCII, and integrate these concerns into AI safety agendas. Researchers entering this high-risk domain should adopt strong safety guardrails, work with experts in sexual violence prevention, and design interventions that protect and center the affected subjects rather than only informing viewers about authenticity.

Abstract: AI-generated non-consensual intimate imagery (AIG-NCII) is not adequately addressed in AI/ML literature regarding AI-generated media, commonly referred to as "deepfakes". While research on deepfakes currently focuses on its epistemic harms -- or harms relating to truth and authenticity -- this is misaligned with the dominant reality of generative AI abuse involving sexualized imagery. We conduct a landscape analysis of highly-cited works to demonstrate that technical interventions addressing deepfakes almost entirely ignore AIG-NCII, limiting the research ecosystem to authenticity detection tools. In this position paper, we argue that existing interventions address viewer-centric epistemic harms, such as fraud or scams, but ignore subject-centric dignity harms, such as AIG-NCII. We illustrate that knowing an image is synthetic does not mitigate harms to subjects and may, in some cases, even exacerbate them. We conclude by offering recommendations to realign the field, including updating threat models to consider subject-centric harms and addressing AIG-NCII in AI safety research. Finally, we caution that researchers should only engage in this high-risk domain if they implement safety guardrails for both subjects and researchers and establish partnerships with domain experts in sexual violence prevention.
