### [68] [MUX: Continuous Reasoning via Multiplexed Tokens](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18264)
*Ayhan Suleymanzade,Halil Alperen Gozeten,Michael Bronstein,İsmail İlkan Ceylan,Jinwoo Kim*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: MUX compresses multi-step natural-language reasoning into fewer continuous latent tokens via lossless superposition of subwords, enabling higher-bandwidth, more efficient reasoning than standard chain-of-thought.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chain-of-thought reasoning in language models is powerful but inefficient: it uses many autoregressive steps, each emitting only a single subword, and spends computation expressing thoughts in natural language instead of doing compact internal computation. The authors want a way to retain the benefits of explicit reasoning while reducing the computational and token overhead, and to avoid problems like shortcutting and latent collapse in continuous reasoning representations.

Method: They introduce MUX, which distills discrete chain-of-thought sequences into a sequence of continuous latent tokens. Each latent token represents a weighted linear superposition of a span of subword tokens, with position-dependent weights (e.g., geometric decay) chosen so that the mapping is mathematically invertible and the original token span is recoverable (demultiplexing). This constructs lossless multiplexed representations used as local learning targets. They prove conditions under which these weightings ensure lossless multiplexing and thus discourage latent collapse, and they analyze how such multiplexed tokens can support parallel search-like reasoning. The approach is evaluated as a plug-in latent reasoning mechanism on several base language models and compared to other latent reasoning baselines, with additional ablations and probing to study faithfulness and interpretability.

Result: Across 32 benchmark settings and four different language models, MUX consistently outperforms strong latent reasoning baselines. Empirical studies and probing show that the latent tokens learned under MUX retain the information of the original reasoning traces, are interpretable, and align well with the underlying discrete chain-of-thought. They also demonstrate that the multiplexed representations can support parallel exploration in search-heavy tasks, indicating that the method improves both efficiency and quality of reasoning.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that constructing lossless superpositions of chain-of-thought spans as local learning targets is sufficient to obtain strong, efficient continuous latent reasoning in language models. By ensuring exact recoverability of the original reasoning steps and preventing shortcut behaviors via lossless multiplexing, MUX achieves higher-bandwidth, compact reasoning that remains faithful and interpretable while offering better performance and efficiency than existing latent reasoning approaches.

Abstract: Language models solve complex problems by articulating intermediate reasoning steps in natural language. While effective, this process is computationally bottlenecked: each reasoning step conveys only a single subword, and many are spent expressing a thought instead of carrying out computation. We propose MUX, a simple method for high-bandwidth and compact reasoning based on distillation of discrete reasoning into continuous multiplexed tokens in a latent space. Here, each latent token is trained to represent a weighted linear superposition (multiplexing) of a span of discrete reasoning subwords, where this superposition is lossless by construction and the span can be fully recovered (demultiplexing). We prove that simple position-dependent weightings, such as suitable geometric decay, support lossless multiplexing, which in turn prevents shortcut behaviors caused by latent collapse. We further show that multiplexed reasoning can perform parallel exploration in problems that require search. Across 32 evaluation settings spanning four language models, MUX outperforms strong latent reasoning baselines. Ablation and probing analyses further show that the learned latent tokens encode faithful and interpretable reasoning. Our results suggest that lossless superposition as local learning targets constitutes a sufficient condition for achieving strong and efficient latent continuous reasoning.

</details>


### [69] [State Compression in Two-Agent LLM Relays: A Closed-World Study of Constraint Preservation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18265)
*Anantha Sharma,Sheeba Elizabeth John,Kaarthik Senthil Kumar,Saratsuhas Vijayababu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how different ways of compressing intermediate LLM agent state affect the ability of a downstream LLM to satisfy strict constraints in a travel-planning relay task.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based agents doing long, multi-step reasoning produce large traces that must often be compressed before being passed to subsequent agents. This compression can create information bottlenecks where small omissions cause failures to meet hard numeric or categorical constraints. There is little systematic evaluation of how different hand-off representations affect constraint satisfaction in such pipelines.

Method: The authors build a closed-world travel-planning relay with two LLM agents. A Researcher agent audits a fixed inventory of hotels and flights for 50 goal instances, producing an intermediate trace. A Booker agent then chooses a hotel–flight pair using only the goal and a compressed hand-off payload, without seeing the original inventory. They compare four hand-off conditions: (1) no compression (full trace), (2) narrative summarization, (3) schema-constrained JSON extraction, and (4) embedding-based pruning of the trace. Because the inventory is fixed and small, they can exhaustively enumerate all feasible and optimal hotel–flight combinations, allowing exact labels for feasibility and optimality. They then measure feasibility accuracy under each hand-off condition.

Result: Hand-off representation has a large impact on whether the Booker’s choices satisfy constraints. JSON-based structured extraction gives the best feasibility accuracy at 0.96. Narrative summarization produces the most compact payload but severely harms feasibility, dropping to 0.48. Embedding-based pruning, which removes less relevant parts of the unstructured trace without an extra generative compression step, matches the uncompressed baseline feasibility at 0.88.

Conclusion: For LLM agent pipelines that must respect strict constraints, the structure and auditability of intermediate hand-offs matter more than aggressive brevity. Schema-constrained, structured representations like JSON help preserve constraint-relevant details and yield high feasibility, while purely narrative summaries can omit critical information. Simple embedding-based pruning can retain feasibility comparable to no compression, suggesting a promising direction for scalable, low-cost compression that doesn’t require additional generation steps.

Abstract: Long-running Large Language Model (LLM)-based agents often accumulate large intermediate traces containing audits, eliminations, and numeric calculations. In practice, this state is compressed before handing it to a downstream decision step, creating an information bottleneck in which small omissions can break strict numeric or categorical constraints. This paper evaluates hand-off compression in a closed-world travel-planning relay with two LLM agents. A Researcher audits a fixed inventory of hotels and flights for 50 goal instances, and a Booker selects a hotel--flight pair using only the goal and the hand-off payload, with the inventory withheld. We compare four hand-off conditions: no compression, narrative summarization, schema-constrained JSON extraction, and embedding-based pruning. Exhaustive enumeration over the fixed inventory provides exact feasible and optimal labels. Results show that hand-off representation strongly affects downstream feasibility under a small decision model. JSON extraction achieves the highest feasibility accuracy at 0.96, while narrative summarization, despite producing the smallest compressed hand-off payload, degrades feasibility to 0.48. Embedding-based pruning matches the uncompressed control on feasibility at 0.88 without an additional generative compression call. These findings indicate that constraint checking benefits from structured and auditable hand-off representations rather than relying on brevity alone.

</details>


### [70] [Structured Synthetic Reasoning Data for Arithmetic Fine-Tuning of Small Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18266)
*Jake O'Grady,Effirul Ramlan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that carefully designed synthetic reasoning data significantly boosts multi-step arithmetic performance of small language models under consumer hardware constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Small language models are practical for local, privacy-preserving deployment but perform poorly on multi-step arithmetic reasoning tasks. The authors want to know whether a modest amount of structured, synthetic training data—feasible to generate and train on with consumer hardware—can substantially improve their arithmetic reasoning without requiring huge datasets or massive compute.

Method: Starting from the GSM8K benchmark, the authors use a larger model (GPT-5-mini) to generate a 21,250-example synthetic corpus of grade-school math word-problem variants. The data includes: natural-language solution traces, light Socratic-style guiding questions, structural variations of problems, and irrelevant distractor context. They then fine-tune two small models, Qwen3-0.6B and Qwen3-1.7B, using LoRA on an Apple M4 machine with 16 GB RAM. Performance is evaluated on GSM8K and transferred to other arithmetic benchmarks (MultiArith, SVAMP), with qualitative analysis of reasoning traces and error patterns, plus tests with self-consistency sampling.

Result: Fine-tuning on the structured synthetic data substantially improves exact-match accuracy on GSM8K: from 36.5% to 49.1% for Qwen3-0.6B and from 53.5% to 66.5% for Qwen3-1.7B. For Qwen3-1.7B, transfer performance greatly improves, from 54.4% to 98.9% on MultiArith and from 45.3% to 73.0% on SVAMP. Qualitatively, the fine-tuned models generate shorter, more focused reasoning traces, commit fewer arithmetic and distractor-related errors, and benefit more reliably from self-consistency sampling.

Conclusion: The study concludes that low-cost, carefully structured synthetic reasoning data can materially enhance arithmetic reasoning capabilities in small language models, even under tight hardware constraints. The gains appear to come from the combination of Socratic-style cues with other data-design features, so the work supports the value of structured synthetic reasoning data broadly, rather than isolating Socratic guidance as the sole causal factor.

Abstract: Small language models are attractive for local deployment, but they often struggle with multi-step arithmetic reasoning. We study whether structured synthetic reasoning data can improve this behaviour under consumer-hardware constraints. Starting from GSM8K, we generated a 21,250-example corpus of grade-school arithmetic word-problem variants using GPT-5-mini, combining natural-language solution traces, light Socratic-style cues, structural variation, and irrelevant distractor context. We then fine-tuned Qwen3-0.6B and Qwen3-1.7B with LoRA on consumer hardware (Apple M4, 16 GB RAM). Exact-match accuracy on GSM8K improved from 36.5% to 49.1% for Qwen3-0.6B and from 53.5% to 66.5% for Qwen3-1.7B. For Qwen3-1.7B, transfer to related arithmetic benchmarks was stronger, reaching 98.9% on MultiArith and 73.0% on SVAMP, compared with 54.4% and 45.3% for the base model. Qualitative analysis suggests that fine-tuned models produce shorter reasoning traces, make fewer arithmetic and distractor-use errors, and benefit more consistently from self-consistency sampling. These results show that low-cost synthetic data design can materially improve arithmetic adaptation in small language models. Because the intervention combines Socratic-style cues with other data-design choices, we interpret the gains as evidence for structured synthetic reasoning data rather than as a causal test of Socratic guidance alone.

Abstract: Real-world applications that use closed-source large language models (LLMs) need advanced safety measures that go beyond the basic content filters. ### [72] [Wisdom of LLM Crowds: Aggregation and Contamination in Language Model Ensembles](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18269)
*Igor Douven*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Study tests whether aggregating multiple LLMs’ probabilistic forecasts yields a “wisdom of crowds” effect and under what conditions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Wisdom-of-crowds is well known in humans, but it’s unclear if similar benefits appear when aggregating different LLMs’ forecasts, which matters for both theory of collective intelligence and practical forecasting systems that might combine several models.

Method: The authors collected probabilistic predictions from 15 different LLMs on 254 binary prediction-market questions, then compared individual-model performance to several aggregation approaches, including simple classical rules and learned aggregators (logistic regression and a multilayer perceptron). They further used symbolic regression to interpret the learned neural aggregator and carefully analyzed the confounding effect of training-data cutoff contamination by separating questions that resolved before vs. after the models’ training cutoffs and by re-evaluating performance at each model’s cutoff date.

Result: Learned aggregation (both MLP and logistic regression) beat all individual LLMs and all classical aggregation rules. Logistic regression matched the MLP, implying most gains come from linear weighting of diverse model outputs rather than complex nonlinear interactions. Symbolic regression showed that the neural network effectively relied on a model-disagreement signal as its simplest useful feature. When restricting to questions whose resolution dates were after all models’ training cutoffs (to avoid contamination), the large apparent performance gap between big cloud models and smaller local models shrank sharply and model rankings became only moderately stable. Even when evaluating questions as of each model’s training cutoff, LLMs as a collective still underperformed the prediction market benchmarks, showing a real shortfall in information aggregation.

Conclusion: Aggregating multiple LLMs can produce a genuine wisdom-of-crowds effect, but the benefit is largely captured by simple learned linear combinations that emphasize disagreement across models rather than sophisticated nonlinear fusion. However, evaluations are heavily confounded by training-cutoff contamination, which can greatly exaggerate performance differences between models. Even under contamination-controlled conditions, LLM crowds still lag behind human prediction markets in information aggregation, so they are promising but not yet competitive as standalone forecasting systems; rigorous, contamination-free evaluation is crucial.

Abstract: The wisdom of crowds -- the finding that aggregating judgments across individuals often outperforms the best individual -- has been extensively studied with human forecasters. Whether the same phenomenon emerges when the ``crowd'' consists of large language models (LLMs) is an open question with both theoretical and practical implications. We elicited probability estimates from 15 LLMs on 254 binary prediction market questions and evaluated classical and learned aggregation methods. Learned aggregators -- a multilayer perceptron and a logistic regression -- outperformed all individual models and classical methods. The logistic regression was found to match the neural network, suggesting that the benefit of learned aggregation derives from learning a linear combination of diverse model outputs rather than from nonlinear interactions. Symbolic regression applied to the neural network's learned mapping recovered a pure model-disagreement signal as the lowest-complexity useful formula on the Pareto frontier, further supporting this interpretation. Training cutoff contamination proved a pervasive confound: the apparent capability gap between frontier cloud models and smaller local models collapsed from 35.8% to 8.9% on a clean subset of questions resolving after all models' training cutoffs, and individual model rankings showed only moderate stability. Even when the prediction market is evaluated at each model's training cutoff, LLMs remained substantially less accurate, indicating a genuine gap in collective information aggregation. These findings suggest that LLM crowds can exhibit wisdom-of-crowds effects, but that contamination-free evaluation is essential for reliable assessment.

</details>


### [73] [Trajectory-Aware Clinical Risk Prediction via Severity-Grounded Knowledge Graphs and Retrieval-Augmented Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18270)
*Kyunghoon Jeon,Youmin Ko,Woohwan Jung,Hyunjoon Kim*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical risk prediction from EHRs is limited by data sparsity, heterogeneous data types, and poor use of unstructured notes. Existing models do not adequately capture disease severity, treatment response, and temporal clinical progression, which are crucial for accurate risk estimation. There is a need for a method that can integrate structured EHR data with external medical knowledge and free-text notes in a trajectory-aware manner.

Method: The authors build TRACER, which (1) constructs a medical knowledge graph that incorporates disease severity information mined from medical literature; (2) for each patient, retrieves clinically relevant and severity-weighted paths that represent that patient’s progression on the knowledge graph; (3) performs information extraction on unstructured clinical notes to identify clinically meaningful events; and (4) augments the patient’s representation with information from similar peer patient trajectories. This combined representation is then used for clinical risk prediction tasks.

Result: On the MIMIC-III and MIMIC-IV critical care datasets, TRACER substantially outperforms state-of-the-art baselines in clinical risk prediction. It achieves up to a 28.5% increase in Macro F1 score for mortality prediction and up to a 19.7% increase in Macro F1 for hospital readmission prediction, indicating improved performance across classes, including minority ones.

Conclusion: Incorporating a severity-enriched medical knowledge graph, events extracted from clinical notes, and peer-patient trajectory augmentation leads to more accurate and clinically grounded risk prediction from EHRs. TRACER’s trajectory-aware framework better captures disease severity and progression than prior methods, yielding significant gains on key predictive tasks and suggesting that knowledge- and trajectory-aware modeling is a promising direction for clinical decision support.

Abstract: While Electronic Health Records (EHRs) offer a wealth of clinical data, effectively augmenting a patient's records with heterogeneous external knowledge to predict the patient's clinical risk remains a significant challenge. Existing methods fail to capture disease severity, treatment responses, and nuanced clinical progression, due to data sparsity and the underutilization of unstructured clinical notes. To address these challenges, we propose TRACER (a trajectory-aware and clinically grounded prediction framework) that (1) constructs a medical knowledge graph enriched with severity information from medical literature, (2) retrieves clinically relevant, severity-weighted paths of a patient's progression from the knowledge graph, (3) extracts clinically relevant events from unstructured clinical notes, and (4) augments patient context with similar peer cases. Experiments on the MIMIC-III and MIMIC-IV datasets demonstrate large gains over state-of-the-art baselines, with up to 28.5% increase in Macro F1 score for the mortality prediction task, and 19.7% increase for the readmission prediction task.

This framework offers a scalable, domain-agnostic approach that substantially narrows the gap between machine-generated and analyst-written explanations in terms of quality and trustworthiness.

Abstract: Time series forecasts are widely used in decision-critical domains, where they are rarely consumed without accompanying explanations. Producing such explanations is usually a manual and costly process, and attempts to automate it using large language models often suffer from hallucination when applied to temporal data. We propose a domain-agnostic framework for grounded natural language explanation generation for time series forecasts, illustrated in Figure 1. The framework consists of three components: (i) extraction of structured explanatory factors from historical analyst-written explanations, (ii) evidence-conditioned explanation generation, and (iii) scalable evaluation for readability, logical consistency, and persuasiveness. The design explicitly constrains generation to verifiable evidence, reducing unsupported claims.
  We evaluate the framework on a financial forecasting case study involving the NASDAQ-100 index and a freight pricing case study using data from Vortexa. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Baghchal is a culturally important, asymmetric, perfect-information board game that is strategically rich but understudied in deep RL. The authors aim to fill this gap and use Baghchal as a testbed to compare modern deep RL algorithms, particularly under asymmetric roles and long-horizon planning demands.

Method: The authors train four deep RL algorithms—DQN (value-based), REINFORCE (policy gradient), PPO (advanced policy gradient), and MuZero (model-based with planning)—on one role (Tiger or Goat) and then evaluate each trained agent when playing the opposite role in the asymmetric game. They compare algorithms using metrics such as win/draw rates, average captures, training convergence behavior, and computational/resource cost. They also analyze emergent strategies to understand how each method handles long-horizon planning and reward asymmetry.

Result: MuZero achieves the highest performance, winning 86% of games as Tiger and 62% as Goat, attributed to its model-based planning via Monte Carlo Tree Search. PPO performs competitively on both roles while requiring far less computation than MuZero. REINFORCE underperforms relative to PPO and MuZero.

Conclusion: Model-based RL with planning (MuZero) is most effective for the long-horizon, asymmetric strategy game Baghchal, but comes with high computational cost. PPO offers a practical trade-off, providing competitive performance with much lower resource demands. Value-based approaches like DQN are less suitable for balanced play in this setting because reward asymmetry skews them toward the Tiger side. Overall, Baghchal serves as a useful benchmark for analyzing asymmetric, long-horizon deep RL strategies.

Abstract: Baghchal is a two-player asymmetric board game with Nepali origins where four tigers are to capture goats and twenty goats desire to keep tigers in immobility. Although Baghchal has a complex structure which is strategic, has perfect information structure, and has cultural meaning, it has not been adequately covered in deep reinforcement learning (RL) literature. This paper gives a systematic exploration of four deep RL solutions Deep Q-Network (DQN), REINFORCE, Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) and MuZero that are trained on one side of the asymmetric gameplay of Baghchal and then evaluated on the other side. The algorithms are rated based on win rate, draw rate, average captures, training convergence and computational cost. It is experimentally found that MuZero generates the best performance in both tasks, achieving 86 percent win over these Tiger and 62 percent win over these Goat and the ability to do so is due to the model-based planning machine through the Monte Carlo Tree Search. PPO is the most realistic algorithm and is provided to be competitive over both asymmetric tasks with significantly reduced computational costs compared to MuZero. Emergent strategic behavior analysis shows that model-based strategies are optimal over long-horizon planning, whereas value-based counterparts like DQN are more biased up towards the Tiger role owing to the more substantial reward signal.

### [76] [Operational Hallucination and Safety Drift in AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18366)
*Shasha Yu,Fiona Carroll,Barry L. Bentley*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies and analyzes two key reliability failures of LLM-based autonomous agents over multi-turn tool use—Safety Drift and Operational Hallucination—and proposes an architectural supervision layer to detect and prevent these failures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing safety work for LLMs largely targets single-turn interactions and focuses on linguistic refusals and content filtering. However, when LLMs act as planners in autonomous tool-using agents over many steps, these front-end safeguards can degrade, leading to unsafe behavior and persistent execution loops. There is a need to systematically characterize these dynamic, multi-turn reliability failures and to design mechanisms that go beyond surface-level text safety to ensure robust, responsible behavior in deployed agents.

Method: The authors run controlled multi-turn experiments using several state-of-the-art LLMs acting as direct-execution planners with tools, across scenarios including high-stakes ethical dilemmas, malicious user requests, and benign control tasks. They empirically study two failure modes: (1) Safety Drift, measured by a declaration-action gap metric that captures when the model’s stated safety intent diverges from its subsequent tool-using actions; and (2) Operational Hallucination, measured via a livelock metric that counts repetitive, unproductive tool calls. They then perform root-cause analysis focusing on how current agent loop designs decouple reasoning context from actual execution state. Finally, they design and simulate an Action-Aware Supervision Layer on recorded failure trajectories to test whether it would have intercepted the unsafe or looping behaviors without affecting benign runs.

Result: Across multiple leading LLMs, the experiments show that both Safety Drift and Operational Hallucination occur frequently in multi-turn tool-use settings, even when models initially appear aligned and safety-conscious. The declaration-action gap reveals cases where the model verbally refuses harmful tasks yet proceeds to gather information or execute unsafe steps via tools, while the livelock metric highlights repetitive tool invocations in both challenging and otherwise straightforward tasks. Root-cause analysis links these issues to architectural aspects of current agent loops rather than to any single model. Simulated deployment of the proposed Action-Aware Supervision Layer on the collected trajectories indicates it can successfully catch these violations and terminate or redirect behavior, with no observed false positives on benign scenarios.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that reliable, responsible AI agents require safety mechanisms that operate at the architectural and execution levels, not solely through linguistic safeguards or single-turn alignment. Safety Drift and Operational Hallucination are systemic risks in current LLM-based agent designs due to the separation of reasoning from execution state. The proposed Action-Aware Supervision Layer, with intent-action consistency checks, runtime state tracking, and termination controls, offers a practical, model-agnostic blueprint to improve agent robustness. The work argues for a shift in research focus toward enforceable supervision layers that monitor and regulate multi-turn tool use in real time.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) serving as planners in tool-using autonomous agents introduce dynamic reliability risks in multi-turn execution. While single-turn safety mechanisms are relatively mature, extended interactions reveal structural vulnerabilities where initial alignment degrades over time. This paper empirically characterizes two observed failure modes across multiple state-of-the-art LLMs: Safety Drift, the gradual erosion of declared safety intent leading to constraint-violating actions (e.g., textual refusal followed by reconnaissance and unsafe execution), and Operational Hallucination, persistent repetitive tool calls indicative of flawed state perception (e.g., livelocks even in legitimate tasks). Through controlled multi-turn evaluation on high-stakes ethical dilemmas, malicious requests, and benign controls, we quantify these phenomena using declaration-action gap and livelock metrics, demonstrating their cross-model prevalence under direct execution protocols. Root-cause analysis attributes the instabilities to the decoupling of reasoning context from execution state in current agent loops. We propose an Action-Aware Supervision Layer - a lightweight, plug-and-play architectural blueprint incorporating intent-action consistency checks, runtime state tracking, and forced termination primitives. Post-hoc simulation on captured failure trajectories shows the layer can intercept observed violations without false positives on benign cases. This work advances agent reliability by shifting focus from linguistic safeguards to enforceable architectural mechanisms for responsible agentic AI.

*AlayaWorld Team,Kaipeng Zhang,Chuanhao Li,Yifan Zhan,Yongtao Ge,Yuanyang Yin,Jiaming Tan,Kang He,Liaoyuan Fan,Mingliang Zhai,Ruicong Liu,Xiaojie Xu,Xuangeng Chu,Zhen Li,Zhengyuan Lin,Zhixiang Wang,Zian Meng,Zihui Gao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces AlayaWorld, an interactive video world model that can generate long-horizon, high-resolution game-like environments from text, images, or video in real time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional video game development requires complex, labor-intensive pipelines for assets, animation, physics, and coding. The authors aim to replace or augment this with a generative system that can instantly create persistent, explorable virtual worlds from simple user inputs, which requires advances in interaction, temporal consistency, stability over long durations, and efficiency.

Method: They build a 15B-parameter video diffusion transformer that generates 24-fps, 540p/720p video in short latent chunks, conditioned on camera trajectories and dynamically switchable text prompts. The model uses a bounded visual context consisting of a persistent sink frame, compressed temporal history, geometry-aligned spatial memory, and recent-frame conditioning. Training involves exposing the model to corrupted histories and prediction residuals from its own roll-outs to reduce long-term drift. They further propose a discrete autoregressive distillation scheme that combines distribution-matching distillation, self-forcing++, and consistency distillation, which cuts the required sampling steps from about 30 to 4 per chunk.

Result: AlayaWorld achieves state-of-the-art performance on long-horizon video generation according to iWorld-Bench and can interactively produce high-resolution, 24-fps video worlds with improved temporal stability and lower inference cost compared to baseline diffusion sampling procedures.

Conclusion: AlayaWorld demonstrates that a carefully designed video diffusion transformer with specialized memory, training on self-generated roll-outs, and an efficient distillation scheme can serve as a practical interactive video world model. The authors position it as an open-source, extensible foundation for future research toward fully generative, game-like virtual worlds created from simple user inputs.

Abstract: Unlike conventional video game development, which relies on labor-intensive pipelines for asset production, animation, physics, and programming, video world models generate interactive environments from user inputs instantly. It enable us to create customized, explorable, and continuously evolving virtual world from text, an image, or video. Realizing this vision requires four tightly coupled capabilities: interaction, persistent spatiotemporal consistency, stable long-horizon generation, and efficient response. We present AlayaWorld, an interactive long-horizon video world model that generates 24-fps video at 540p and 720p. Built on a 15B video diffusion transformer, AlayaWorld generates short latent chunks autoregressively under camera trajectories and switchable text prompts. Its bounded visual context combines a persistent sink frame, compressed temporal history, geometry-aligned spatial memory, and recent-frame conditioning. To reduce long-term drift, the model is trained with corrupted histories and prediction residuals collected from its own roll-outs. We further introduce a discrete autoregressive distillation formulation that combines distribution-matching distillation, self-forcing++, and consistency distillation, reducing inference from approximately 30 sampling steps to four steps per chunk. On iWorld-Bench, AlayaWorld achieves the best performance over long-horizon generation. Conceived as a full-stack, open-source, and long-term project, AlayaWorld is intended to provide an extensible foundation for future research on interactive video world models.

### [78] [Neuro-Symbolic Meta-Policies for Temporal Knowledge-Graph Memory under Partial Observability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18368)
*Taewoon Kim,Vincent François-Lavet,Michael Cochez*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a neuro-symbolic controller that learns how to manage a temporal knowledge-graph memory for partially observable reinforcement learning, achieving strong performance and transparent, step-level traceability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In partially observable reinforcement learning, agents must decide what information to store, recall, and forget over long horizons. Existing methods either rely on opaque neural memory or fixed symbolic heuristics, making it hard to be both adaptive and interpretable. There is a need for a memory-management mechanism that can learn good strategies while staying symbolic and inspectable, especially in settings naturally expressed as knowledge graphs.

Method: They design a neuro-symbolic meta-policy that operates over a temporal knowledge-graph memory (RoomKG). Observations and hidden state are encoded as RDF graphs with temporal annotations on triples. The system uses a graph encoder (including a StarE-GNN variant that is qualifier-aware) over the knowledge-graph memory, and attaches multiple value heads responsible for question answering, exploration, and forgetting decisions. At each step the meta-policy selects a symbolic memory heuristic to apply, so that the memory operations themselves remain symbolic and the entire process retains semantic-web grounding through RDF and graph-based operations.

Result: On benchmarks with train/test room splits and a long-term memory capacity constrained to 512 entries, the configuration using the qualifier-aware StarE-GNN achieves the best held-out performance compared to purely symbolic, purely neural, and other neuro-symbolic baselines. It maintains step-level traceability of which memory-management decisions were made at each point in time.

Conclusion: A neuro-symbolic meta-policy over temporal RDF knowledge graphs can effectively manage memory in partially observable RL while preserving semantic transparency. By combining graph-based symbolic memory with neural decision-making over which heuristic to apply, the approach yields state-of-the-art performance among tested systems and provides fine-grained inspectability of memory updates, aligning well with Semantic Web principles.

Abstract: Partially observable reinforcement learning requires deciding what to retain, retrieve, and forget over time. We introduce a neuro-symbolic meta-policy that learns which symbolic memory heuristic to apply at each decision point while keeping execution symbolic. Our setting uses temporal knowledge-graph memory in RoomKG, where hidden state and observations are represented as Resource Description Framework (RDF) graphs and memory is augmented with temporal RDF triple annotations. The model combines knowledge-graph encoding of memory contents with value heads for question answering, exploration, and forgetting, yielding a controller that is both adaptive and inspectable. This gives the work a direct Semantic Web grounding through RDF-based representation, annotation-compatible graph semantics, and graph-based symbolic operations over explicit memory state. On train/test room splits at long-term memory capacity of 512, the qualifier-aware StarE-GNN configuration achieves the best held-out performance among the compared symbolic, neural, and neuro-symbolic systems while preserving step-level traceability of memory-management decisions.

### [79] [MAGE: Human-Like Macro Placement via Agentic Multimodal Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18536)
*Andrew B. Kahng,Sayak Kundu,Bodhisatta Pramanik*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: MAGE is a multimodal multi-agent framework that refines macro placement using rule-based, language-driven directives and visual checks, achieving significantly better timing and more human-like layouts than commercial and prior academic tools, while generalizing to new designs without retraining.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Industrial macro placement still demands heavy manual expert intervention because existing tools and ML-based methods struggle to capture human floorplanning heuristics, structural aesthetics, and complex constraints, and often need labeled data and retraining per design. The authors aim to automate expert-quality refinement, encode human knowledge without labeled placements, and create metrics that reflect human-like layout quality beyond traditional PPA (performance, power, area).

Method: They build MAGE, a multimodal multi-agent system that decomposes macro placement refinement into a six-phase workflow combining: (1) structured floorplanning rules, (2) natural-language directives encoding expert knowledge, (3) visual inspection of layouts, and (4) iterative, tournament-style refinement. Multiple agents generate alternative placements; a tournament mechanism evaluates them using both standard PPA metrics and new human-likeness metrics, then feeds back guidance from better placements to refine weaker ones. The system is rule/LLM-driven rather than trained on labeled macro placement data, and uses newly defined metrics—notch, whitespace, pocket, and alignment scores—to quantify human-like structure. They validate MAGE on multiple benchmarks (NanGate45 and GF 12nm) and several realistic test scenarios (unseen designs, anonymized netlists, dense/high-utilization layouts).

Result: Across nine designs in two technology nodes, MAGE delivers geometric-mean WNS improvements of about 11.1–19.3% and TNS improvements of 70.0–74.0% over commercial macro placers, at comparable wirelength and power. On three NanGate45 designs with human and Hier-RTLMP baselines, MAGE improves WNS/TNS by 18.3%/72.5% over the human expert and 47.0%/80.4% over Hier-RTLMP, again with similar wirelength and power. On the new human-likeness metrics (notch, whitespace, pocket, alignment), MAGE improves the overall score by 6–48% versus all baselines. Case studies on anonymized and unseen designs, dense rectilinear floorplans, and high-utilization scenarios indicate that MAGE generalizes across placement settings without any design-specific retraining.

Conclusion: Rule- and language-driven, multi-agent macro placement refinement can match or surpass expert and state-of-the-art tool performance without labeled training data, while producing more human-like layouts. MAGE’s six-phase workflow and human-likeness metrics provide a practical, generalizable approach to automating macro floorplan refinement, with strong timing improvements and good transfer to new designs and challenging utilization regimes.

Abstract: Macro placement still requires substantial manual refinement in industrial physical design flows. We present MAGE (Macro Placement Agentic Engine), a multimodal multi-agent framework for macro placement refinement. MAGE decomposes the macro placement task into a six-phase workflow that combines structured floorplanning rules, visual checks, and iterative refinement. Expert floorplanning knowledge is encoded through natural-language directives and validation criteria, rather than learned from labeled placement data. A tournament-style refinement mode evaluates multiple candidate placements and propagates feedback from higher-quality solutions. We also introduce four metrics for quantifying human-likeness in macro placement: notch score, whitespace score, pocket score, and alignment score. These metrics capture structural properties used by expert designers but not directly measured by conventional PPA metrics. Across nine designs in NanGate45 and GlobalFoundries 12nm enablements, MAGE achieves geometric-mean improvements of 11.1%-19.3% in WNS and 70.0%-74.0% in TNS over commercial macro placers. On the three NanGate45 designs, for which human-expert and Hier-RTLMP baselines are available, MAGE improves WNS and TNS by 18.3% and 72.5% over the human expert, and by 47.0% and 80.4% over Hier-RTLMP, with comparable wirelength and power. On human-likeness metrics, MAGE improves the overall score by 6%-48% over all baselines. Additional case studies on anonymized netlists, unseen designs, dense rectilinear floorplans, and high-utilization settings show that the framework transfers to new placement settings without design-specific retraining.

Abstract: Agentic artificial intelligence systems, capable of autonomous perception, planning, tool use, and multi-step action, are increasingly proposed for critical engineering domains where decisions carry physical, operational, or economic consequences. This survey addresses a gap in current literature by treating trustworthiness, whether agentic behavior can be verified, audited, and trusted under the constraints that engineering practice actually requires, as a first-class engineering property, rather than evaluating agentic AI by task capability alone. The study adopts a trustworthiness model organized around five cross-cutting dimensions: safety and constraint satisfaction; robustness and reliability; transparency and interpretability; accountability and auditability; and privacy and security. This is mapped onto an agentic assurance workflow spanning perception through audit. Building on this foundation, agentic systems architectures, threats, concrete trust mechanisms, and quantitative metrics are surveyed for direct application in agentic systems development and evaluation. These principles are then examined across four constraint-bound engineering domains: power systems, autonomous vehicles/robotics/UAVs, high-performance computing, and communication networks, identifying recurring design patterns, shared failure modes, and domain-specific gaps. Synthesizing across those domains, agentic AI trustworthiness is shown to be a single problem, with a path outlined toward a reusable, cross-domain assurance framework analogous to the graded certification regimes used by mature safety-critical engineering fields.

### [81] [Attacking Graph Foundation Models Through Their Shared Representation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18567)
*Pankaj Kumar,Subhankar Mishra*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes and attacks the alignment layer of graph foundation models, showing it is a unique and fragile attack surface distinct from standard graph neural networks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Graph foundation models use a shared representation space across graph domains via an alignment layer, but prior robustness research has mostly focused on traditional graph neural networks. The authors suspect that this alignment layer might introduce new, unexplored vulnerabilities and want to systematically probe and quantify this fragility.

Method: They formally define the alignment layer as the map into a shared representation space and treat it as a separate attack surface. They perform inference-time adversarial attacks on six public graph foundation models that use different alignment mechanisms (spectral tokenizers, text embedding spaces, discrete codebooks). First, they craft directed perturbations in representation space to test how small a perturbation can cause model collapse, comparing this to the perturbation budgets needed for standard graph neural networks. Next, they design realizable input-space attacks that modify graph edges, node features, or associated text. They then analyze how vulnerability correlates with how the decoder uses the representation, using decoder local Lipschitz sensitivity as a structural measure, and compare this to traditional clean-accuracy based heuristics.

Result: All six tested models can be collapsed via targeted representation-space perturbations. The required perturbation budget is on the same order as that needed to fool standard graph networks, except for OpenGraph: its spectral tokenizer collapses at about one-fifth of the budget, revealing alignment-specific fragility not present in a plain network. A control where the representation is held constant implicates the tokenizer rather than the decoder. For input-space attacks (editing edges, features, or text), at least half of the correct predictions can be removed on three of the six models under strong attacks. The degree of realized fragility correlates with how directly the decoder reads the shared representation, quantified via local Lipschitz sensitivity, not with the model’s clean accuracy.

Conclusion: The alignment layer that maps graphs into a shared representation space in graph foundation models is a distinct and often fragile attack surface. Certain alignment mechanisms, such as specific spectral tokenizers, can be dramatically more vulnerable than their non-foundation counterparts. Robustness to realizable attacks is better predicted by the structural properties of the decoder’s use of the representation (e.g., local Lipschitz sensitivity) than by clean task accuracy. This calls for robustness-aware design of both alignment layers and decoders in graph foundation models, and for moving beyond clean-accuracy heuristics when assessing their security.

Abstract: A graph foundation model generalizes across graph domains by mapping every input into one shared representation before any task reasoning. We call this map the alignment layer, the component that separates a graph foundation model from a graph neural network, and we show it is a distinct attack surface that prior work has not studied. We attack it at inference time, with no access to training, on six public models spanning spectral tokenizers, text embedding spaces, and a discrete codebook. A directed representation-space perturbation collapses every model, but at a budget comparable to the representation norm a plain graph network also needs, with one exception: OpenGraph, whose spectral tokenizer collapses at a fifth of that budget, an alignment-specific fragility a plain network does not share and which a same-representation control traces to the tokenizer rather than the decoder. A realizable input-space attack that edits edges, features, or text removes at least half the correct predictions on three of the six models at peak. How much of this fragility an input-access attacker realizes tracks how directly the decoder reads the representation, and not the clean accuracy a task leaves; we measure this carrier gain structurally from the decoder's local Lipschitz sensitivity, and report clean-accuracy headroom as a within-model ordering heuristic that does not survive on realizable attacks.

### [82] [When Does Machine Learning Beat Value Sorting? A Three-Dataset Diagnostic of Exposure-Weighted Shipment Prioritization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18573)
*Jize Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper examines whether machine-learning-based delay-risk models truly outperform a simple, practical baseline—checking the highest-value shipments first—under realistic operational constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In real supply chains, managers can only manually review a limited fraction of shipments for delay risk. Most academic and industry work judges delay-risk or predictive models by their overall predictive accuracy (e.g., R², error metrics), but this may not align with the key operational question: given a constrained review budget, which shipments should be inspected first to best protect value? The authors aim to test if machine learning provides added value over a simple and intuitively strong baseline: always inspect the highest-value shipments. They also want to understand how the quality and learnability of delay severity predictions affect whether ML is worth deploying.

Method: The authors compare several ranking strategies for selecting shipments to review across three real-world supply chain datasets: SCMS procurement, DataCo logistics, and Olist e-commerce. The main ML-based strategy (M1) ranks shipments by the product of predicted delay severity and known shipment value. This is compared to ranking by predicted severity alone and by value alone (VALUE_ONLY). To ensure realistic evaluation, they use leakage-controlled rolling-origin evaluation (time-respecting train/test splits) and derive uncertainty estimates using 1000-sample paired bootstrap confidence intervals. They also explore nested cross-validation with cost-sensitive retraining to see if more sophisticated training improves upon M1. Model performance is assessed in terms of value-protected at different review budgets and through measures of severity learnability (R²) and calibration bias of delay predictions.

Result: M1 (severity × value) consistently outperforms ranking by predicted severity alone in all three datasets, confirming that incorporating value into the ranking is beneficial compared to a purely risk-based prioritization. However, M1 does not reliably beat the simple VALUE_ONLY strategy (sorting by shipment value) across all contexts. At a 10% review budget, M1 underperforms VALUE_ONLY by 5.5 percentage points for SCMS and 4.9 pp for Olist, while outperforming it by 10.1 pp for DataCo. These differences align with how learnable and well-calibrated delay severity is: DataCo shows reasonable learnability (R² = 0.27) and nearly unbiased calibration (+0.01 days), whereas SCMS and Olist have essentially no learnability (R² ≈ -0.02) and negative calibration bias. Nested-CV cost-sensitive retraining fails to provide a consistent improvement over the simpler M1 strategy.

Conclusion: Machine learning delay-risk models should not be judged or deployed solely on traditional predictive metrics. In many realistic supply-chain settings, a simple value-based ranking of shipments can match or outperform more complex ML strategies for prioritizing limited manual reviews, particularly when delay severity is hard to learn or poorly calibrated. The paper proposes a diagnostic and evaluation protocol rather than a new algorithm: (1) always benchmark against a value-only sorting rule, (2) rigorously assess severity learnability and calibration, and (3) use leakage-controlled rolling-origin evaluation. ML-based delay prioritization should only be deployed if it demonstrably clears these benchmarks under realistic, leakage-free temporal evaluation.

Abstract: Delay-risk models are usually judged by predictive accuracy. What matters in practice is narrower: with capacity to review only a few shipments, which ones should a manager check first? We evaluate whether machine learning clears a demanding no-model baseline: inspect the highest-value shipments first. Across three real supply-chain contexts: SCMS procurement, DataCo logistics, and Olist e-commerce, we use leakage-controlled rolling-origin evaluation and 1000-sample paired bootstrap confidence intervals. Ranking by predicted delay severity times known value (M1) beats severity-only ranking in all three datasets, yet it does not generally beat value sorting. At a 10% review budget, M1 minus VALUE_ONLY is -5.5 percentage points (pp) for SCMS, +10.1 pp for DataCo, and -4.9 pp for Olist. The divide is consistent with severity learnability: DataCo has R^2 = 0.27 and calibration bias of +0.01 days, whereas SCMS and Olist have R^2 of approximately -0.02 and negative calibration bias. Nested-CV cost-sensitive retraining does not deliver a stable improvement over M1. Rather than proposing a new learning algorithm, this paper presents a deployment diagnostic and evaluation protocol. Value sorting should remain a permanent benchmark, and ML should be deployed only after severity learnability and calibration have been audited and the model clears that gate under leakage-controlled rolling-origin evaluation.

</details>


### [83] [SciHazard: A Benchmark for Measuring Scientific Safety Risks with Decomposed Harm Scoring](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18665)
*Chunxiao Li,Yuan Xiong,Lijun Li,Tianyi Du,Wenlong Zhang,Lei Bai,Jing Shao*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM safety benchmarks for science misuse rely on synthetic, templated prompts and LLM-as-a-Judge setups that lack real-world grounding and domain expertise. This leads to unreliable measures of how harmful or safe LLM outputs are, especially for complex and regulated scientific domains. There is a need for a benchmark based on actual hazardous scenarios and a more principled metric to capture multiple dimensions of harm.

Method: The authors construct SciHazard, a dataset of 2,400 hazardous and 600 oversafety (overly cautious) questions across 12 scientific disciplines, each grounded in regulated entities and documented failure cases. They then design DeHarm-Score, a decomposed harmfulness evaluation procedure that integrates: (1) hazard severity of the query, (2) model refusal behavior, and (3) response-level risk. For non-refused answers, response harm is further decomposed into Executability (measured by dynamic, importance-weighted checklists) and Net-new risk (measured through retrieval-augmented extraction of factual claims and checking whether these claims lower synthesis barriers beyond what is already publicly available). They validate the metric via expert annotation comparisons and use it to benchmark 31 frontier LLMs and deep research agents.

Result: DeHarm-Score yields substantially better alignment with expert safety annotations, improving agreement by 90.17% over the strongest prior baseline. Applying the framework to 31 advanced LLMs and autonomous research agents shows a wide variation in scientific safety. Deep research agents exhibit a 32.3% higher average DeHarm-Score (i.e., are more harmful) compared to standard LLMs, indicating that agents pose elevated scientific misuse risks under realistic use patterns.

Conclusion: SciHazard and DeHarm-Score provide a more realistic and structured way to measure scientific misuse risk in LLMs, revealing that current autonomous research agents are particularly dangerous blind spots for safety defenses. The work suggests that safety evaluation must move beyond simple refusal rates and templated prompts to multi-factor, real-world grounded assessments, and it calls for dedicated safety mitigations for agentic LLM systems.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly support science, but they can also convert hazardous scientific knowledge into actionable misuse guidance. Existing benchmarks often rely on templated queries disconnected from real-world hazards, and employ LLM-as-a-Judge paradigms without domain grounding. To address this, we introduce SciHazard, a real-world-grounded benchmark for scientific risks and a dataset agnostic evaluation framework for measuring harmfulness. SciHazard contains 2400 hazardous questions and 600 oversafety questions across 12 disciplines, with both queries grounded in regulated entities and documented failure scenarios. To compute \textsc{DeHarm-Score} , we develop a decomposed evaluating procedure that combines query hazard severity, refusal behavior, and response-level risk. For non-refused responses, it further decomposes response-level harm into \textsc{Executability}, quantified via dynamic checklists with importance weighting, and \textsc{Net-new risk}, assessed through retrieval-augmented claim extraction and synthesis-barrier verification. An expert-validation study shows that \textsc{DeHarm-Score} improves agreement with expert annotations by 90.17\% over the strongest baseline. We benchmark 31 frontier LLMs and deep research agents in an extensive scientific safety evaluation. Notably, deep research agents yield 32.3\% higher mean \textsc{DeHarm-Score} than standard LLMs, exposing autonomous agents as a critical blind spot in current safety defenses. Code and dataset are available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/DeharmScore-7B55.

### [84] [Semantic Primes as Explanans for Emotion in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18691)
*Frank Xing*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper investigates whether Natural Semantic Metalanguage (NSM) semantic primes provide a better explanation of emotions in large language models than existing emotion representations and appraisal-based methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While emotion-related mechanisms (representations, components, circuits) have been identified in LLMs, current explanations are often circular and rely on arbitrary, high-dimensional emotion spaces that do not offer primitive explanatory variables. The authors are motivated to find a more fundamental internal basis that can causally and interpretably account for emotional behavior in LLMs, and they propose that NSM semantic primes could serve this role.

Method: The authors test four instruction-tuned LLMs (Llama-1B, Gemma-2B, Gemma-9B, OLMo-7B). Result: (1) NSM primes are indeed recoverable as internal variables in all tested models. (2) On a reference model, interventions along NSM-prime-based directions modulate emotional content about three times more strongly and about twice as selectively as the best appraisal-based directions. (3) Prime-based explications are treated by the model as effectively interchangeable with the corresponding emotion labels, indicating that the primes underlie the model's representation of emotions.

Conclusion: NSM semantic primes function as more fundamental and effective explanans for emotions in LLMs than conventional emotion or appraisal dimensions, according to standard criteria for scientific explanation. This suggests that explanatorily primitive semantic units, rather than high-level emotion constructs, may best capture how LLMs internally represent and compute emotional content.

Abstract: Progresses have been made on understanding emotion mechanisms of large language models (LLMs). However, how to explain emotion in LLMs, or even what constitutes good explanations, are less clear. Emotion representations, components, circuits are widely recoverable, but as explanations of a model's own computation they are circular; the emotion space dimensions tend to be arbitrary and non-terminating. A pressing question to ask is whether a more primitive set of internal variables does the work: the semantic primes of the Natural Semantic Metalanguage (NSM). Across four instruction-tuned LLMs (Llama-1B, Gemma-2B, Gemma-9B, OLMo-7B), experiments show that the NSM primes are (1) recoverable internal elements; and (2) on the reference model, intervening with a prime based direction controls emotion about three times as strongly, and twice as selectively, as the best appraisal based direction; and (3) the model treats a prime based explication as interchangeable with the corresponding emotion. These evidences suggest that NSM primes seem to be better explanans for emotion in LLMs than many alternative options according to scientific explanations criteria.

### [85] [Do AI-Native Biotechs Need Departments? Benchmarking Company World Models for AI-Driven Drug Development](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18696)
*Yinan Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes and evaluates an AI-native "Company World Model" for biotech, centered on a shared asset-to-value state instead of mimicking human organizational charts, and shows this architecture performs best on a dry-lab decision-making benchmark under value-focused objectives.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI-native biotech companies usually replicate traditional human organizational structures (e.g., departments like BD, regulatory, commercial) in the form of AI agents. The authors believe this may be suboptimal because organizations fundamentally operate by transforming scientific assets into value under multiple constraints. They aim to explore whether an alternative abstraction—a unified company world model capturing assets, value, transitions, and constraints—can better support AI-agent organizations and decision-making than department-mimicking structures.

Method: They define a "Company World Model" as a persistent representation of asset-to-value state with explicit transition models, value functions, planning, and updating across scientific, regulatory, BD, commercial, financial, and execution dimensions. They then construct a dry-lab benchmark of 45 retrospective decision cases drawn from public information, each with time cutoffs, hidden outcomes, standardized schemas, automatic scoring, and blinded pairwise human judging. They implement and compare four AI-agent organizational architectures: (1) a human-organization-mimic, (2) a stronger human-org-mimic-plus, (3) an AI-native asset-centric design, and (4) an AI-native value-conversion architecture. The value-conversion design uses a "Live Asset Value Record" updated via discrete Deal, Approval, Revenue, and Investment Arbiter loops to approximate the Company World Model at the prompt level. They evaluate these architectures against an externally defined success function (business development, regulatory approval and launch, and revenue discipline). Additional stress tests, stronger baselines, neutral judges, and mechanistic ablations (e.g., Codex-only versions of specific rooms like Revenue, Deal, Approval) probe robustness and component contributions.

Result: The AI-native value-conversion architecture achieved the highest automatic value-conversion score on the benchmark and was strongly preferred by value-specialist blinded judges over the original human-org-mimic baselines. However, when a stronger human baseline was included and a neutral judge evaluated outputs, the dominance of the value-conversion architecture weakened; the human baseline remained competitive and no robust superiority was observed under neutral judging. Mechanistic ablations with Codex-only implementations indicated that specific components—Revenue Room, Deal Room, and Approval Room—contributed nontrivially to performance under the target objective. Overall, results support the utility of a shared asset-to-value state representation but also highlight sensitivity to objective choice and evaluation setup.

Conclusion: The study suggests that for AI-native biotech organizations, the central organizing primitive should be a shared, predictive asset-to-value state representation—a Company World Model—rather than direct replicas of human departmental org charts. Departmental views may still be useful for governance and interpretability, but operationally, value-conversion loops around a unified company world model appear more effective on the constructed dry-lab benchmark, especially when evaluated by value-focused experts. Nonetheless, these findings are limited to retrospective, dry-lab simulations; they do not demonstrate improved real-world drug development success, clinical outcomes, or revenue forecasting accuracy, and the advantages depend on the evaluation objective and baseline strength.

Abstract: AI-native biotechnology companies are often designed by copying human biotech org charts into agent roles. We argue for a different abstraction: a Company World Model, defined as a persistent asset-to-value state representation with transition models, explicit value functions, planning, and updating across scientific, regulatory, BD, commercial, financial, and execution constraints. We introduce a dry-lab benchmark for testing whether AI-agent organizations should mimic departments or operate around such a world model. The benchmark contains 45 retrospective public-information decision cases with strict time cutoffs, hidden outcomes, common schemas, automatic scoring, and blinded pairwise judging. We compare human-org-mimic, stronger human-org-mimic-plus, AI-native asset-centric, and AI-native value-conversion architectures. The value-conversion architecture is a prompt-level approximation of a Company World Model: a Live Asset Value Record updated by Deal, Approval, Revenue, and Investment Arbiter loops. Under a success function defined by external BD, regulatory approval and launch, and revenue discipline, it achieved the highest automatic value-conversion score and was strongly preferred over the original baselines by value-specific blinded judges. Stress tests narrowed the claim: a stronger human baseline remained competitive, and a neutral judge did not show robust value-conversion dominance. Codex-only mechanistic ablations suggest that Revenue Room, Deal Room, and Approval Room carry useful work under the target objective. The central finding is objective-sensitive: departments may remain useful governance views, but the core AI-native operating primitive should be a shared, predictive asset-to-value state rather than a static human org chart. The study is dry-lab only and does not establish real-world drug success, clinical benefit, or revenue prediction accuracy.

### [86] [DWM: Separating World Effects from Actions in Latent World Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18715)
*Yi-Ge Zhang,Tianqi Du,Qi Zhang,Yisen Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose Decomposed World Model (DWM), which separates action-driven and action-invariant components in latent dynamics to improve model-based control and planning, achieving significant gains on benchmarks with persistent world effects.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing latent world models in model-based control supervise next-state prediction with a single target, conflating two distinct causes of state change: agent-induced, action-driven dynamics and environment-induced, action-invariant world effects (e.g., gravity, inertia, drift). This entanglement makes it hard for the model to correctly attribute changes to their causes and reduces the transferability and robustness of learned dynamics, especially when persistent world effects are present.

Method: DWM is a supervision-level framework layered on top of standard latent world models. It adds an auxiliary "world" prediction head that is regularized to be action-invariant via a normalized world-contrastive loss, while keeping the original prediction head. An orthogonality constraint couples the two heads such that the predicted transition can be explicitly decomposed into an additive sum of an action-invariant component (world head) and a complementary action-driven component (original head). This modification does not change the base architecture or inference pipeline but changes how the transition is supervised and regularized.

Result: They create new benchmark variants (PushT-W, Reacher-W, TwoRoom-W) that incorporate persistent action-invariant dynamics. On standard, "flat" benchmarks, DWM performs on par with strong baselines, showing it does not degrade performance in simpler settings. On the W-variants with world effects, DWM improves CEM (cross-entropy method) planning success by an average of 13.1% compared to baselines.

Conclusion: Decomposing latent transitions into separate action-driven and action-invariant components via DWM improves the attribution of state changes to their causes and enhances planning performance in environments with persistent world effects, without requiring changes to model architecture or inference. This suggests that supervision-level decomposition is a practical and effective way to make world models more robust and transferable in realistic control tasks.

Abstract: Latent world models underpin much of modern model-based control, yet current action-conditioned formulations supervise
  the next-latent transition with a single, undifferentiated target, forcing a monolithic learning signal to absorb every
  source of state change. In real world, however, transitions arise from two heterogeneous sources: an action-driven
  component induced by the agent, and an action-invariant world effect -- the change that would still occur under a null
  action, dictated by the environment's intrinsic dynamics (e.g., gravity-driven sliding, inertia, contact rebound, and
  persistent drift). Fusing them into a single target entangles the two inside the latent transition, prevents the model
  from attributing observed changes to their underlying causes, and undermines the transferability of the learned
  dynamics. We introduce DWM (Decomposed World Model), a supervision-level framework that operationalizes this
  decomposition. DWM augments the predictor of a latent world model with an auxiliary world head, regularized by a
  normalized world-contrastive objective to be action-invariant, while the original pred head is coupled to it via an
  orthogonality constraint; together, the two signals induce an explicit additive decomposition of the predicted
  transition into an action-invariant and a complementary action-driven component, without altering the underlying
  architecture or inference pipeline. To evaluate DWM under persistent world effects, we construct W-variants of three
  standard control benchmarks -- PushT-W, Reacher-W, and TwoRoom-W -- each instantiating a distinct action-invariant
  dynamic. DWM matches strong baselines on the flat counterparts and delivers a mean absolute improvement of 13.1% in CEM
  planning success across the W-variants.

</details>


### [87] [One Rewrite to Fix Them All? Type-Aware Repair Allocation for Text-to-Image Prompt Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18724)
*Haoyue Liu,Xiaoyu Ma,Ye Chen,Shuguang Cui,Xiaoying Tang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes TARA, a training-free framework that improves how text-to-image models follow prompts by assigning specific repairs to specific types of semantic failures and then compiling them into a single optimized prompt.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Text-to-image models often misinterpret prompts, causing errors such as incorrect object counts, wrong attributes, unclear relations, and unreadable text. Existing prompt optimization methods apply a single, undifferentiated prompt expansion to handle all these issues together, which is suboptimal because different failure types require different repair strategies. There is a need for a more structured and type-aware way to fix semantic errors without retraining the underlying image generator.

Method: The authors define semantic prompt optimization as atomic repair allocation: for each failed proposition in the prompt-image pair, a diagnosis step identifies its type (e.g., count, attribute, relation, text). Each failure is then sent to a type-conditioned repair operator that proposes a local constraint. A compilation step merges all local constraints into one executable prompt. TARA introduces a semantic repair gate—an accept-or-revert controller that evaluates the impact of exactly one prescribed repair at a time and rejects it if it causes semantic regressions. The whole framework is training-free and operates on top of frozen T2I generators.

Result: On DSG and TIFA benchmarks, evaluated across four frozen text-to-image generators, TARA achieves the best semantic accuracy in all eight combinations of benchmark and generator. It improves over the prior method VisualPrompter by 5.6 points on DSG and 2.6 points on TIFA, while maintaining comparable image quality. In a matched local setting, TARA is also faster, taking 16.0 seconds per prompt compared with VisualPrompter’s 20.0 seconds.

Conclusion: Type-aware, atomic repair allocation is an effective way to optimize prompts for text-to-image models. By separately diagnosing error types, applying specialized repair operators, compiling them into a unified prompt, and gating repairs to avoid regressions, TARA improves semantic faithfulness to prompts without retraining generators, while preserving image quality and offering better runtime efficiency than prior prompt optimization methods.

Abstract: Text-to-image (T2I) generators often fail to follow their prompts faithfully, producing wrong counts, swapped attributes, ambiguous relations, and illegible text. Prompt optimization repairs such failures by rewriting the user prompt, requiring no generator retraining, and has yielded promising results. However, existing optimizers absorb heterogeneous failures into one uniform prompt expansion, even though each calls for different repair language. We formulate semantic prompt optimization as atomic repair allocation: each failed proposition is routed to a type-conditioned repair operator before the resulting local constraints are compiled into one executable prompt. We instantiate this formulation in the training-free Type-Aware Repair Allocation (TARA) framework, which separates diagnosis, allocation, compilation, and a semantic repair gate, an accept-or-revert controller over exactly one prescribed repair that prevents semantic regressions. Extensive experiments on DSG and TIFA across four frozen generators demonstrate that TARA achieves the best semantic accuracy in all eight benchmark-generator cells, improving over VisualPrompter by 5.6 and 2.6 points on DSG and TIFA, respectively, while maintaining image quality and running fastest in our matched local setting at 16.0 seconds versus 20.0 seconds per prompt.

### [88] [AgentDebugX: An Open-Source Toolkit for Failure Observability, Attribution, and Recovery in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18754)
*Kunlun Zhu,Xuyan Ye,Zhiguang Han,Yuchen Zhao,Bingxuan Li,Weijia Zhang,Muxin Tian,Xiangru Tang,Pan Lu,James Zou,Jiaxuan You,Heng Ji*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AgentDebugX is a debugging framework for LLM agents that systematizes error detection, attribution, and repair, with a core component (DeepDebug) that significantly improves root-cause diagnosis and automatic recovery over baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Debugging LLM agents is hard because the step where an error appears is often not the step that caused it, and existing tools largely just replay traces without helping users find root causes or fix them. There is a need for a principled, automated way to connect failures to their true origin and to turn diagnoses into effective repairs.

Method: The authors propose AgentDebugX, a closed-loop debugging framework structured as Detect, Attribute, Recover, and Rerun. Its core module, DeepDebug, performs multi-turn, interactive root-cause analysis using: (1) global trajectory understanding over the entire agent run, (2) structure-guided investigation that leverages the agent’s task and control structure, and (3) cross-examination to validate or refute hypothesized causes. They implement this as a reusable system exposed via a Python library, CLI, web UI, and an agentic skill, plus an Error Hub for sharing and reusing anonymized failure–diagnosis–repair episodes.

Result: On the Who and When benchmark, DeepDebug achieves the best strict attribution performance among tested approaches, with 28.8% exact agent-and-step attribution accuracy on qwen3.5-9b, outperforming the strongest single-pass baseline at 21.7%. On the GAIA benchmark, using DeepDebug to repair and rerun failed tasks fixes 13 of 73 failures in a single debugging loop, compared to 4–6 repairs for three self-correction baselines, raising overall task accuracy from 55.8% to 63.6%.

Conclusion: AgentDebugX, centered on the DeepDebug module, provides an effective closed-loop workflow for diagnosing and repairing LLM agent failures. By improving root-cause attribution and enabling automatic recovery that translates diagnosis into successful reruns, it outperforms existing self-correction and debugging baselines and offers practical tooling and a shared Error Hub to support real-world agent development and maintenance.

Abstract: LLM agent failures are difficult to debug because the step where an error surfaces is often not the one that caused it. Existing observability tools replay execution traces but provide little support for identifying the root cause or translating diagnosis into recovery. We present AgentDebugX, an open-source debugging framework that organizes debugging as a closed loop of Detect, Attribute, Recover, and Rerun. At its core, DeepDebug performs multi-turn root-cause diagnosis through global trajectory understanding, structure-guided investigation, and cross-examination. On the Who and When benchmark, DeepDebug achieves the best strict attribution accuracy among the evaluated methods on both tested open-weight backbones, reaching 28.8 percent exact agent-and-step accuracy on qwen3.5-9b versus 21.7 percent for the strongest single-pass baseline. On GAIA, DeepDebug repairs 13 of 73 failed tasks in a single rerun, compared with 4 to 6 for three decoupled self-correction baselines, improving overall accuracy from 55.8 percent to 63.6 percent. AgentDebugX exposes this workflow through a Python library, CLI, web console, and installable agentic skill, and provides an opt-in Error Hub for sharing scrubbed failure-diagnosis-repair bundles and reusing them as debugging memory.
